# The First Art

# Chapter 1

The compartment smelled like dust and old upholstery charm. There were three of them in a space meant for six: Gavin by the window, Duncan beside him with his bag on his lap, Miren across with her feet tucked under her on the seat. The train pulled out of King's Cross eleven minutes late, and the sound was wrong even before they cleared the station.

Usually the Hogwarts Express was loud from the start. The sound was a layered thing — compartment doors banging open, someone's cat loose in the corridor, older students shouting for friends or sweets, the trolley rattling before anyone was settled. Today the corridor was quiet. The doors stayed shut. When someone did pass outside their compartment, Gavin could hear their footsteps on the carpet, which he had never been able to hear before over the noise.

"She didn't come," Miren said. She had been watching the platform through the grimy window as London slid past, and now she turned with the expression she wore when she was working herself up to something, though the something hadn't arrived yet. Her dark hair was in the tight plait she always wore for doing things, and she had a sunburn across her nose and the tops of her cheeks — she'd told them in a letter that she'd spent August helping her mum repaint the house in Lewisham, the whole outside, on ladders. The sunburn was peeling at the edges. She picked at it when she was thinking.

"She wasn't there. I watched the whole platform."

"Her family might've come through a different entrance," Gavin said.

"There's one entrance, Gav."

"Right. Yeah."

"That's four from our year. Four that I know of. Collins, Abiodun, Singh, and now her. And that's just Hufflepuffs."

Duncan hadn't said anything since they'd settled into the compartment. He was looking at the stretch of seat beside Miren — the empty space where someone should have been sitting — and his hands were on his bag and he was holding very still. He'd been holding very still since King's Cross. On the platform, Gavin had seen Duncan's parents standing together in their Muggle clothes — his mum in a rain jacket despite the clear weather, his dad in the kind of shoes you wore to a job interview even though there was no interview. His mum had pressed a Tupperware container of sandwiches into Duncan's hands and Duncan had tried to give it back and she'd pressed it in again and they had both been smiling and the smiles had been terrible.

Duncan was Muggle-born. Both parents, no wizarding relatives, no pureblood grandfather to claim. He had been a name on a list since August, the list the Muggle-Born Registration Commission had drawn up, and the fact that he was on the train at all was a decision his family had made that Gavin couldn't fully imagine the weight of. Come back and be watched, or stay home and be found.

Gavin didn't know what to say about any of it. He had spent his own summer doing OWL revision in his bedroom in Swindon, which now felt like something that had happened in a different country. His parents were both wizards, both unremarkable, both fine. His dad worked in Magical Transportation — the scheduling division, not the policy side — and his mum did part-time apothecary supply. They had been worried about this year, but in the general way parents worried, which was to send extra socks and tell him to keep his head down.

He kept his head down naturally. It wasn't something he had to work at.

"Have you heard anything about what it's actually going to be like?" Miren asked. She was asking both of them but looking at Gavin, because Duncan had the expression that meant he would rather not be in this conversation. "The Carrows. Amycus and Alecto. Do you know what they're planning?"

"Snape's headmaster," Gavin said. "Everyone knows that."

"I know everyone knows that. I mean what are the Carrows going to do? With us. In lessons."

"They've made Dark Arts mandatory. Not Defense Against."

"I know. And Muggle Studies. That's mandatory now too." She stopped herself. The anger was coming up, the thing she had been building toward, and she was trying to say it carefully because everything she was about to say pressed against Duncan in a way that couldn't be helped but shouldn't be made worse.

Duncan opened his bag and took out a textbook. Advanced Potion-Making. He opened it to somewhere near the middle and looked at it.

"Muggle Studies is going to be —" Miren lowered her voice. "It's not Muggle Studies anymore. You know that. It's going to be about how Muggles are animals, or whatever they — it's propaganda. And they're making everyone take it."

"I know what it is," Duncan said. His eyes hadn't moved on the page.

"Yeah. I know you know."

They were quiet. The train was past the city now, into the green stretches of middle England, and the sky was flat and grey and had been grey since morning. Gavin could hear the rain start on the roof, a faint drumming that made the compartment feel smaller.

Duncan closed the textbook. "Did you hear about the Potions change?"

"What change?"

"Slughorn's staying on, apparently. He wanted to leave, but." Duncan shrugged with one shoulder. It was a minimal movement, precise, the kind of shrug that conserved energy. "Someone told me on the platform."

"Well, that's something," Miren said. "At least Potions won't be —"

"Won't be what?"

"I don't know. Something."

They went quiet again. None of them slept. The train moved through the Midlands and the countryside looked the same as it always had and the rain came and went and came back. Miren got up once to look down the corridor and came back without saying what she'd seen, which was probably more closed doors and fewer faces than there should have been.

Sometime around Doncaster, Duncan opened the Tupperware. The sandwiches inside were ham and English mustard on white bread, cut into triangles, each one wrapped separately in cling film. He held the container out without looking at either of them. Gavin took one. The bread was soft and the mustard had bite and it tasted like somebody's kitchen — a specific kitchen in Sheffield where a woman in a rain jacket had wrapped each triangle carefully and pressed the whole container into her son's hands twice on a platform because once hadn't been enough. Duncan ate one himself, slowly, looking out the window at fields that were beginning to darken. Miren shook her head when he offered and Duncan didn't push it, and the three of them sat with the open Tupperware between them and the rain on the roof and the quiet that shouldn't have been there.

Gavin read through his Transfiguration notes, which he'd been meaning to review since July and hadn't. On the page about Switching Spells, he'd copied a line from McGonagall in careful blue ink: *Similarity assists intent; it does not replace it.* He stared at that for a while because every example underneath seemed to lean almost entirely on similarity, and he couldn't make the wording and the practice sit together. He put a question mark in the margin and turned the page.

He was going into his OWL year and he was average at Transfiguration, average at Charms, slightly below average at Potions, passable at Herbology, bad at History of Magic in the specific way that everyone who wasn't Hermione Granger was bad at History of Magic. His best subject was Ancient Runes, and even there he sat in the comfortable middle of the class. His end-of-year marks the previous June had been a smooth line of Acceptables with a single Exceeds Expectations in Care of Magical Creatures, which Professor Grubbly-Plank had given him, he suspected, because he'd been the only student who hadn't flinched when the Fire Crab sneezed.

He wasn't bothered by any of this. Some students agonised over marks — Ravenclaws especially, though there were a few in every house — and to Gavin it felt like a foreign custom: recognisable but not applicable. He wanted to pass his OWLs. He would probably pass most of them. He didn't know what he wanted to do after school, and the not-knowing didn't bother him either, which bothered Miren, who had plans.

"What do you think they're going to do about Quidditch?" Miren said sometime in the late afternoon, when the rain had stopped and the hills were getting steeper through the window.

"Probably still have it," Gavin said.

"How can they still have Quidditch when they're —" She stopped. "I don't know. Maybe they will."

"They will," Duncan said. It was the most he'd said in hours. "That's the thing you keep normal so the rest of it looks normal too."

Miren looked at him. Gavin looked at him.

"My dad talks about it," Duncan said. "About his work. When management changes something big, they keep the Christmas party going." He seemed to hear himself — hear the analogy reaching back into his Muggle life and dragging it into this compartment, this train, this world that was becoming specifically dangerous for people whose fathers compared Death Eater governance to office politics. He went quiet and put his textbook back in his bag.

"That's smart," Miren said after a moment. "That's actually — yeah. The Christmas party."

"Right."

The light outside was changing, going long and golden over the Scottish hills, and Gavin could see first-years pressing their faces to windows further up the train. He remembered that — the first sight of Hogwarts from the lake, the sheer overwhelm of it, the towers and the lit windows and the sense that you had come somewhere vast and ancient and specifically yours. He wondered what the first-years would feel this time. He wondered what the castle would feel like now.

The train was slowing. The corridor filled briefly with noise — trunks dragged over carpet, someone's owl protesting, a prefect's voice calling something about leaving belongings. They were normal sounds, but there were fewer of them, and in the spaces between lay a quiet that didn't belong on the Hogwarts Express on the first of September.

Miren stood and pulled her robe straight. She'd been picking at her sunburn again and there was a flake of dead skin on her collar that Gavin thought about mentioning and didn't.

"Right," she said. "Let's go."

Duncan put his bag over his shoulder. He had packed light — no owl, no extra trunk, just the one bag. Gavin wondered if that was because he'd packed in a hurry or because he'd packed to leave in a hurry. He didn't ask.

They stepped off the train into a September evening that smelled like wet pine and lake water and the particular cold that Hogsmeade station always had, the kind that settled in your ears and stayed. The platform was less crowded than it should have been. Gavin could count the heads around him. He had never been able to count heads on this platform before.

---

The Great Hall looked right and was wrong.

The four house tables were in their places. The enchanted ceiling showed a clear September night, deep blue and scattered with stars, and the floating candles threw warm light over the golden plates and goblets. The house banners hung behind the staff table. The ghosts drifted near the high windows. It was all correct, all in order, and none of it landed the way it was supposed to.

The seats were the problem, or the absence in them. Every table had gaps — not one or two empty spots but long stretches of bench where nobody sat, the spaces visible and specific. The Hufflepuff table was missing perhaps a dozen students. The Gryffindor table looked worse. The Slytherins were nearly full, which Gavin noticed and then felt bad for noticing, and then stopped feeling bad because it was a fact and facts didn't require guilt.

He sat between Miren and a sixth-year named Fenwick who was trying to read a letter under the table without anyone seeing. The letter was long and the handwriting small and Fenwick's hands weren't steady. Duncan sat across from them with his hands flat on the table and his face pointed at the staff table, at the chair in the middle.

Snape was in Dumbledore's chair.

Gavin had known this. Everyone had known this since the summer. But knowing it and seeing it were different things, and the seeing settled in his stomach in a way the knowing hadn't. Snape sat very still, his black robes precisely arranged, his expression a kind of practised blankness that was nothing like Dumbledore's openness or warmth or any of the qualities you associated with someone sitting in that particular chair. He looked at the students as if they were a room he meant to keep in order.

On either side of Snape, in chairs that hadn't been at the staff table last year, sat two people Gavin had never seen.

The man was squat and heavy-shouldered, with a lopsided face and teeth that caught the candlelight wrong. He was sweating, though the Great Hall was cool, and he'd pushed the sleeves of his robes up past his forearms like someone about to do physical work. He kept shifting his weight and looking out at the students, scanning the tables with the quick, unsettled movements of someone who was both excited and nervous about what was coming. The woman beside him was similar enough in the face to be related — siblings, Gavin thought — with the same dense, graceless build and an expression that was steadier than her brother's, more certain. She didn't look at the students. She looked at the staff, at the other professors, and something in that look made Professor Sprout, who was nearest, angle her body slightly away.

The Sorting went quickly. Fewer first-years than usual — a short line of eleven-year-olds who looked even smaller than first-years usually did, dwarfed by the emptiness of the Hall around them. Professor McGonagall read the names and her voice was the same voice it had always been, crisp and Scottish and giving nothing away, but she held the scroll differently. Gavin couldn't identify what was different. Her grip, maybe. The angle of her wrist. Her hands held the scroll with the same careful grip Duncan had used on his bag on the train.

The new students sat down at their tables and looked around for what was supposed to come next — the cheering, the welcome, the older students pulling them into seats and loading their plates and asking about their houses with the performative enthusiasm of people who remembered being that small and scared. Some of it happened. The Hufflepuff prefects tried. But the trying was audible in a way that succeeding never was, and the first-years' faces settled into polite confusion as the effort showed through.

Snape stood. The Hall went silent — not the gradual settling that had preceded Dumbledore's speeches, where you could hear stragglers whispering and someone dropping a fork, but an immediate total silence that started at the staff table and moved through the room like a door closing.

"The appointments to the staff this year are as follows," Snape said. His voice carried without effort. "Amycus Carrow will teach Dark Arts." Not Defence Against the Dark Arts — just Dark Arts. "Alecto Carrow will teach Muggle Studies, which is now compulsory for all students."

The squat man — Amycus — surveyed the students with an expression Gavin recognised from a specific type of adult: the kind who had been given authority late and meant to use it while it lasted. He looked at the students the way Gavin's uncle looked at his allotment when the neighbour's cat had been through it. Ownership and the intent to correct.

Alecto Carrow did not look at the students. She was still watching the staff, and Professor Sprout was still angled away, and between those two positions Gavin could read something that didn't need to be spoken.

"The school rules are unchanged," Snape said. "They will be enforced."

That was it. No welcome-back, no feast-opening remarks, no commentary about the year ahead. Snape sat down. The food appeared on the plates — roast chicken, potatoes, bread, pies, all of it the same abundance as every year — and the silence held for three seconds, four, five, before someone at the Ravenclaw table picked up a serving spoon and the sound of it broke whatever was holding the room and people began to eat.

Gavin took a piece of chicken and a roll. He ate because the food was there and because not eating would be conspicuous. The chicken tasted like chicken. The roll was warm. None of it felt like the start of term. It felt like eating in a waiting room.

"Don't look at them," Miren said quietly, as Fenwick beside Gavin craned toward the staff table. Fenwick pulled back. Miren was already eating, steady and deliberate, each movement more controlled than it needed to be. She buttered her roll with more force than bread required.

"Muggle Studies is compulsory," Duncan said. His voice was flat and carefully empty. "That's interesting."

"It's not interesting. It's —"

"I know what it is."

They ate. Around them the Hufflepuff table produced the sounds of a meal — silverware on plates, glasses being filled, low conversation — but the conversation kept stalling and restarting, circling the same ground without settling. A fourth-year asked if Hogsmeade weekends would still happen and nobody knew. A seventh-year said something about Quidditch tryouts and the word landed and sat there like it belonged to a different school.

Gavin looked at the enchanted ceiling. Clear sky, bright stars, the Milky Way running faintly above the floating candles. It was the same sky that had been there every September, and it was beautiful, and he was the only person at the table looking up at it.

From the staff table, Amycus Carrow laughed at something. The sound was loud and wrong in the half-quiet Hall, and several students flinched. Not dramatically — a scattering of small movements: heads dipping, shoulders tightening, a fork paused between plate and mouth. The kind of flinch that was already becoming habit, three hours into the year.

Professor Sprout looked down at her plate. McGonagall did not.

Gavin reached for another roll and then pulled his hand back because the basket was closer to Duncan and Duncan hadn't eaten anything.

"Do you want —" Gavin started.

"Not hungry." Then, as if correcting: "Maybe later."

People were leaving. That was the other wrong thing — usually the feast went on, dessert appeared, the talk got louder, the first-years warmed up, and the older students stayed because the Great Hall on the first night was the whole point, the feeling of being somewhere together. Tonight students were standing up in ones and twos as soon as the dessert appeared, heading for their common rooms, a few of them carrying napkins wrapped around biscuits as if they were stockpiling. The Gryffindors left first, a group of them moving together without discussion. The Ravenclaws followed. Even the Slytherins thinned out, though a few stayed and drifted toward the staff table with the ease of students who had nothing to fear from this particular change.

"We should go," Miren said.

They went. Through the corridors to the Hufflepuff basement, past the barrels, through the round door into the common room that smelled, as it always did, of earth and warm wood and whatever the kitchens were making next door. The common room was the same — the plants in the windows, the round porthole-like panes showing nothing but the castle's stone foundations and dark earth, the copper fixtures and the low ceiling and the fat yellow armchairs that had been there since Gavin's first year.

He sat down in one of them and the upholstery was warm from the fire and the cushion had the shape his body already knew and for a moment, just that moment, the wrongness of the evening pulled back and he was a fifth-year in his common room on the first night of term and the year ahead was just a year.

Then Miren sat beside him and said, quietly, "We need to figure out who's missing. All the houses. I'm going to start a list." She had a piece of parchment already — she must have brought it from home. She unfolded it on her knee and Gavin saw names in her small, definite handwriting, the missing Hufflepuffs she'd been counting since August, and now she was adding columns for the other houses, leaving blank spaces for names she didn't have yet. Her quill moved in quick, certain strokes. She wasn't angry anymore, or the anger had turned into something she could do, which was better than the kind that sat in your chest and didn't go anywhere, which was Gavin's kind.

Duncan went upstairs without saying good night.

---

The first weeks were an education in the distance between what a school looked like and what a school was.

The timetables were issued. The classes met. Homework was assigned and collected and returned with marks in the margins. The mechanisms of Hogwarts continued to function, and the students moved through them with a new drag in every step. Nothing had stopped, but everything took more effort than it used to.

Dark Arts under Amycus Carrow meant desks shoved against the walls and a burnt smell that clung to robes after the bell. The Muggle Studies lesson Gavin kept coming back to was on a Thursday morning in the second week, when Alecto Carrow spent forty minutes explaining Duncan's life back to him.

A brazier burned by her desk although the day was mild. The room smelled of hot metal and chalk dust. On the board she'd written *Muggle Adaptation* and drawn a plug beside it in neat white lines.

"Flett. Up."

Duncan stood so fast his chair legs squealed on the stone.

"Tell the class how your father gets light in the house."

"He turns on a lamp," Duncan said.

"Using?"

"Electricity."

Alecto nodded, brisk and satisfied. "Write this down. Muggles build machines because they can't do magic. Every wire in their walls is an admission of lack."

Behind Gavin, a quill stopped scratching. Miren said, "That's not true."

Alecto looked at her. "Did I ask you?"

Miren's jaw set. She didn't speak again.

Alecto turned back to Duncan. "And when the power fails?"

"It gets dark."

A few people laughed because the answer sounded stupid once she'd made him say it. The laugh died at once, but Duncan had heard it. He sat when she flicked two fingers at him, caught the edge of the desk with his thigh hard enough to rattle the inkpot, and bent over his notes until the bell. When Gavin passed him in the corridor afterward, Duncan's quill had split at the nib and there was a blue-black stain across his fingers with a bead of blood welling at the side of his thumb.

Transfiguration was the same as it had been. Charms was the same. Herbology, Potions, History of Magic — these ran as they always had, the professors doing their work with a carefulness that felt deliberate, as if maintaining their subjects was a form of resistance, or at least of insistence. Professor Sprout gave longer assignments than usual and spent more class time on them, and Gavin suspected this was her way of keeping students in her greenhouse and out of the corridors where the Carrows walked.

The corridors between classes had changed. Students moved quickly and in groups, not lingering as they used to — nobody sat on the stone window ledges to talk, nobody leaned against the walls outside classrooms waiting for friends. The Carrows walked the upper floors between lessons, separately, and their presence reached the hallways before they did, a small chill and a tightening in everyone's pace. Students learned to check the corridor ahead before turning a corner. Gavin learned it too, without thinking much about it, another small habit that kept him from being noticed.

In the evenings the common room was fuller than it had been in other years. Students who used to study in the library or wander the castle after dinner came straight back and stayed put. The room was warm and the fire was always going and the Carrows were not there, and that was enough. People did homework and played Gobstones and talked in the low voices that had become the standard volume for indoor conversation. Miren worked on her list most nights, comparing what she heard from other houses with what she could count at meals. She had a system — parchment organised by house, names in columns, absences marked in red — and several people gave her information without being asked. Duncan did his homework with the lamp pulled close and his handwriting neat and small, and he went to bed early, and he didn't talk about what happened in his classes with the Carrows. His hands sometimes shook when he packed his books away, and he was careful to set them down slowly so the shaking wouldn't show.

Gavin studied. He attended class. He did his homework with the slow thoroughness of someone who needed thoroughness because quickness wasn't available to him. He was mediocre at all of it and the mediocrity was familiar and in a strange way comforting — a piece of his life that the year's strangeness hadn't touched.

Three weeks in, on a Tuesday night in late September, he was in the library working on a Binns essay. The topic was the Statute of Secrecy: its origins, its implementation, its major revisions. Binns had assigned it with the same droning indifference he brought to everything, as though the political crisis currently restructuring the wizarding world had no bearing on a historical analysis of wizarding secrecy legislation, which maybe for a ghost it didn't.

The library was nearly empty. Madam Pince sat at her desk with her lamp and her eagle-feather quill and her expression of permanent suspicion, which had deepened this year into something closer to fear. She watched the few remaining students with the guarded suspicion of a shop owner serving customers she didn't trust but couldn't refuse. The Restricted Section behind its rope was darker than Gavin remembered, though he might have been imagining that — this year lent itself to imagining things darker. Two Ravenclaws studied at a table near the windows. A first-year had fallen asleep on an open copy of One Thousand Magical Herbs and Fungi, her cheek pressed against an illustration of a Bouncing Bulb. Gavin sat at his usual spot, a table near the back, close to the History of Magic shelves, and worked through his essay with the steady, uninspired competence that characterised everything he did.

He was checking a citation in A History of Magic — Bagshot's account of the Wizards' Council debates preceding the Statute, somewhere in the seven-forties — when he turned a page too far and his eye caught on a footnote at the bottom of page 742.

It was small type. Parenthetical. The kind of footnote that existed to show the author had been thorough rather than to convey anything the author thought mattered. Bagshot's footnotes were like that — dense, fussy, mostly about sourcing decisions, the scholarly equivalent of showing your working.

This one read:

*The question of a pre-wand magical substrate, occasionally raised by Continental theorists of the Sardinian school, was investigated by the author during the preparation of this work. A manuscript purporting to describe such a substrate was consulted in the Hogwarts collection (sub-collection, uncatalogued) and found to be of uncertain provenance and doubtful reliability. The notation system employed bears no resemblance to any known runic or proto-runic script. The manuscript's claims regarding the nature of magical law are inconsistent with the established theoretical framework and are not treated further in this text.*

Gavin read it. He read it again.

His essay was about the Statute of Secrecy. The footnote had nothing to do with the Statute of Secrecy. He had only seen it because he'd turned one page past what he needed, and Bagshot had buried it in print small enough to say she'd looked into it while also saying she didn't think much of what she'd found.

Pre-wand magical substrate. He didn't know what that meant, exactly. Wand magic was magic — the thing they learned, the thing the school taught, the thing that made the wizarding world wizarding. A substrate beneath it would be like finding out there was a floor under the floor you'd been walking on. Which was probably nonsense, which was probably why Bagshot had dismissed it, but the phrase sat in his head with the particular weight of a thing that was either nothing or not nothing, and he couldn't tell which yet.

The manuscript was in the Hogwarts collection. A sub-collection, uncatalogued. Somewhere in the school, in a part of the library system that didn't appear in the catalogue — he had used the catalogue enough times to know he'd never seen a section labelled "sub-collection." Bagshot had found it, or someone had shown it to her, and she had read it and decided it was unreliable, and she had written this footnote, and that had been that.

He looked up. Pince was still at her desk. The Ravenclaws had gone. The first-year was still asleep on her textbook, and her arm had shifted so that her hand hung off the edge of the table, fingers loose. The library was quiet with the particular quality of Hogwarts at night — the building settling around itself, the faint sounds of portraits shifting in their frames, the wind outside and the creak of old wood adjusting to the cold.

Gavin folded the corner of page 742 down. It left a small crease, barely visible.

He went back to his essay. He finished the paragraph about the Wizards' Council vote of 1692, wrote two more sentences about enforcement mechanisms, and put his quill down because his hand was cramping and he was tired and the library was closing in ten minutes anyway. The essay was adequate. It was always adequate.

He packed his bag. He shelved the reference books he'd pulled but kept his own copy of A History of Magic — the one with the dog-eared page — and put it in his bag on top of his Transfiguration notes.

The corridors back to Hufflepuff were cold. The castle was colder this year on the lower floors — someone had said the Carrows had adjusted the heating for the basement levels, though Gavin didn't know if that was true or just the kind of thing people said when they needed the administration's pettiness to have a shape they could point at. His footsteps echoed on the stone. A portrait of a round-faced witch in a green hat watched him pass and said nothing, which portraits usually didn't, late at night, unless you startled them.

He tapped the barrel in the rhythm Professor Sprout had set for the year, and the round door swung open, and the common room was empty except for the fire, which was low, and the smell of bread from the kitchens. He crossed to the stairs and went up to the dormitory and got into bed without lighting his lamp because his roommates were asleep and the curtains around their beds were drawn and the only light was the faint glow from the window, which was underground and showed nothing but the pale roots of some plant pressing against the glass from outside.

He lay there. He thought about the essay, which he'd hand in tomorrow and which would come back with an A in Binns's faded ink. He thought about the Charms homework he needed to start. He thought about Duncan not eating at the feast and going to bed without a word and what it would be like to sit through a class designed to teach you that your parents were less than human.

He thought about the footnote. Pre-wand magical substrate. A manuscript in the Hogwarts collection, uncatalogued, in a part of the library system he'd never seen referenced anywhere else. Bagshot had looked at it and decided it was nothing — uncertain provenance, doubtful reliability, a notation system she couldn't read. She'd written her footnote and moved on and nobody, as far as Gavin could tell, had followed the thread.

It was probably nothing. It was almost certainly nothing. Bagshot was a real historian and she'd dismissed it and that should have been the end of it. But the thread was loose now. He hadn't gone looking for it, but his finger had caught, and tomorrow it would still be there.

Sooner or later, he'd look into it. Not urgently. Not with any particular expectation. He wanted to know what was in the uncatalogued part of the collection and why Bagshot had bothered to mention it at all.

He rolled over. The pillow was cool on the other side. From the next bed he could hear Ernie Macmillan breathing in the slow, controlled pattern that meant Ernie was awake and pretending not to be, and from somewhere deeper in the castle the pipes clanked once and the building gave one last heavy shift before going still.

Gavin closed his eyes. The footnote sat in his bag in the dark, on a dog-eared page, in small type, waiting for whenever he got round to it.


# Chapter 2

The catalogue didn't have it.

The next afternoon, Gavin had been through every drawer that made sense and a few that didn't. The library's filing system went by topic rather than author, which meant he had to guess what heading someone, decades or centuries ago, had decided fit a manuscript about pre-wand magical substrates. He tried "Substrate." He tried "Pre-Wand." He tried "Magical Theory (Historical)" and "Foundational Studies" and "Notation Systems" and, feeling slightly desperate, "Old." Nothing. The manuscript that Bagshot cited in a footnote on page 742 of her *History* was not listed. There was no misfile, no cross-reference. It simply wasn't in the system.

He leaned against the cabinet and looked at the footnote again. It was still there, small and parenthetical: a reference to a manuscript in the Hogwarts collection concerning what Bagshot called, with apparent distaste, a "speculative account of a pre-wand magical substrate." She'd dismissed it in the same breath she cited it. The kind of footnote you could read fifty times and never notice if you weren't in the specific frame of mind that made it snag.

The library was quiet. It was always quiet this year, but the quality had changed — less the comfortable hush of students working and more the silence of people who were trying not to be noticed. Two fourth-year Ravenclaws sat at a corner table, their heads close together over a single textbook, and a sixth-year Gavin didn't recognize was copying something from a reference volume near the window. The window showed darkening grounds. October had settled in and the afternoons were shortening fast, the sky going grey and staying there.

Madam Pince was at her desk.

She looked worse than Gavin remembered from last year. Thinner in the face, and she'd developed a habit of arranging the quills on her desk by length — lining them up, adjusting one, lining them up again — that had the rhythm of something she didn't know she was doing. A pair of dragonhide reading gloves sat on the desk beside her, the fingertips worn smooth from decades of handling old parchment. She'd been wearing them earlier; now they sat neatly aligned with the quills, another object she was keeping in order.

He walked over. She looked up before he'd gotten within ten feet, which was normal for Pince. What wasn't normal was the way her eyes went immediately to his hands, checking what he was carrying.

"Mr. Tench."

"Hi. Um, Madam Pince. I'm looking for a manuscript that's supposed to be in the collection, but I can't find it in the catalogue."

She didn't respond immediately. She picked up one of the quills and put it back down in exactly the same position. "The catalogue is comprehensive."

"Yeah, I know, but this one — it's cited in *A History of Magic*. Bagshot references it. A manuscript about pre-wand magical substrates. She says it's in the Hogwarts collection."

Something happened in Pince's face. A slight tightening around her mouth, as if she'd bitten down on something she didn't want. She reached for the reading gloves and began folding them, precisely, along creases that were already well established.

"Bagshot's footnotes aren't always reliable," she said.

"Right, but she specifically says it's here. 'In the Hogwarts collection.' So either she's wrong about it being here, or —"

"She is wrong." Pince folded the gloves a second time, which wasn't necessary, and set them down. "I have managed this collection for twenty-seven years, Mr. Tench. If a manuscript existed under any heading related to pre-wand magical substrates, I would be aware of it. Bagshot cited a great many sources she never verified personally. That footnote is an error."

She said it the way you'd say something you'd decided was true before the conversation started.

"OK," Gavin said. "What about the Restricted Section? Could it be —"

"The Restricted Section is restricted. You'd need a signed permission form from a member of staff, which I strongly doubt anyone will provide this term." Her voice dropped half a register, and she glanced toward the library entrance and then back. "In any event, I've reviewed every item in that section within the past three years and there's nothing matching your description."

Pince smoothed a thumb along the folded glove. Her eyes went to the entrance again.

Gavin stood there for a moment. He had the textbook under his arm and his bag was heavy on his shoulder and the fourth-years were glancing over and Pince was staring at him with the fixed intensity of a woman who had built her life around the orderly management of books and was now watching everything she managed come under the authority of people who would burn them.

"OK," he said. "Thanks."

He walked back to the table where he'd left his things. He packed his bag slowly. When he glanced up, Pince was still watching the door, the gloves folded into a hard little square under her hand.

He walked out of the library and took the stairs down toward the common room, thinking about the Restricted Section.

***

He went at half past midnight.

The three days between the conversation with Pince and the night he actually tried it were spent considering the risk, which was considerable. Sneaking into the Restricted Section had always been a punishable offense, but "punishable" had a different meaning this year. Last year it would have meant lost house points and a detention scrubbing trophies. This year it meant the Carrows.

He thought about it anyway. The footnote kept pulling at him. He'd catch himself in Potions or Charms thinking about what "pre-wand magical substrate" might mean, and the thought sat in his head like a loose tooth — not urgent, not obsessive, just there. You couldn't stop checking it.

On Thursday night he lay in his dormitory listening to his roommates breathe and decided to go. The decision didn't feel brave. It felt like the next thing.

The corridors were dark. Gavin didn't risk a Lumos — too visible from too far away. He navigated by touch and memory, one hand trailing the wall, counting the turns. The castle at night had its own sounds: the settle of old stone, the drip of water somewhere below, the occasional groan of a staircase repositioning itself floors above him. His shoes were too loud on the flagstones. He took them off and carried them and his socks were immediately cold and wet from the stone.

The library door was locked. He tried Alohomora, which worked, which surprised him — he'd expected something more serious. The door swung open into the dark and the smell of the library hit him, old paper and binding glue and dust that had been settling in the same place for centuries.

The Restricted Section was at the back, behind a rope. In daylight the rope looked like a boundary. At night, in the dark, it was just a rope. He stepped over it.

His wand-hand wanted to light up. He let it, barely — the faintest Lumos he could manage, just enough to read the spines. The books in the Restricted Section didn't like being looked at. Some of them whispered. One, bound in what looked like actual skin, vibrated faintly against its shelf as he passed. He ignored them and scanned the titles, looking for anything about substrates, pre-wand magic, anything that matched Bagshot's description.

He was in the third row, crouching to read the lower shelves, when he heard the shuffle.

It wasn't footsteps. It was a shuffle — the scraping gait of someone who walked with one foot dragging, accompanied by a thin, reedy sound that might have been breathing or might have been a cat.

Filch.

Gavin's stomach dropped. He killed the Lumos and stood still. The dark closed in and the whispering books seemed louder. He heard the lantern before he saw it — the creak of the handle, the faint hiss of the flame. Then the light came around the end of the row, uneven and jumping, thrown through a cracked glass that cut the beam into crooked segments.

Argus Filch stepped into the Restricted Section holding his lantern high, and Mrs. Norris wound between his ankles, and he was smiling. It was the smile of a man having a good night. Under the old regime, catching a student out of bed had been the highlight of Filch's week. Under this one, it was something better, because now there were consequences he didn't have to administer himself.

"Well, well," Filch said. His voice had a wheeze behind it. His cardigan was buttoned wrong — one button off from the bottom, so the whole thing sat skewed across his chest — and a cold sore sat at the corner of his mouth, raw and uncovered. He didn't seem to know it was there, or he'd stopped caring. "Out of bed, in the Restricted Section, no permission slip. That's a nice evening you're having."

"I was just —"

"Save it. You're not the first and you won't be the last. On your feet. Shoes on." He noticed the shoes in Gavin's hand and his smile widened. "Trying to be quiet about it. That's an extra helping, that is."

Gavin put his shoes on. His hands were shaking and he fumbled with the laces and Filch watched him fumble with the patience of a man who didn't mind waiting.

Mrs. Norris sat on the floor and cleaned her paw. She was old, Gavin noticed. Her eyes were the same lamp-yellow they'd always been, but the fur around her muzzle had gone white and there was a faint rattle in her breathing.

Filch marched him out of the library and down two corridors and up a flight of stairs and through a door Gavin had never been through before, into a room that had been a storage closet and was now bare. The floor was stone, the walls were stone, and the only furniture was a desk and a chair, both shoved against the far wall. The room was cold — the kind of cold that gets into your knees the moment they touch the ground.

Amycus Carrow was sitting on the desk.

He wasn't doing anything. He was just sitting there, legs dangling, as if he'd been waiting, and maybe he had. The Carrows rotated detention shifts, and Filch had known where to bring him, which meant this was organized. Carrow wore his robes with the sleeves pushed up to the elbows, his forearms thick and pale and hairy, and he was chewing something — gum or a toothpick or the inside of his cheek — with a slow, grinding motion that made his jaw look even more lopsided than it was.

"Restricted Section," Filch said, with the pleased air of a man making a delivery.

"Right." Carrow looked at Gavin. He didn't look angry. He looked the way you'd look at a task you didn't mind doing. "What's your name?"

"Tench. Gavin Tench."

"House?"

"Hufflepuff."

"Year?"

"Fifth."

Carrow nodded slowly, still chewing. "Hufflepuff. Fifth year. In the Restricted Section at —" He looked at Filch.

"Quarter to one," Filch said.

"Quarter to one in the morning without a permission slip." Carrow slid off the desk. He was shorter than Gavin expected, shorter standing than he'd looked sitting, squat and physically graceless in a way that made the authority he carried seem borrowed. His face had a pushed-in quality, like a thumb had pressed between his eyebrows when the clay was still wet. "What were you looking for, Tench?"

"A book. For a — for homework."

"Homework." Carrow said the word like he was tasting it. "At quarter to one. In the Restricted Section. What class?"

"Ancient Runes."

"Babbling's class?" Something crossed Carrow's face — not recognition exactly, but the flicker of a man sorting through what he knew about the staff and what he might do with it. The flicker passed. "Kneel."

Gavin didn't understand at first. He stood there.

"On the floor, Tench. Kneel down."

He knelt. The stone hit his knees and the cold went straight through his trousers and into the bone, and the cold was the first thing, the thing he would remember later when the rest got blurred. The stone was uneven. His right knee had landed on a ridge in the flagstone and it dug in like a blunt edge.

"Arms up. Over your head."

He raised his arms.

"You'll hold them there," Carrow said. He walked around behind Gavin. His shoes were heavy on the stone, unhurried. Filch was still in the doorway. Mrs. Norris had gone. "Restricted means restricted, Tench. Not a hard word, is it?" He came back around and sat on the desk again, wand out, resting it across his thigh. "Your arms come down, you get hexed. They come down again, you get hexed again. We keep going till I reckon you've learned something." He settled in. "Arms up, Tench. I can see them dropping."

They weren't dropping. Not yet. His arms were straight above his head and the position was uncomfortable but holdable. For now.

After three minutes, his shoulders began to burn. After five, the burn spread into his upper arms and across his back, and his hands started to tingle. The cold of the stone floor had moved up from his knees into his thighs, a dull, deep ache that was getting worse in a way that had no ceiling to it.

After ten minutes, his arms began to shake.

"Dropping," Carrow said.

"I'm not —"

The stinging hex caught him across the shoulders.

It wasn't like pain from impact — there was no weight behind it, no sense of being struck by something physical. It was more like someone had dragged a wire across his skin and then set the wire on fire. The line of it ran from his left shoulder blade to his right, and his body tried to arch away from it, but there was nothing to arch away from because the hex was already done. It just burned where it had been.

Gavin gasped. The sound came out smaller than he expected. His arms had dropped to his sides, both of them, and his hands were clenched into fists.

"Arms up."

He raised them. The hex-burn across his shoulders screamed at the movement. He held the position. His arms shook.

Carrow sat on the desk and watched. He chewed. He hummed, toneless and low, the kind of sound you'd make while waiting for a kettle to boil.

Gavin's arms dropped at sixteen minutes. He didn't decide to let them drop. His muscles gave out — the deltoids first, then the whole structure, and his arms came down like they'd been cut loose. The stinging hex hit his upper arms this time, both of them, a crossing pattern, and the pain layered on what was already there and his vision went white at the edges for a second and then came back. He heard himself make a sound.

"Arms up, Tench."

He raised them. They went up slower this time and stopped lower, his elbows bending where they hadn't bent before. The ridge in the flagstone was still digging into his right knee and he couldn't shift off it without Carrow seeing.

The next drop came at twenty-two minutes. Then nineteen. Then fourteen. Then ten. Each time, the hex. Each time, the order to raise them again. Carrow never raised his voice. He never showed frustration or impatience. He sat on the desk and delivered the hexes with the same steady timing, and between them he talked — about the importance of rules, about what happened to students who thought they were special, about blood purity and the natural order — the phrases sounding memorized and slightly wrong in his mouth, like lyrics he'd learned phonetically.

After the first hour, the intervals between drops shortened to five or six minutes. Gavin's arms went up shaking and stayed there only until the shaking turned to failure. When he managed to get them nearly straight, Carrow waited for the smallest bend and called it dropping anyway. Once the hex landed low and sideways, across Gavin's left ribs. He couldn't tell if Carrow had meant that or not; the line of fire folded him over on the stone before he dragged himself upright again.

The hex-burns had stopped being individual events. They overlapped now, layered across Gavin's shoulders and arms and upper back and the left side of his ribs in a lattice of hot lines that his shirt pressed against. The pain didn't ebb. It kept burning at the same intensity instead of fading the way a cut or a bruise would. His knees were worse. They'd gone from cold to numb to a deep, grinding ache, and the right one was definitely bleeding where the ridge in the stone had worked through the fabric of his trousers.

At some point Filch left. Gavin heard the shuffle of his shoes in the corridor and then it was just him and Carrow, and Carrow's humming was the only sound in the room besides Gavin's breathing, which had gone ragged.

"How long?" Filch had asked, earlier, from the doorway. The question was practical, not sympathetic. He wanted to know if he could leave.

"Hour should do it," Carrow had said. "Maybe two. We'll see how he does."

It was closer to three.

"You can go," Carrow said eventually.

Gavin tried to lower his arms and couldn't. They were locked above his head in a cramp that felt like the muscles had solidified. He brought them down in stages, his shoulders making sounds he'd never heard from his own body, and then he tried to stand and couldn't do that either. His knees wouldn't unbend. He had to lean to one side and use the wall and pull himself up while his legs did something that wasn't quite supporting him, and Carrow watched it all without helping or commenting, still chewing, still sitting on the desk with his legs dangling, and the watching was part of it — the lesson extended to include the indignity of a boy unable to stand up on his own.

He stood. His legs felt like they belonged to someone larger and less coordinated. The burns across his back and shoulders and arms throbbed in unison, a single pulsing heat.

"Off you go, Tench. Stay out of the Restricted Section."

Gavin left. He didn't say anything. There was nothing to say that wouldn't be another opportunity for Carrow to teach him something.

***

The corridors were empty and cold and Gavin walked through them badly.

His knees had been locked in position for three hours and each step sent a spike up through his thighs. His left side hurt too, a hard hot band under the ribs that pulled whenever he twisted. The hex-burns were settling into something more like sunburn — a constant, radiating heat across his back and arms that the cold air of the corridor sharpened rather than soothed. His palms had been clenched for so long that the fingernail marks were bleeding, half-moons pressed into the meat of each hand. His robes had a wet patch on the right knee where the stone had worked through.

He needed to get back to the Hufflepuff common room. He was on the third floor. The route was familiar — down two flights to the ground floor, left past the Great Hall, down the last stairs to the kitchen corridor, and the barrel entrance at the end.

He went down the first flight without problems. The second flight moved while he was on it. The staircases at Hogwarts shifted constantly, and normally you just waited for them to reconnect or took a different route, but Gavin's legs were unreliable and the shift caught him off balance and he grabbed the railing and held on and the staircase deposited him on a landing he didn't recognize.

He should have gone back up and waited for the staircase to cycle around. He didn't. He was tired and his knees hurt and he wanted to be in bed, so he went forward, through a door that was standing open, into a corridor that was narrower than it should have been at this level of the castle. The stone was different here. Darker. Rougher-cut. The torches that normally lined the Hogwarts corridors were gone, and the light came from somewhere he couldn't identify — a faint luminescence from the walls themselves, not warm like firelight but cold, the color of old ice.

He stopped.

The air was different. Drier, cooler, with a mineral quality that reminded him of the time his parents had taken him to a cave system in Devon when he was eight. Limestone and stillness and the pressure of being underground even when you weren't underground.

He was on the seventh floor. He was certain of it because the staircase had gone up instead of down and the landing had been marked with a VII carved into the archway, barely visible under centuries of soot. The seventh floor was where the Room of Requirement was, though this was nowhere near the tapestry of Barnabas the Barmy. This was somewhere else entirely, a part of the castle he hadn't been in before and hadn't known about.

The corridor turned, and then turned again, and opened into a small room.

It was barely a room — more of an alcove that someone had given a ceiling. The walls were the same dark, rough stone, and the sourceless light was the same, and there were shelves cut directly into the rock. Not attached, not built — carved out, as though the room had been made around them. Most of the shelves were empty. A few held objects Gavin couldn't make sense of in the dim light, things that were the wrong size or shape for books.

Against the far wall was a cabinet. Small, wooden, very old. The wood was almost black with age and the brass fittings had gone green. It had three drawers, narrower and deeper than the ones in the library catalogue, and the labels on the drawers were in a hand Gavin didn't recognize — written in ink that had faded to a brown so pale it was barely legible.

He opened the first drawer.

The cards inside were not paper. They were something thinner, smoother, slightly stiff — the material of very old documents, though he couldn't name it. Each card was smaller than the library's catalogue cards, about the size of a playing card, and the writing was in the same faded brown ink.

He could read it, though. It was English — old, but English. Each card began with a collection line and a shelf number in the same faded brown hand, then listed a title, a description, and a location expressed in terms that meant nothing to him: directions that referenced rooms by qualities rather than numbers. *Third left past the cold room. Below the room where the air hums. Past the narrow place, up, and right at the smell of water.*

He went through the cards one by one. His hands left faint red smears on them because his palms were still bleeding where the nails had dug in, and on the fourteenth card he found it.

At the top of the card was *Sub-Collection C. Pre-Foundation Acquisitions.* Off to one side, in smaller writing the color of dust, was a shelf number. The title beneath it was not in English. It was in a notation he didn't recognize — a set of marks that didn't map to any alphabet he knew. But below the title, in the same faded brown handwriting, someone had written a description: *A teaching instrument concerning the substrate upon which all secondary magical structures depend. Not to be removed.*

Gavin read it twice. His knees throbbed. His back burned. The light from the walls was too dim and he had to hold the card close to his face to make out the location written below the description: *Past the narrow place, up, through the room of empty shelves, left where the stone changes color.*

He put the card back in the drawer and closed it.

He stood there in the small room with its carved shelves and its ancient cabinet and its cold, dry air, and the pain in his knees and back felt very far away — not because it had lessened but because something else was closer. The card's heading stayed in his head: *Sub-Collection C. Pre-Foundation Acquisitions.* There were no author names and no subject tabs, only rooms known by temperature and sound and smell.

He looked at the shelves again. The objects on them were still indistinct — dark shapes he couldn't resolve. He didn't touch them. The card had said *Not to be removed*, and the instruction was not addressed to him specifically, but it had the quality of a rule that was still in force.

He needed to leave. His knees were bad and his back was bad and it was sometime between three and four in the morning and he had Potions at nine. He left the room and went back down the corridor, and the corridor was cooperative this time — it led to a staircase that went down, and the staircase connected where he needed it to, and within twenty minutes he was at the barrel entrance to the Hufflepuff common room, tapping the rhythm, and the barrel opened, and the common room was dark and warm and smelled of earth and whatever the kitchens were baking for morning, and he went to his dormitory and got into bed without undressing and lay on his stomach because his back wouldn't tolerate the mattress any other way.

His shoulders and arms pulsed. His knees felt like they'd been worked over with hammers. His palms stuck to the sheet where the half-moon cuts had dried tacky.

He thought about the card: *Sub-Collection C. Pre-Foundation Acquisitions,* the little shelf number in dust-brown ink, the title he couldn't read, the description in old brown ink: *a teaching instrument concerning the substrate upon which all secondary magical structures depend.* The location directions that assumed you could identify a room by the temperature of its air or the sound a wall made.

He had found it. Not the manuscript itself, but where it was. And he'd found it not because he was clever or skilled or brave — he'd found it because Carrow had beaten him for three hours and the staircase had moved and he'd been too tired and hurt to go back the right way.

His right knee was definitely bleeding. He could feel it, a warm stickiness inside his trouser leg that was different from the rest of the pain, more ordinary, more familiar. Like a skinned knee from falling off a bicycle, except he hadn't fallen off anything. He'd been kneeling.

He closed his eyes. The room was dark and his roommates were breathing and tomorrow he had Potions and his knees were ruined and his back was a map of hex-burns and somewhere up on the seventh floor, past the narrow place and the room of empty shelves and the stretch where the stone changed color, there was a manuscript that Bathilda Bagshot had dismissed in a footnote and Madam Pince had tried to push him away from.

He fell asleep with his shoes on.


# Chapter 3

The first flight left his knees throbbing. Gavin stopped on the landing, hand tight on the banister, and waited for the ache to ebb before he made himself keep climbing toward the seventh floor.

This was not strategy or discipline. He'd waited three days because the bruises from Carrow's detention still ached through his kneecaps whenever he climbed stairs, and the seventh floor was a long way up from the Hufflepuff basement. His left side was tender where a stinging hex had caught him across the ribs — whether Carrow had aimed there or was just a bad shot, Gavin hadn't decided. Either way, stairs were a problem.

He was going tonight because the ache had settled from sharp to dull and because he had not been able to stop thinking about the catalogue entry. _Sub-Collection C. Pre-Foundation Acquisitions._ It listed a shelf number in handwriting so old the ink had gone the color of dust and a title in a language he didn't recognize. The ink, at least, had stayed still.

He left after dinner. Miren was helping a second-year with a Switching Spell at one of the common room tables, her patience visibly thinning as the boy turned a teacup into a slightly different teacup for the fourth time running. Duncan had gone upstairs early, which he did most nights now — not because he was tired, Gavin thought, but because the common room required a version of himself that was getting harder to maintain. Nobody asked where Gavin was going. Nobody was watching him. He was the kind of person people's eyes moved past without stopping, and that had never bothered him, and tonight it was useful.

The seventh floor was quiet. It was an hour before curfew, but students avoided the upper corridors after dark this year — too many empty classrooms where Filch could find you, too close to the route the Carrows took between their quarters and Snape's office. Gavin kept his wand out and walked quickly, counting turns the way he'd memorized them from the catalogue room three nights ago. He turned left at the tapestry of the dancing trolls. He went straight past the suit of armor with the dented visor and one gauntlet missing. He turned right at the landing where a tall window looked out onto the grounds. The lake was a flat dark shape, and beyond it the Forbidden Forest was a darker one, and the sky above both was clear and cold and full of more stars than he'd ever seen from London.

The corridor he needed should have been the next left turn.

It wasn't. He stood at a junction he was certain had not existed on Sunday night, when he'd stumbled through these corridors with his knees bleeding through his trousers. The left branch went down — he could see the top of a spiral staircase. The right branch ended at a wall twenty feet away, lit by a single torch that popped and hissed in its bracket. Neither was right.

Gavin turned around and went back to the suit of armor. He counted again. He turned left at the tapestry, went straight past the armor, and turned right at the landing with the window. The junction was gone. In its place the corridor continued straight ahead in a shallow curve, and its far wall wore a pale wash of light.

There was light, though: a thin, pearl-grey sheen over the stone. It seeped from every surface at once, floor to ceiling, and held steady on every edge. The corridor looked scrubbed clean by it. The glow had the settled presence of something older than torches.

He stopped walking. The stone underfoot had changed. He noticed it the way you notice a step that's slightly higher than the others — his body registered the difference before his brain caught up. The blocks were larger here, paler, smoother. The grey had a chalk-and-ash cast the rest of the castle's stone never carried, as if it had been quarried from some deeper, cleaner layer of earth. A pale seam ran between the blocks, rough as dried paste from a few feet away and glittering like packed salt when he bent closer.

The air was different. It was colder by several degrees, and it was cave-dry, the kind of dry that came from stone holding the same air for centuries. It smelled of minerals, clean and cold, of limestone and still water and nothing alive.

Gavin walked in.

The corridor opened into a room. It was small — he could have crossed it in four long strides. The ceiling was low, maybe seven feet, and the sourceless light was a little brighter here, enough to see clearly by. Shelves were cut directly into the walls, and the stone of the shelves and the stone of the wall were the same continuous piece. Each shelf rose out of the wall with the smooth finish of river-cut rock. The shelves looked like they had formed that way, the way a riverbed forms ledges where the water runs over different kinds of rock.

A few objects sat on the lower shelves. There was a roughly round stone whose curves looked too even to be accidental, as if water or hands or time had kept correcting it. Beside it sat a piece of something that might have been metal — solid, smooth, and colored like tarnished straw seen through a skin of oil, yellow with a green-blue slip at the edges. Looking at it produced the same feeling as hearing a chord with one note slightly off — you knew something was wrong but you could not say which part. He did not touch either of them.

Through a doorway in the back wall he found a second room, smaller than the first, with six shelves cut into the stone on each side. A few held objects — another piece of the wrong-colored not-metal, a flat disc of something translucent, what appeared to be a very small cup made of the same pale stone as the walls. The light was the same sourceless glow. The air was the same dry mineral cold.

The silence was specific. He noticed it here, standing between the two doorways, his breathing the only sound. It had weight to it, as if the room had been holding its breath for centuries. The still air seemed layered, settled into place the way sediment settles in water. His own breathing seemed too loud, too wet, too alive for the space.

Through another doorway he found a third room. It was the smallest of the three. Three shelves were cut into each wall. Most of them were empty.

The manuscript was on the middle shelf of the far wall, and he knew it was the manuscript because there was nothing else it could be. The catalogue had said _sheet collection, unbound, material unclassified_, and that description was accurate the way saying the ocean was wet was accurate — true and completely useless as preparation.

The sheets were stacked loosely, not bound. He reached for the top one and his fingers met a skin-thin sheet with a faint waxy drag, softer at the edges than parchment and more supple than paper. It was thin and flexible and faintly warm. The warmth sat in it the way heat sits in paving stone after a long August day, except that it was October and this room was cold enough that he could feel the chill in his teeth.

The sheet did not feel old. That was the wrongest thing about it. He was standing in a room that smelled like it had been sealed since before the castle was a hill, holding something that the catalogue listed under _Pre-Foundation Acquisitions_, and it felt as if it had been made this morning. It felt unworn, untouched by the slow roughening that took the shine off everything else. The process of getting old was something that happened to other materials, and this one had not agreed to it.

He held the sheet up to the sourceless light.

There were marks on its surface. That much was clear. They lay dark against the warm-toned material in lines and clusters with the deliberate order of writing. Some stood alone like posts. Others hooked into one another or folded inward, building compact shapes inside larger ones. They shared the hard purposeful feel of the Sardinian temple marks and Balkan fragments in his Ancient Runes textbook, but they did not share their human logic.

These marks belonged to no category he knew.

Some of them bore a passing resemblance to Elder Futhark symbols, the way a cloud sometimes resembles a face. You could see it if you wanted to, but the resemblance was a product of your own pattern-matching, not anything the marks had intended. Other marks were shapes he had no category for: compound curves that did not resolve into anything he could name, angles that seemed to shift depending on where he focused, elements that might have been individual symbols or might have been parts of larger structures or might have been something outside the distinction between individual and composite entirely.

And they moved.

They did not move the way wizarding photographs moved. Gavin had been in the magical world long enough to know how that worked. Portraits moved because they were depictions of people, and the depicted people moved the way people moved. Photographs captured moments and replayed them. Even the writing on the Marauder's Map — everyone in school had heard the stories — tracked physical positions through physical space. All of it was mimetic, a picture of a thing doing what the thing did.

The marks on the sheet shifted with the slow purpose of pieces being set out for use. Two marks that had been adjacent separated. A third, from the far side of the sheet, drifted closer. A new configuration settled into place. The pattern had direction. It behaved like an explanation happening in front of him, only in a grammar he could not hear. It suggested a relationship between the marks, or between whatever concepts the marks represented, or between something Gavin did not have a word for because the word did not exist in any language he spoke.

He put the first sheet back and picked up another. It was made of the same material. It held the same warmth. It carried the same impossible resistance to age. The marks were different — different shapes, different arrangements — but the quality of their movement was the same. The relational patterns changed, but the feeling did not: he was watching something organized and meaningful and completely, absolutely beyond him.

He went through six sheets. Then ten. Then all fifty-seven. His fingers were tingling from contact with the warm material in the cold room, or from something else he couldn't identify, and his wand-light was starting to feel intrusive against the clean sourceless glow of the walls.

He counted the stack three times and got fifty-seven each time.

He looked around the small stone room with its seamless shelves and its ageless light and its mineral silence, and he thought about what he was going to do. Taking the manuscript felt like theft, even without a sign or a lock or a checkout card or Pince to forbid him. But the sub-collection was not the library. It was not part of the castle the way the classrooms and corridors and dormitories were part of the castle. It was older and it was other, and the things on these shelves had been placed here by someone or something that had assumed they would stay here, and that assumption had held for longer than the rest of Hogwarts had existed.

Gavin gathered the fifty-seven sheets into a stack. They were lighter than they should have been — lighter than the same volume of parchment, lighter than seemed right for how solid they felt. He slid them into his school bag between his Charms textbook and a roll of Potions notes.

The walk back through the three rooms felt shorter than the walk in. The corridor's pale stone gave way to the castle's regular grey, and a torch appeared on the wall, and then another, and the quality of light changed from sourceless and steady to the familiar flicker and shadow of a normal Hogwarts hallway. The air warmed. He could smell stone dust and old wood and the faint overlay of cooking from the kitchens somewhere below. He was standing on the seventh-floor landing by the window, and the grounds outside were dark, and the lake reflected a thin moon.

He went back to Hufflepuff. The barrel entrance thumped its rhythm and rolled open. The common room was mostly empty — three sixth-years near the fire, one of them with her legs draped across the other two's laps while all three read from the same textbook, arguing in murmurs about the properties of Bubotuber pus. Gavin crossed to the dormitory stairs and went up and put the manuscript in his trunk, underneath his spare robes and the Hufflepuff scarf he never wore because he didn't care about Quidditch, and he closed the lid and got into bed.

The sheets were down there in the dark, under the wool and cotton of things that aged normally. He could still feel the warmth of them on his fingertips, or thought he could. He lay on his back and stared at the canopy of his four-poster and tried to think about something else.

He was still trying when Owen Kirby came in an hour later, brushed his teeth at the bathroom sink with the noisy thoroughness he brought to everything, and fell asleep within minutes. Owen's breathing had the wheezing inhale-and-whistle that Gavin had been hearing since first year — Owen's lungs had never properly agreed with the castle's damp — and tonight the sound was almost comforting in its familiarity. Owen had left an unfinished letter to his mother on his nightstand, the quill laid across it at an angle, careful sentences visible even in the dim light. He wrote to her every week. He never said why.

Gavin listened to the whistle-and-pause. The manuscript was under his robes under his scarf in the trunk at the foot of his bed, and the marks on the fifty-seven sheets were moving in the dark, rearranging themselves into configurations that meant something to someone and nothing to him.

---

It took two weeks for the excitement to curdle into something worse.

The first three nights, he worked with the careful optimism of someone who believed the problem had a solution and the solution was effort. After the dormitory went quiet — Owen's whistle, Ben Alderton muttering about Quidditch formations in his sleep, the scratch and shuffle of someone's Pygmy Puff in its cage — Gavin pulled the sheets from his trunk, spread them across the mattress inside the closed curtains of his four-poster, lit his wand, and got to work.

He started with substitution analysis. If the notation was a cipher — each mark standing for a letter or a rune — then what he needed was the frequency distribution. He assigned numbers to the marks. Mark 1, angular, appeared twelve times across the first three sheets. Mark 2, a compound curve, appeared nine times. By the end of the first night he had forty-seven provisional marks and two pages of counts.

By the middle of the second night he was crossing numbers out. Shape was not enough. A mark that looked identical on one page sat beside different neighbors on the next and behaved like a different thing. Orientation changed it. Distance changed it. Whether a curve opened into a cluster or away from it changed it. He wrote _unit may be relation, not symbol_ in the margin of his notebook and stared at the sentence until it stopped feeling like language and started feeling like the problem itself.

He spent the next several days in the library pulling everything he could find on non-standard notation systems: goblin metalwork engravings, the Mermish syllabary, pre-Roman runework from the supplementary reading list Babbling had posted at the start of term. He found _Comparative Symbolic Systems of the Mediterranean Basin_ on a high shelf near the Restricted Section and spent an evening with it, cross-referencing illustrations of Sardinian temple inscriptions and Basque funerary notation against what he remembered from the manuscript. A few shapes triggered a flicker of recognition — a curve here, an angle there — but the useful part of the exercise was the part where each resemblance died. Every real writing system on the page assumed that marks stayed themselves long enough to be read. His did not.

He was careful to do this research openly, as if it were coursework. Anyone watching — and he was aware, now, that anyone could be watching, that this year the watchers included people with the authority to hurt you — would have seen a fifth-year doing extra reading for Ancient Runes, which was both boring and plausible and therefore invisible.

On the eighth night, lying on his back in the dark at two in the morning while Owen breathed and Ben muttered and the castle made its slow settling noises around him, Gavin thought: the marks were not writing.

He did not mean that in the sense of some other form of record — not pictures, not diagrams, not music notation. Writing was marks standing for other things. Letters stood for sounds. Runes stood for concepts or sounds or both. The manuscript did not feel like that. It felt closer to the thing itself, whatever the thing was. It was not a description of movement but movement arranged on a flat surface where a description ought to have been.

He did not know what that meant. But it was new, and because it was new he held onto it as if it were progress.

Miren noticed. She always noticed things.

It was a Wednesday of the second week, and they were at lunch, and Gavin sat at the Hufflepuff table shoving potatoes from one side of his plate to the other while thinking about whether the relational movement of the marks might encode temporal information, whether the sequence of configurations mattered, whether what he needed was a timeline instead of a key.

"You're not eating."

He looked up. Miren was across the table with her hair in its usual plait and her robe sleeves pushed up past her elbows the way she always wore them, and she was looking at him with an expression he recognized as the thing that came before concern — the attentive noticing that, given enough evidence, would harden into worry.

"I'm eating," he said. He ate a potato to prove it.

"You've been weird lately."

"I haven't been —" He stopped. "Weird how?"

"Like that." She pointed her fork at him. "You're sitting here but you're not here. You've been doing it for over a week. You're always somewhere else."

This was accurate enough that it was hard to argue with. "I'm just tired. Haven't been sleeping great."

"Since the detention?"

"Yeah," he said, and it was easy to let her think it was the detention, which had been bad enough to explain some sleeplessness. His knees still ached in the mornings. The stinging-hex bruise on his ribs had turned yellow-green and was taking its time about fading.

"I heard Harper got it worse," Miren said, and the conversation shifted to the familiar territory of who had been punished for what and how badly, which was the background noise of social life at Hogwarts this year — the constant low-grade accounting of damage. Gavin ate his lunch and contributed small agreeing sounds at the right moments and thought about temporal encoding and whether he'd been looking at the sheets wrong this whole time, reading them as static documents when they might be something closer to — what? A process? A demonstration?

Duncan was sitting on Miren's other side, eating with the focused attention he brought to meals this term. He had lost weight since September — not dramatically, but his collarbones showed through his neckline in a way they hadn't before, and his school robes had a careful mend over the right shoulder from something he'd never explained. He ate everything on his plate and two pieces of bread from the basket and did not volunteer anything to the conversation.

"There's pudding," Miren told him. "Treacle tart."

"I'm all right."

"Take some anyway. You like treacle tart."

Duncan took some. He ate it with the slow thoroughness of someone who'd learned to take food seriously when it was available. Miren watched him eat and did not say whatever she was thinking, and Gavin noticed the exchange the way he noticed the weather through the enchanted ceiling — something that was happening in the world around him that he registered without deciding to do anything about it.

That evening, back in his curtained bed with the sheets spread across his knees, Gavin tried the temporal approach. He selected Sheet 3 — the one where two marks in the upper left had maintained a consistent proximity even while everything else shifted — and set his watch on the mattress beside him. He mapped the full configuration at the start, then again at ten-minute intervals.

After an hour he had six maps. After two hours he had twelve. Laid in sequence, the page looked less like writing than a set of instructions acted out too slowly to follow. The two marks in the upper left held together through every change. Everything else bent around them.

That was enough to feel like a lead. He went back through the rest of the manuscript and found three more locked pairs — on Sheets 9, 14, and 16. For nearly an hour he believed they were fixed points, the equivalent of margins or coordinates, something you could sort the other sheets around. He spread page after page across the bed, grouping them by where the paired marks sat and how the surrounding clusters leaned toward them. The whole mess began to look almost organized.

Then one of the pairs on Sheet 14 opened just wide enough for a third mark to slide between them and stay there. The grouping collapsed at once.
