He stared at the page until his eyes watered and wrote down the new arrangement anyway, because having been wrong did not make the clue feel less real. It made it worse.

By the third week, he was angry.

The anger was specific and self-directed. The manuscript existed. The marks meant something. The relational movement was organized. All of this was obviously, frustratingly true, and the only obstacle between the truth and understanding was that Gavin Tench — fifteen years old, low Acceptable in Ancient Runes, middling marks in everything, exceptional at nothing — was not smart enough to bridge the gap. The problem in front of him had been set by someone or something that operated at a level of complexity he could not reach. He didn't know who or what had made the manuscript, but it had not been made for him.

He kept trying anyway, because the alternative was stopping.

He fell asleep in History of Magic on a Wednesday. Binns did not notice — Binns noticed nothing, had possibly not noticed that he himself was dead — but the girl beside him, a Ravenclaw with four different-colored quills arranged in a neat row and notes so dense and organized they looked like a printed textbook, nudged his elbow when his head slid off his hand and cracked against the desk hard enough to make the front row turn around.

"Sorry," Gavin said, straightening up. His neck ached. His eyes felt as if they'd been rolled in grit.

"You were drooling," the Ravenclaw said. She had a smudge of green ink on her left index finger and the weary patience of someone who had sat next to worse. "Binns didn't notice, but everyone else did."

"Sorry. Thanks."

She turned back to her color-coded notes without comment. Gavin opened his textbook to a page about the Goblin Rebellions of 1612 and stared at it. The ink on the page did not move. He thought about the paired marks he'd started calling stable and whether their consistent proximity implied a semantic connection or whether he was seeing structure in noise because he needed there to be structure in order to justify what he was doing with his nights.

He should stop. He knew this. He could put the sheets back in his trunk and close the lid and catch up on three weeks of neglected homework and get some sleep for the first time since the sub-collection. He could come back to the manuscript later — next year, maybe, or after OWLs, or when he'd learned more in Ancient Runes and had better tools and was older and could think more clearly. That was the sensible thing. Miren would have told him it was the sensible thing, if he'd told Miren, which he hadn't, because there was nothing he could tell her that would make sense. _I found some sheets in a hidden room and they've got marks on them that move and I can't read them and I've been trying every night for three weeks._ Even in his own head it sounded thin. It sounded like something a person made up to justify not sleeping.

He put the sheets away that night. He lay in the dark and listened to Owen's breathing and the Pygmy Puff and Ben Alderton saying something about a Wronski Feint in his sleep, the words slurred and earnest, and the castle making its settling noises all around them — the tiny groans of stone adjusting to stone, the sound of a building that was a thousand years old and still working out its relationship with gravity.

He got the sheets out again twenty minutes later.

He did not have a new approach. He had one thought he could not leave alone: if the paired marks were not fixed points, they might be directions. They might not be the answer itself. They might just tell him where on the page to look next. It was a thin distinction, almost stupid, but it was enough. He spread the sheets across his knees and lit his wand and looked at them, and the marks moved in their slow, deliberate patterns, and he watched them the way you watched a fire — not because you expected to learn anything from the flames, but because looking away had become harder than looking.

In the morning his eyes were dry and his Charms homework was four days overdue and he'd missed the first twenty minutes of breakfast. The seat next to Miren was taken by the time he reached the Great Hall. He ate toast standing at the end of the Hufflepuff table and went to Potions and produced such a dismal Strengthening Solution that Slughorn peered into his cauldron, sighed, and told him to tip the whole thing out and start again while everyone else bottled theirs.

That night he spread the sheets out again. The marks shifted into a configuration he had not seen before — a cluster on Sheet 5 rearranged itself so that three marks formed a triangular shape that seemed to point toward a fourth mark he'd been ignoring, one he'd classified as isolated because it never seemed to participate in the clusters. The shape held for ten minutes, then eleven, long enough for his pulse to pick up and his tiredness to drop away.

If the triangle was pointing, then it was pointing _at_ something. He pulled every page that carried the isolated mark, then every page where a three-mark cluster narrowed to a point, then his notebook, then the Runes text he was supposed to be reading for class. By the time the arrangement dissolved he had half the manuscript spread around him and no more idea what he was looking at than when he'd started. He wrote down the page numbers anyway, turned to another sheet, watched the marks for another hour, and fell asleep with his wand still lit and the pages still spread across his knees. He woke to Owen's alarm clock at half six with a crick in his neck and a crease from Sheet 11 pressed into his cheek.

He folded the sheets into his trunk and went to wash his face. In the bathroom mirror he looked tired in a specific and unflattering way — not dramatic, not interestingly haggard, just a kid who wasn't sleeping enough, with circles under his eyes and a dent in his cheek from falling asleep on something he shouldn't have been holding.

He went to class. He sat through Transfiguration and Charms and tried to concentrate and mostly didn't. He ate dinner and sat in the common room and did not do his homework and went to bed and waited for the dormitory to go quiet and got the sheets out again.

He did not think of this as a decision. It was just what his evenings were now — the thing he did instead of homework and sleep and sitting in the common room with people who would have talked to him if he'd been present enough to talk to. If anyone had asked him why, he would not have been able to give an answer that made sense, because the only honest answer was that the marks were there and they meant something and he was the only person looking at them, and putting them away felt like leaving a sentence half-read. He didn't understand a word of it. That was not the point. The sentence was there, and it was addressed to someone, and nobody else was trying, and so he kept sitting in his curtained bed with his wand-light guttering and his homework undone and the sheets spread warm across his knees, turning from one to the next to the next while the marks moved and moved and meant whatever they meant without him.

By then he knew one thing, even if he knew nothing else: he wasn't going to get anywhere alone, and if he wanted help he'd have to risk letting someone else see one page.


# Chapter 4

Once he'd decided to risk showing someone one page, he spent three days choosing which page to bring.

The manuscript had fifty-seven sheets — he had counted them twice, turning each one by the edges with his thumbs clear of the notation because even now he still half expected the material to react if he handled it like an ordinary page. Most of the sheets were dense with notation that moved when he looked at it, relationships between symbols shifting like tidal patterns he could not predict. But a few pages were sparser. One near the middle had what looked like paired entries: a symbol cluster on the left, a simpler arrangement on the right. If any page was a key, this was it.

He folded it carefully — the material was not paper and did not crease easily, taking the fold with a faint resistance, like bending something that was used to being flat — and slid it into his Ancient Runes textbook between the chapter on Elder Futhark variations and the one he had not read yet.

Ancient Runes was the last class on Wednesdays. The classroom was on the third floor, a small room that still smelled like a school — ink and old binding glue and the specific warmth of reference books that had been absorbing body heat from generations of students leaning over them. Professor Babbling had not changed the room this year. The shelves were still lined with comparative notation charts and stone fragment replicas and the framed rubbing of the Rök runestone that Babbling touched sometimes when she passed it, an absent, habitual gesture, like checking a doorknob.

The class was smaller this year. Fourteen students instead of twenty-two. Three had not returned to Hogwarts. Five had dropped the elective because OWL preparation was difficult to prioritize when the school was what it was. Gavin had almost dropped it himself, but Ancient Runes was one of the subjects where his marks were merely poor rather than embarrassing, and he liked the work. Babbling was a good teacher. She treated her students as if they were capable of adult attention, which was either flattering or exhausting depending on the week.

Today she was working through consonantal mutation patterns in late Anglo-Saxon runic inscriptions. Gavin took notes. The notation on the folded page in his textbook lay against the chapter heading like a foreign body the book was trying to reject.

He waited until the classroom emptied. This required less strategy than he had expected — the sixth-years left in a group, still arguing about a translation exercise, and the two other fifth-years were out the door before Babbling had finished writing the homework on the board. Gavin packed his bag slowly.

"Mr. Tench." Babbling was at her desk, sorting through the quiz papers from the previous week. Her reading glasses were on their chain, resting against her sternum. "Is there a question about the assignment?"

"No, I — it's not about that." He was already regretting this. She would think he was wasting her time. She would think it was a prank. She would look at the page and identify some known system that Gavin should have recognized from the Year Four curriculum, and then he would have to stand there being embarrassed while she explained it.

He pulled the textbook from his bag and removed the folded page.

"I found something," he said. "Some notation I can't identify. I was wondering if you could — I don't know. If it's anything."

He unfolded the page and set it on the edge of her desk, careful not to put it on top of her papers. The notation shifted as he smoothed it flat, the left-column symbols rearranging their internal relationships in a slow drift that made the page look almost alive.

Babbling looked at it.

Then she stopped looking at the quiz papers, and the pen in her hand went still, and she picked up the page by one corner and brought it closer.

She put on her reading glasses.

For perhaps ten seconds she said nothing. Gavin watched her eyes move across the notation, tracking the symbol pairs, going back to the top, starting again. Her lips parted slightly and then closed. She set the page down on a clear space on the desk and leaned back in her chair. She took one slow, visible breath before she spoke, and when the question came it was almost too even.

"Where did you find this?"

"In the library. Sort of. In an old —" He stopped. The sub-collection was not something he wanted to explain. "An old reference collection. It was uncatalogued."

"I see." Babbling looked at the page again. She touched the edge of it with one ink-stained finger, testing the material. "This is not parchment."

"No."

"And this notation — it is moving. Not in the photographic sense. The relationships are active."

"Yeah. I've been watching it for — for a while. It shifts, but it's not random. There are patterns."

Babbling took off her reading glasses and cleaned them, which Gavin recognized as the thing she did when she needed a moment. She threaded them back on and looked at the page a third time.

"If I had to place it anywhere," she said, "I would put it before the families we teach as distinct had separated into recognizable forms. A few of the joins remind me, very faintly, of early western Mediterranean stones, but the resemblance stops at shape. The organizing rule is doing something else entirely, and I do not know any attested system that behaves like this." She stopped with her mouth still slightly open, then pressed two fingertips to the desk. "Those paired entries look instructional to me. One form is elaborated, the other reduced. That is how primers behave."

"A teaching text," Gavin said.

"Possibly. I would want to examine more of it before drawing conclusions. Considerably more." She paused. Her fingers were very still on the desk. "How much more is there?"

"Fifty-six other pages."

Something tightened in her face — not a whole expression, just a brief hard line at the corners of her mouth — and then it was gone. She nodded once, briskly.

"I am not going to ask you where the full manuscript is stored, Mr. Tench. And I am not going to ask what you intend to do with it. I think you understand why."

He did. The Carrows. Unauthorized research. A professor showing interest in an uncatalogued manuscript that dealt with unknown magic was not something that could be explained away this year.

"But I can give you tools." She stood, crossed to the shelf behind her desk, and ran her finger along the spines until she found what she was looking for: a thin book, green cover, badly foxed. She pulled it out and held it for a moment before offering it. "Wexworth's comparative notation study. It is not widely read — Wexworth's conclusions were unfashionable in his time and the field has not revisited them. But his method for approaching unknown systems through structural invariants is, I believe, sound."

"Structural invariants?"

"Elements that remain constant regardless of content. In any notation system designed to encode meaning — as opposed to decorative or purely symbolic use — there are structural features that persist across different messages. Positional relationships. Frequency distributions. Boundary markers between conceptual units." She was speaking faster now, the academic in her overriding the caution. "You will not be able to read your manuscript by matching its symbols to known runes — I can tell you that now. Whatever this system is, it does not derive from any tradition I have studied, and I have studied all of them."

Gavin listened and held the information carefully, aware that she was giving him something she would not give again.

"But Wexworth's method does not require derivation. It identifies the architecture of an unknown system — the grammar, if you like, before the vocabulary. You begin with what repeats, what occupies fixed positions, what varies. You establish the structural skeleton and then fill in meaning by isolating units that behave like definitions, like your paired entries." She gestured at the page on her desk. "It is slow work. It is tedious. If there are fifty-seven pages, you are looking at —" She stopped herself again, and the caution came back into her face like a curtain drawn. "You are looking at a significant amount of time."

"I'm not in a hurry," Gavin said, which was not true, but he did not have a way to explain the urgency he felt about a manuscript he could not read.

Babbling held out the Wexworth. "When you have finished with it, put it back on my desk yourself. Not in the return pile. The return pile passes through too many hands."

He took it. "Thank you, Professor."

She nodded. Then she began straightening the quiz papers again, which was a dismissal. But as Gavin reached the door, she said: "Mr. Tench."

He turned.

"The notation on that page is genuinely unprecedented. In twenty-three years of teaching Ancient Runes, I have not seen anything like it." She looked at him over her reading glasses. "I hope you are being careful."

He was not being careful. He was being fifteen and obsessive, which was as far from careful as he knew how to get. But he said, "I am," and she accepted it because she needed to, and he left with the folded page in one pocket and the Wexworth in the other, and the door closed behind him on the last expert who might have helped him, standing at her desk with quiz papers she was no longer reading.

---

The Wexworth was ninety-two pages of dense, dry scholarship that assumed its reader had a working knowledge of at least three runic systems and a tolerance for long footnotes. Gavin had a shaky grasp of Elder Futhark and no tolerance for footnotes. He read it anyway, twice, in the common room over three nights while the fire burned low and the kitchen smells shifted from dinner to bread dough to the cold-grease emptiness of the very late hours.

The method was not elegant. Wexworth did not claim it was elegant. He called it "a set of procedures for establishing minimum structural correspondence between an unknown notation system and the concept of notation itself." In plain language: before you could read an unknown script, you had to prove it was a script — demonstrate that the marks on the surface were organized to carry meaning, not randomly arranged or purely decorative. Wexworth's procedures for establishing this were laborious and mechanical and, Gavin suspected, exactly what he needed.

He started with the page he had shown Babbling — the paired entries, the possible key. He catalogued every distinct symbol. Forty-three, which was within the range of a syllabary or an expanded alphabet but too many for a simple alphabetic system and too few for a logographic one. He tracked their positions. He counted frequencies. He noted which symbols appeared in fixed positions — always at the beginning of a cluster, always at the end, always adjacent to a particular other symbol — and which moved freely.

It was brutal, meticulous work. He did it in the margins of his Potions notes, on scraps of parchment, on the back of a Transfiguration exercise he had already failed. He worked in the common room mostly, because the common room was warm and the sub-collection was not, and because sitting in a room with other people — even if he was not talking to them — kept him tethered to something. He sat in the same armchair every night, the one nearest the fire with the cushion that listed left, and the manuscript lay open on his lap hidden inside his History of Magic textbook. The notation drifted and he watched the drift and mapped it and slowly, over days, the drift resolved into something with structure.

His Potions essay came back with a D. His Charms practical went badly enough that Professor Flitwick asked him to stay after class, looked at him for a long moment, and said he knew this was a difficult year for everyone. Gavin said thank you and left and did not think about it again until dinner, when he remembered he had not started his Herbology reading either.

Most nights produced nothing. He would sit with the Wexworth open and the manuscript on his lap and the two systems would refuse to talk to each other, and he would stare at the same cluster for an hour and go to bed at two in the morning with a headache and ink on his fingers and no progress. One night he spent three hours convinced he had identified a modifier, only to realize at midnight that what he was tracking was an artifact of the notation's movement — the symbol he had been counting appeared in different positions because it was drifting, not because it was performing different grammatical functions. He closed both books and sat there for a while, too frustrated to sleep and too tired to keep working, listening to the rain outside and the settling noises of the common room and the distant sound of someone's owl.

But the structural invariants emerged. Slowly. Grudgingly. The first one announced itself when a small curved mark kept surfacing between clusters no matter how the notation drifted. Other symbols slid against each other or changed their spacing; that one held its place like a nail in timber. Boundary marker, maybe a space, maybe a full stop. Then came the positional classifiers — hooks that stayed at the front of a cluster even when the rest of it rearranged itself, governing what followed the way a subject governs a verb. And the paired entries on the key page, at last, revealed their logic: the left column was a concept rendered in full notation, and the right column was the same concept rendered in a simplified form, stripped of qualifiers and context markers.

It was a primer. It was teaching him how to read it.

The first word he decoded with any confidence began as a repeating three-symbol knot that appeared on thirty-one of the fifty-seven pages. It tightened and loosened on the page without ever breaking apart, the outer marks leaning in around the middle one while the surrounding notation drifted. By position and frequency it was either a key concept or a grammatical function word. Through the primer's paired entries, he matched it to a right-column simplified form, and through the simplified form's internal structure — positional classifiers, root and modifier, a suffix that seemed to carry tense or aspect — he built a tentative reading. When he stared at the simplified form long enough, the relation between the three marks became almost physical. The outer two settled around the middle one and held. He noticed only afterward that his jaw had gone tight.

*Agreement.*

Not "agreement" as a contract or a document. Agreement as a state: two things in alignment. Two things that had consented, at some structural level, to operate together. The closest English he could manage was "agreement," but the word was wrong in every direction that mattered — too legal, too human, too small.

He wrote it on the corner of a piece of parchment in his own handwriting and stared at it for a while. Agreement. It did not look like much. It looked like a word.

He decoded more. One cluster per night if he was lucky, and most nights he was not lucky, and some nights the headaches got bad enough that the common room fire blurred into a single orange smear and he had to close the manuscript and sit with his eyes shut until his vision cleared. But the fragments accumulated. A second concept announced itself first as the agreement-cluster with a hooked mark riding its edge, there one moment and, after the notation drifted, still there in the same relation to the root. It felt less like a new word than like a hand turning an object to show another face. He tentatively read it as "negotiation" or "renegotiation" — the same root as "agreement" with a modifier that suggested change, or the possibility of change. The agreements were not fixed. They could be renegotiated. Whatever the manuscript was describing, it was describing a system where the fundamental relationships — the agreements that made something what it was — could be adjusted.

A third concept emerged, and then a fourth, and each one was stranger than the last. The third was something he could only render as "permission" — not in the social sense but in the structural one, the way physics permits certain configurations and forbids others. The fourth was harder. On the page it kept dropping one band of marks behind another: a shallow arrangement first, then the same arrangement seen underneath, as if the sheet had depth and he was looking through it. The word seemed to mean both "layer" and "depth" and "the thing beneath the thing," and it was this word that finally cracked the manuscript open for him.

By the third week, he had decoded enough to understand that the manuscript was describing magic. Not spells, not effects, not the magic he practiced with his wand in class and failed at with comfortable regularity. Something underneath. He looked back through the clusters he had marked and saw the same structure everywhere: intention set against material, then the joining mark, then the conditions that made the joining hold. The agreements the manuscript described were the relationships between intention and material that made magic possible in the first place. A spell worked because the substrate — the deep layer of agreements — permitted it. The spell was an action taken within a framework of rules, and the rules themselves were editable.

He sat with this for a long time one night, the common room empty, the fire down to embers, the Wexworth open on his knee and the manuscript showing him relationships he could almost but not quite follow. Outside the round windows, rain ran down the glass in the thin tracks of late October, and the grounds were dark, and the cold from the stone floor crept up through the soles of his shoes.

Magic as agreements that could be renegotiated.

He did not understand what this meant. He understood the words he had decoded, and the words pointed at something enormous, and the shape of it was just beyond the edge of what he could hold in his head. It was like trying to see the building from inside the wall — knowing the wall was part of something, feeling the weight of everything it was holding up, but not being able to step back far enough to see the structure.

He closed the manuscript. His fingers were stiff from the cold — the fire had been embers for an hour and the common room air had turned, the way it did after midnight when the kitchen ovens went cold and the earth-and-stone smell of the Hufflepuff basement reasserted itself. He flexed his hands and put the Wexworth and the manuscript back in his bag and sat for another minute, staring at the round window and the dark beyond it.

He went to bed. The decoded fragments sat behind his eyes, strange and partial and stubbornly suggestive, and he lay there for a long time not sleeping while the rain hit the windows and the common room settled into its late-night sounds — the fire clicking as it cooled, the pipes in the walls, someone's owl making a low sound from the dormitory above.

---

Duncan came back from Muggle Studies on a Thursday in late October with bruises on both forearms.

He was wearing his robes with the sleeves down, which was not how he usually wore them — Duncan ran warm, always had, and rolled his sleeves to the elbow as automatically as blinking. But the left sleeve had ridden up as he set his bag down at the dinner table, and Gavin saw the marks before Duncan pulled the fabric back into place. Purple and yellow, the colours of a bruise that was at least a day old, distributed in a pattern that was not from falling or bumping into something. Someone had gripped him there. Someone with fingers.

Duncan sat down. He pulled a plate toward him and put food on it in neat, measured amounts: potatoes first, then carrots, then a spoonful of stew. He chewed without looking at either of them. His face had gone blank in the way Gavin had first noticed on the train, careful enough that even his eyes seemed to stop short of meeting anyone else's.

Miren was across the table. She had seen too. Her eyes tracked the line where Duncan's sleeve met his wrist, and then she reached across the table and moved the bread basket six inches closer to him without saying anything.

"Thanks," Duncan said.

"There's rolls left. The ones with the seeds."

"Yeah."

He took a roll. He ate it. They sat there. The three of them at the Hufflepuff table, in the same seats they had occupied since first year — Miren across from the boys, Duncan on the inside, Gavin on the aisle — and the silence between them was not hostile but it was not comfortable either. It was the silence of people who knew something was happening and had agreed, without discussing it, to leave it in the silence.

The Great Hall was the same as it was every evening — the enchanted ceiling showing an overcast autumn sky, the low hum of conversation from the other tables, the clink of forks on plates. At the staff table, Alecto Carrow was eating something and not looking at the students, which was worse than when she did look, because it meant she had finished whatever she wanted to do today and was satisfied with it.

Gavin ate his dinner. He was thinking about positional classifiers — specifically about a recurring three-part structure in the manuscript's longer clusters that might be a verb form, a way of indicating action or change within the agreement framework. The modifier that suggested renegotiation appeared to be part of this verb structure, and if he could isolate the other components —

"How was your day?" Miren asked Duncan. Her voice was ordinary. The question was ordinary. Everything about the way she asked it was ordinary except the way her hands were folded on the table, very still.

"Fine," Duncan said. "Long."

"What did Carrow cover?"

"The usual. Why wizards are better." He said it flat, not bitter. The flatness was its own kind of statement. "We had to write essays about the natural superiority of magical blood. I wrote a good one. She gave me an E."

Miren's jaw tightened. Then she said, "You want pudding? I think it's treacle tart."

"Maybe in a bit."

They were managing something between them — Gavin could see the shape of it, the way Miren offered practical things instead of the emotional ones she wanted to offer, the way Duncan accepted the practical things and let the emotional ones stay unspoken. They had a rhythm for this that was new and practiced, and it had developed without Gavin while he sat at the same table counting symbol clusters in his head.

A first-year farther down the table knocked over a goblet and Miren flicked her wand to catch the pumpkin juice before it hit someone's homework. She was good at that — the quick, practical spell, the instinct to help before thinking about it. Gavin would have noticed the spill and reached for his wand and by then someone would have been mopping up with their napkin.

Duncan ate his roll. He did not take another one. His left hand stayed on the table with the fingers loosely curled, and when he reached for his goblet with his right hand, the motion was stiff in a way that suggested the bruises extended past his forearms.

Gavin thought about saying something. He thought about what he would say — *are you all right, what happened, can I help* — and each of these felt like a door he did not know how to walk through. Duncan did not want to be asked. That was obvious from the sleeves pulled down and the careful face and the way he had said "fine" and meant *do not pursue this.* Then his mind went back, automatically, to the verb structures and the renegotiation modifier and the shape the decoded fragments were making. He swallowed against the taste of gravy and could not get a word out.

"I'm going to the library," he said eventually. "After dinner."

"Okay," Miren said.

"You can come, if you want."

"I've got a thing." She did not specify what thing. She had been not-specifying things lately — meetings, errands, a network of activity that Gavin understood was related to the DA without ever being told directly. He did not ask. She did not offer.

Duncan said nothing. He kept breaking his roll into pieces on his plate, thumb pushing through the crust, forefinger and middle finger pinching each piece smaller before he let it drop. He had neat, square-tipped fingers. The nails were bitten short, which was new — they had not been bitten at the start of term. Before this year, Duncan's hands had been steady enough to build model ships in the summers, something he had mentioned once in third year with the offhand embarrassment of a boy who suspected his hobby was childish. Gavin had not thought about it since. He thought about it now, looking at the bitten nails and the bruised forearms and the careful, methodical destruction of a bread roll.

Gavin left the table and went to the library. He worked on the manuscript until Madam Pince chased him out at closing, then went back to the common room and worked until the fire died and the cold crept up from the stone floor and the last of the kitchen warmth bled out of the walls. He decoded another cluster that night: a concept that might have been "substrate" or "foundation" or "ground" — the thing the agreements were agreements about, the medium in which they existed. The agreements were not floating abstractions. They were embedded in something, and that something was what the manuscript was trying to teach him to perceive.

He closed the Wexworth and the manuscript and sat in the empty common room for a while. Through the round windows the grounds were invisible in the dark, but he could hear the rain, and somewhere past the rain the edge of the Forbidden Forest was a sound more felt than heard — the deep vegetable presence of old trees in weather.

Duncan's bruises were still behind his eyes. The purple and yellow on the pale skin of his forearms, finger-shaped. The careful way he had moved his right arm reaching for the goblet. The roll broken into eight careful pieces on his plate, then ten, then fragments.

Gavin did not go check on him. He did not find Miren to talk about it. He sat for a while longer and then went to bed, sliding the manuscript into his trunk the way he always did now — facedown, under the spare robes, where nobody would look and where the faint warmth of the material was absorbed by the fabric and became indistinguishable from the warmth of ordinary things. The decoded words stayed with him in the dark: *agreements, renegotiation, substrate.* They were starting to cohere into something — a shape, a system, a way of understanding magic that had nothing to do with wands or incantations or anything his professors could teach him. The paired entries still gave him somewhere to start. The later pages were getting denser, stranger in a way he could feel before he could name it.

Duncan's bruises kept breaking into it. The finger marks on the pale skin of his forearms. The stiff reach for the goblet. The roll reduced to crumbs. Gavin lay on his side and started again from the paired entries every time they came back.


# Chapter 5

Gavin sat on the floor of the innermost room with his back against the wall, the manuscript across his knees and his parchment notes spread around him in a loose semicircle. The stone was cold through his robes — it was always cold in the sub-collection, a deep mineral cold that the castle's heating charms didn't reach, or had never been asked to reach, as though the rock down here predated the agreements that kept the rest of Hogwarts warm. His wand was propped against the lowest shelf with a Lumos on it, throwing a hard white circle across his workspace. Beyond that circle the walls gave off their own faint glow, the self-lit stone that needed no torches and predated the castle's torch system by centuries, maybe longer. In the overlap between the two kinds of light his parchment looked yellowed, wrong-coloured, like he was working under two different suns.

He crossed out the same approximation for the fifth time, and it still wasn't any closer.

Half-eleven by his watch. He'd been here since dinner, which he'd eaten too quickly and couldn't remember. The walk back to Hufflepuff would take at least fifteen minutes if the seventh-floor corridors cooperated, longer if they shifted — they'd sent him on a twenty-five-minute detour three nights ago, routing him past the Astronomy corridor and back around through a stretch of hallway he'd never seen. Herbology first thing tomorrow. Professor Sprout had set them a practical on Fanged Geraniums and he hadn't revised.

None of which mattered because the passage he was trying to decode had changed character in a way he hadn't seen before, and he couldn't make himself leave it alone.

The notation in this section was denser than anything in the previous pages. The symbol clusters were packed tighter on the manuscript's warm, not-parchment surface, and their representational movement was faster — the shapes reconfiguring in overlapping patterns that seemed to require his attention to hold three relationships at once while he was still trying to sort out the first one. He'd spent weeks learning the pace of the notation, the way the symbols shifted in a kind of slow rotation that gave him time to run them through Babbling's framework, identify the structural invariants, and write down an approximation before they rearranged into their next configuration. This section didn't give him that time. The rotation was faster, the configurations more complex, and his notes were a mess of half-captured relationships that wouldn't resolve into anything he could put into English.

He put his quill down and rubbed his eyes with the heels of his hands. The ink on his fingers left a smudge on his eyelid. His lower back had gone stiff from sitting on stone and he shifted, trying to find a position that didn't hurt, and settled for a position that hurt differently.

The previous sections — the ones he'd decoded over the past three weeks, filling fourteen feet of parchment with notes that were mostly crossings-out — had been difficult but graspable. The central concept, the one he'd circled and double-underlined and still didn't fully trust himself to have gotten right, was magic as agreements. Not spells in the way he'd learned them in class — words, wand movements, intent directed through a focus. The manuscript described something underneath that. When a witch or wizard cast a charm, what happened was not just energy being shaped. It was an agreement being invoked. A relationship between the caster's intent and the thing being acted on, mediated by the rules that said what magic could do and how. The spell's words and wand movements were how a human accessed the agreement — like a key fitting a lock, he'd written in his notes, except the key wasn't the door but without the key you couldn't open it, and that analogy was wrong too but it was the closest he'd gotten.

He picked up his quill again and dipped it. The ink was cold. Everything in the sub-collection was cold except the manuscript itself, which held its faint warmth against the limestone air the way a living thing held body heat, though it wasn't alive. Probably wasn't alive. He'd stopped being sure about that.

The cluster he'd been stuck on — seven interlocking symbols in the upper-left corner of the page, their movement describing what Babbling's framework classified as a hierarchical subordination — seemed to be pointing below the agreements themselves, past the content the spells determined and down to their structure. Down to the thing that made agreements possible in the first place.

He'd written on his parchment: *What makes an agreement possible? What sits underneath?*

Below that: *The rules for the rules?*

Below that, crossed out: *Like a language that tells you what languages are.*

He tried the cluster again, following the symbols through their rotation more slowly this time, trying not to force them into English but to let the structural relationships register on their own terms the way Babbling had taught him. The invariant in this cluster was definitely hierarchical — one element governing the others, not through force but through definition. It was saying what the other elements *were*. Saying what counted as an agreement. What shape an agreement could take. What it was allowed to change and what lay outside its reach.

He sat with this for a while. The sub-collection was very quiet. No portraits down here, no ghosts, no pipes or settling stone. Just the sound of his own breathing and the faint scratch of his quill when he wrote, and underneath both, the silence of old rock.

If agreements were what spells rested on, then there had to be a layer beneath the agreements that decided what an agreement could be in the first place, what pieces it needed, what changes counted, and where the edge lay between valid and impossible.

The comparison that kept coming back was not a spellbook at all but the rules before a game started.

It wasn't the moves once people were playing. It was the part where somebody decided what counted as a board, what the pieces were allowed to do, what sort of move the game would even recognise. The thought sounded childish and he nearly binned it for that reason. But the symbols kept settling around it. The manuscript was pointing below charms and below the agreements charms invoked, toward the rules everything else leaned on.

And the manuscript — he was almost sure now, as sure as he could be when his certainty was built on approximations stacked on guesses stacked on Babbling's framework, which was itself a scaffold thrown over a gap — was saying those rules weren't simply there.

They had been laid down. They hadn't grown or drifted into place. Something — the Builders, the civilisation the earlier sections had hinted at without naming — had made the underlying terms and fixed them there, deliberately, the way house rules were fixed before the first piece moved. And set things could be reset.

He wrote on his parchment, in small careful letters because his hand had started to shake and he didn't want the words to be illegible later: *The First Art = the underlying rules. The rules can be altered.*

The term had appeared three times in previous sections, untranslated because it mapped to no concept he had language for. Now it mapped to this. The First Art was not magic. It was not a spell, not a technique, not a branch of magical study. It was the layer where the rules got written — the thing beneath spells, beneath technique, beneath what anybody at Hogwarts would call magic — and it could be rewritten.

He put down his quill. The nib left an ink spot on the stone floor next to his knee, a small dark circle on pale limestone. He stared at it for a while because it was easier to look at than the sentence he'd just written.

If those underlying rules could be changed — not the spells, not the agreements, but the foundational structure that made spells and agreements possible — then someone who understood them could go beneath the spells and the agreements to the architecture itself — and change what magic was allowed to do.

He sat with this in the cold room with the self-lit walls and the silence and the ink drying on his notes, and it was too big. The concept was too big for the room, too big for a fifteen-year-old sitting on a stone floor past midnight with Herbology in the morning. He couldn't hold it. He kept reaching for the edges of it and it kept sliding past his grasp, not because it was unclear but because his mind wasn't shaped right to contain it. He could see the outline but not the interior.

Then something else shifted, and this was worse.

He'd been staring at the manuscript's notation while he thought, not actively translating, just looking at the movement of the symbols on the page. And he noticed — the way you notice a change in the sound of rain, not suddenly but as a gradual awareness that the rhythm is different from what it was — that he could follow the movement more easily than he should have been able to.

The notation in this section was denser and faster than anything he'd worked on before. By rights he should have been stuck at the first cluster for the entire evening, cross-referencing through Babbling's framework symbol by symbol, barely extracting fragments. Instead he'd decoded the section's central concept in a few hours. Roughly, imperfectly, but decoded.

That wasn't practice. His skill had improved gradually over the past weeks, the way any skill improved — a little less fumbling each night, a slightly faster recognition of recurring patterns. This was different. He was perceiving the structural invariants more directly. Still imprecise, still slow — but the framework, which two weeks ago had been a necessary intermediary step between the notation and his comprehension, was becoming less necessary. He was starting to see the relationships without translating them first.

His fingers found the edge of the manuscript and pressed into it. The warm, not-parchment material gave slightly under his nails. The sub-collection's cold pressed back against the warmth from the other side, and he sat in the gap between the two temperatures and understood what had happened to him.

The manuscript was a teaching tool. That was what the earlier sections had been circling — or maybe they'd stated it directly and he hadn't had the perceptual apparatus to understand. The notation wasn't just a medium for storing information. It was designed to restructure the reader's cognition through the act of reading. Each section didn't just convey a concept. It built the perceptual framework required to grasp the next section. The reading itself was the teaching, and the teaching was not knowledge transfer.

It was alteration.

He looked down at his parchment, at the weeks of notes surrounding him on the floor — the early attempts, almost entirely wrong, the middle attempts, less wrong, the recent ones that were starting to approximate meaning — and he understood what the progression represented. The frustration of the early weeks, the hours of staring at notation that refused to become meaning, the feeling of banging his head against something that would not give — that wasn't failure. That was the process working. Each failed attempt was a pass through the manuscript's cognitive training sequence, building pathways in his mind that hadn't been there before, gradually reconfiguring how he processed magical information until the pathways were developed enough for meaning to begin arriving.

He'd thought he was learning a notation system. He had been being rebuilt.

The evidence was there, now that he knew what to look for. Two weeks ago, in Flitwick's Charms class, he'd noticed something during a demonstration — not the spell itself but something in the air around it. A kind of density, a texture, as if the space near the charm's effect was slightly thicker than the space around it. He'd thought it was a draught from the window. A week ago, crossing the courtyard after Potions, he'd caught himself watching a third-year practising a Shield Charm and for a fraction of a second the spell had looked — not looked, that wasn't the right word — he'd almost perceived something behind it. Beneath it. The way you sometimes almost caught a sound that turned out to be nothing. And three days ago, lying in bed in the dark after another late night, the dormitory had felt different. Everything was the same — the temperature, Duncan's breathing in the next bed, the familiar creak of the beds above — but the space itself had changed. As if the room had a quality he'd never noticed — a depth, a density, something underneath the ordinary dimensions that he couldn't name and couldn't quite bring into focus.

He'd attributed each of these to tiredness. Late nights, too many hours hunched over notation in cold rooms, the accumulating exhaustion of weeks of work done in stolen hours between classes and sleep. Of course the world looked a bit strange when you were running on five hours a night.

But it wasn't tiredness. It was his mind being reshaped. These were early signals of new perceptual pathways activating — messy, partial, unreliable, fragments of a mode of perception that hadn't been there two months ago and was now under construction whether he wanted it or not.

The manuscript had done this deliberately. Not to him specifically — it hadn't known who would pick it up. But whoever made it had designed it to restructure any mind that engaged with it for long enough, and Gavin had engaged with it for long enough, and now his mind was not the mind he had brought to the sub-collection floor six weeks ago.

He tried to think a thought the way he would have thought it a month ago. Something simple. The stone floor is cold. Herbology is early. The Lumos needs recasting. And the thoughts came — they were there, they were ordinary — but they arrived inside a mind that now contained architecture that hadn't been there before, and the thoughts sat differently inside it, like objects on a shelf that had been repositioned. The thoughts were the same. The space holding them was not.

The nausea arrived then, somewhere further back than his stomach, somewhere structural: a vertigo that had nothing to do with heights. The ground of his own cognition had been moved without his knowledge, and the shift would not reverse. He understood this without needing to reason through it — the same flat finality as knowing that a word, once learned, couldn't be unlearned. The pathways were built. The perception would continue developing whether or not he ever touched the manuscript again. The process had passed the point where it required the tool that started it.

He put the manuscript down on the stone floor beside him. The warm sheets settled against the cold limestone with a faint sound that was almost organic, a sigh of material against rock. His Lumos had dimmed to an ember without him noticing, and the room's own glow — the self-lit stone, older than torchlight, older than the castle — was the only light left. In that dim luminescence his hands looked like they belonged to someone else because the person looking at them had changed first.

He sat on the floor for a long time after that. The cold worked deeper into his legs and his lower back, and the silence of the sub-collection pressed against his ears like a sound of its own, and the manuscript lay beside him on the stone, warm and patient and finished with what it had done tonight. The ink spot on the floor near his knee had dried to a flat dark disc. His notes surrounded him like the evidence of a crime that had been committed against his own mind with his full cooperation.

He didn't know how long he sat there before he stood up. His left knee cracked when he straightened it. He collected his parchment notes, rolled them loosely, and slid the manuscript between them, the warm sheets pressing against his papers the way a hand might press against a pane of glass. He picked up his wand. The Lumos had gone out entirely and he cast it again — "Lumos" — and the hard white light leapt back and the self-lit walls faded to almost nothing and the room was just a room, small and cold and made of stone.

The corridors were cooperative on the way back, which meant the walk took only twelve minutes. He was grateful for the shortness because every minute of it felt exposed. Not physically — the castle was quiet, no Carrows, no Filch, just the torches guttering in their brackets and the portraits sleeping in their frames. But he kept catching himself looking at things differently. The torchlight on the walls. The stone underfoot. The way the dark gathered in stairwells. There was a quality to the castle's magic that he could almost perceive — a background pressure that wasn't sound and wasn't temperature but sat in some register between the two. It was faint. It came and went. Like the first stars coming out: he knew they were there before he could resolve them individually.

He spoke the password to the barrels and slipped into the Hufflepuff common room. Empty. The fire banked to embers, the air warm and earth-scented after the sub-collection's mineral cold. He crossed toward the dormitory entrance and paused.

The warming charms on the common room walls. He'd lived with them for four and a half years without thinking about them. Now he could feel the structure underneath the warmth, a deeper arrangement he hadn't had access to before tonight. He couldn't see it clearly. It was like trying to read a sign in heavy fog — he knew the sign was there, knew it said something, couldn't make out the words. But the knowledge that the sign existed was new.

He went to bed. He lay in the dark with his curtains drawn and listened to Duncan's slow, careful breathing in the next bed — the breathing of someone who had learned to sleep lightly, to take up as little space as possible even unconscious — and the common room's heat seeped through the dormitory walls and the manuscript's warmth pressed through the rolled parchment in his trunk and he did not sleep for a long time.

---

He came to breakfast late and sat at the Hufflepuff table in the first open seat, which put him across from Miren and two places down from a sixth-year named Euan Abercrombie who was explaining to anyone who'd listen that the Tutshill Tornados' keeper situation was a disgrace, a genuine disgrace, even though there was no Quidditch this year and no prospect of it. Euan had opinions about Quidditch the way weather had opinions about the ground — relentlessly, on schedule, regardless of context. He was wearing a Tornados pin on his robes that he'd been told three times to remove and had not removed.

Miren was across from Gavin with a letter unfolded beside her plate, reading it while her toast went cold at her elbow. Her plait was falling apart on the left side, the way it did when she'd overslept and braided it while still half-asleep, and she was frowning at the letter in the particular way she frowned at things her parents wrote — not worried, just annoyed in the specific manner of a fifteen-year-old whose parents insisted on being parental from a distance. She folded the letter into her robe pocket without finishing it and picked up her cold toast and bit into it with the resigned expression of someone who didn't have time to butter a new piece.

Gavin put food on his plate because not putting food on his plate would make Miren look up. Toast. A boiled egg he didn't crack. He picked up his fork and set it down again. His eyes felt gritty and the Great Hall's light — grey November morning through the high windows, the enchanted ceiling showing dense cloud that sat on the castle like a lid — was too bright for the amount of sleep he'd had, which was none.

The air in the Great Hall was different this morning.

He'd felt it walking in. Stronger than what he'd noticed in the common room last night. The Hall was the most heavily enchanted space in the castle — the ceiling, the warming charms, the perpetual candles, the house tables with their identity magic, the protections layered into the walls and floor by a thousand years of headmasters and headmistresses. All of it was doing something. Producing something. A kind of pressure, not physical exactly but present on his skin the way humidity was present, registering in some sense he couldn't name. The air was thick with structure he was only beginning to perceive, and every time his attention drifted from his untouched plate it drifted toward that pressure, that density, the hum of agreements running beneath everything.

He made himself look at his toast. His toast was toast. It sat on his plate being toast. That was fine.

A first-year was sitting at the far end of the Hufflepuff table, mostly hidden behind Euan's shoulder and the gestures he was making about the Tornados' defence formation. She was small — undersized even for eleven — and her school jumper was too big, the sleeves rolled up in thick cuffs at her wrists that kept unrolling past her hands. She had a round, serious face and a smear of porridge on her chin that she didn't know about. Her wand was under the table. Gavin could see the tip of it past the table's edge, angled upward at a teaspoon next to her porridge bowl, and she was pointing it at the spoon with the focused, lip-biting concentration of someone who had been practising this for two months and was only just getting it to work.

Her lips moved. He wouldn't have caught the words if he hadn't been watching — they were barely whispered, almost inaudible under Euan's ongoing analysis of keeper statistics. "Wingardium Leviosa."

The spoon wobbled. Lifted off the table by an inch. Held there, trembling, for about two seconds. Then it settled back down with a small clink against the bowl.

And Gavin saw it.

He saw the underlying agreement.

It came into focus the way meaning came into focus when you suddenly understood a sentence in a language you'd been studying — not word by word but all at once, the structure and the content arriving together in a single apprehension that was larger than any of its parts. Between the spoon and the table and the air and the girl's wand and the girl's intent, there was a relationship. An agreement. It had been there before she cast the charm — it was always there, the agreement that said this spoon has mass and this mass pulls it toward the table and the table is solid beneath it and the spoon will rest where it is placed. The simplest set of rules. The most ordinary arrangement of object and world.

And the charm — her whispered Wingardium Leviosa — was a temporary addition to that agreement. A rider. It said: for this duration, the spoon will also be subject to a force directed upward. The agreement accommodated the rider. It flexed — and he felt it flex, felt it the way you felt a chord change in music, not in your ears exactly but in the part of you that understood what sounds were doing to each other — and the spoon lifted inside that brief new permission.

The spoon lifted an inch off the table, trembling. The girl held it. The agreement held it. The structure beneath both held everything.

The girl let the spoon come down. The release travelled back through the wood into Gavin's wrists, a small slackening, the way a stretched string felt when someone let it go. She glanced around to make sure nobody had seen her practising, and when she was satisfied that nobody had — Euan was still talking, Miren was eating her cold toast, a pair of third-years were arguing about a Transfiguration essay — she adjusted her grip under the table and tried again. "Wingardium Leviosa." The spoon lifted again, and the second lift tugged at him in a new place. He felt it not in his eyes this time but along his teeth, a faint pressure like biting down on tinfoil. The change around the spoon ran outward through the table's edge, down the bench legs, into the floorstones beneath all of them. It wasn't visible, not as lines, more the way you could feel somebody shift their weight on the far end of a wooden bridge. The whole Hall answered the spoon's small rebellion against gravity.

The girl's spoon came down. She smiled to herself — a private, pleased smile, the smile of someone who had a skill that was new and still exciting — and went back to her porridge. Gavin sat very still while the rest of the Hall separated into layers. The ceiling charm pressed cool and low over his scalp. The wall-charm warmth came across the backs of his hands in steady bands instead of one general wash. The floating candles each pricked the air with a tiny hot point. Even the Hufflepuff table had its own feel under his wrists, a house-bound steadiness in the wood, as if belonging here was one more condition the room kept checking.

He didn't eat. He knew he should eat because his plate sat there untouched long enough for the butter to turn dull and for someone three seats down to ask him for the marmalade twice. The second time Gavin handed over the salt. Euan laughed without any real unkindness, and Miren looked up properly at last.

The first-year tried her spoon again. It rose, this time almost two inches, and the room gave a small inward jerk around it. Gavin felt it in the bench under him, a tiny transfer of strain, and in the skin between his shoulder blades where the Hall's larger enchantments pressed back into place around the lift. The girl's whole face opened with the quiet delight of a child discovering what she could do.

"Gavin."

He looked up. Miren had put her letter away and was watching him across the table. She had ink on her thumb from writing a reply and a crumb of toast at the corner of her mouth and the expression she used when she thought someone was being strange but hadn't decided yet what kind of strange.

"You all right? You haven't touched anything."

He looked at her. Her plait coming apart. The ink on her thumb. The crumb she didn't know about. He had taken long enough not to answer that her expression had already changed, concern flattening into something more watchful. She was sitting in a room held together by thousands of interlocking agreements, a room whose walls and ceiling and floor and air were governed by rules that had been set before the castle was built, and she could not see any of it. She never would. For a moment the distance between what he was perceiving and what she was perceiving sat heavier than the table between them.

"Did you see the spoon?" he said.

"What spoon?"

"Down there. The little one with the porridge. It went up and the room sort of —" He stopped. "Sorry. No. I didn't sleep."

"You look like you didn't sleep at all."

"Yeah." He picked up his fork and put it down. "Maybe."

She watched him for another second, her eyes steady in the way they got when she was deciding whether to push. Then she reached across the table and put her remaining toast on his plate. "Eat something. You've got Herbology in fifteen minutes and Sprout'll have you repotting Fanged Geraniums, so you'll want your fingers working."

He reached for the toast without looking. His knuckles clipped the egg instead. It spun off the plate, hit the bench, and burst on the flagstones by his shoe. Euan stopped talking long enough to glance over. Miren's hand stayed in the air between them for a second longer than it should have.

Gavin stared at the split shell and the yellow spreading into the grooves of the stone. Somewhere farther down the table the first-year whispered the charm again, and the Hall drew itself taut around the spoon. "I'm fine," he said, still looking at the floor.

No one answered. Miren drew her hand back slowly. When Gavin stood a moment later, leaving the egg where it was and the toast untouched, he could feel her still watching him as he walked toward the doors. The Hall's pressure followed him into the corridor.


# Chapter 6

He had the manuscript spread on his bed with the curtains drawn, the sheets of not-quite-parchment weighted at the corners with his Potions textbook, a sock, his wand, and a quill he wasn't using. Outside the dormitory windows the light was already gone. Late November in Scotland meant dark by half four, and the windowpanes had that black-mirror quality where you saw the room reflected back rather than the grounds.

His left hand was smudged with ink where he'd been making notes in the margin of his Ancient Runes textbook, which was the only notebook he had that Miren wouldn't borrow. He'd been stuck on the same three lines since Tuesday. The passage had been resisting him for three days.

Not the notation itself — he could read the notation now, or at least follow its structures the way someone who had studied a language for a term could follow a newspaper article, guessing at half the words and filling in the rest from context. The problem was that this section of the manuscript was describing relationships between agreements, and each relationship assumed he understood things he did not, and each of those assumed a framework he could not quite see, and the whole thing kept folding in on itself like a sentence that referenced its own grammar.

This section seemed to show two agreements overlapping, occupying the same conceptual space without canceling each other, and he could almost see how that related to the way a Levitation Charm rested on a substrate structure more complex than the charm itself — the way a word sat on top of a grammar the speaker never thought about. Almost. The almost was the problem. He kept reaching for it and it kept not being there.

The dormitory door opened. Ernie Macmillan stood in the gap, his prefect's badge slightly crooked, his face holding the particular expression of someone delivering news they already knew was bad.

"Everyone to the Great Hall. Now."

"What's—"

"Now, Tench. Everyone means everyone."

Gavin closed the manuscript inside his Runes textbook, slid both into his trunk under his spare jumpers, and shut the lid. He straightened his robes — he hadn't changed out of them, hadn't been to dinner — and followed Ernie into the common room, where students were already pulling on shoes and buttoning cloaks with the hurried, tight-shouldered movements of people who had learned that summonses were not optional.

The walk from the Hufflepuff common room to the Great Hall was short — two minutes, same level — and by the time Gavin reached the entrance corridor he could see it wasn't just Hufflepuff. Students poured out of stairwells from every direction. Ravenclaws coming down from their tower in a quiet column, Gryffindors in a tight group that moved like something trying not to scatter, Slytherins walking a bit faster and looking a bit less frightened, though not by much. A first-year near the entrance was crying without making noise, her face wet and her expression controlled in a way that no eleven-year-old should have known how to produce. A prefect — Gavin didn't see which house — had a hand on the girl's shoulder and was steering her forward with the practised efficiency of someone who had done this before.

The Great Hall had been cleared. The four house tables were gone, vanished or shunted against the walls, and the stone floor was bare and cold and enormous — the full length of the Hall laid open, hundreds of students filing in to stand shoulder to shoulder under the enchanted ceiling. The ceiling showed the November sky: low cloud, no stars, the kind of solid darkness that settled over the Highlands and stayed until morning. The candles floated in their usual constellations but the light they cast felt wrong without the tables beneath them, as if the room had been undressed.

Amycus Carrow stood at the far end, where the staff table normally sat. He was sweating. His robes were plain black with the sleeves pushed up to his elbows, and the skin of his forearms was pale and lightly freckled in a way that Gavin wished he hadn't noticed because it made Carrow look like a person standing in a kitchen, not a man with a wand pointed at five hundred children. His sister Alecto stood a pace behind him, arms folded across her chest, her chin slightly raised. She wore rings on three fingers — heavy, dark-stoned — and her hands looked like they'd been dressed for an occasion.

Carrow let the silence build. He was not sophisticated about it, just willing to wait while the sound drained from a room full of frightened students, and the waiting worked. Students stopped shuffling. Someone's shoe squeaked on stone and the sound was enormous. The first-year near the door stopped crying, or at least stopped audibly.

"Someone," Carrow said, and his voice carried without amplification, pitched to fill space the way someone who had been drilling others would pitch it, "thought they'd have a laugh."

He paused. Gavin was near the middle of the crowd, between a Ravenclaw boy a year younger than him — fourth-year, with ink on his fingers and the compressed, swallowing look of someone trying not to be sick — and a Hufflepuff girl he thought was named Chambers. The Ravenclaw was pressing his thumbs against his fingertips one at a time, methodically, some private counting sequence that kept his hands busy and his mind on something other than the front of the room.

"Dumbledore's Army, Still Recruiting." Carrow's lips pulled back from his teeth. The expression was not a smile, but it used the same muscles. "Painted on the wall outside this very Hall. Letters high as a man. Charm-resistant paint. Somebody put real thought into it."

He rolled his wand between his fingers, a slow rotation, and his eyes moved across the crowd the way you'd scan a shelf for a particular item you didn't expect to find.

"Now, I could ask who did it. Could drag you up here one by one and find out. But that'd take all night, and I've got better things to do with my evening." He looked at the crowd. "So here's what's going to happen instead. You're all going to learn a lesson. Every single one of you."

The lesson was the Cruciatus Curse.

He demonstrated first. He pointed at a boy near the front row — a fourth-year Gryffindor, red-haired, freckled, who flinched when Carrow's wand came up but did not step back because there was nowhere to step and the boy behind him was pressed against his shoulder blades. The curse hit and the Gryffindor went down.

The sound a person made under the Cruciatus was not a scream in any way that Gavin understood screaming. It was the sound the body made when every nerve fired simultaneously with a pain signal that had no physical source, no location, no injury to correspond to. The boy's back arched off the stone and his mouth was open and the sound that came out was high and airless and sustained, a single note that went on longer than Gavin would have thought a person could hold without breathing. His hands clawed at the floor, fingers scraping stone, and his heels drummed and his head rolled and there was no part of his body that was not trying to escape a thing that existed inside every part of his body.

And beneath it — under the sound, under the visible — Gavin saw something flicker. The same kind of layered structure he'd perceived beneath the first-year's Levitation Charm at breakfast weeks ago, except this one was larger and organized around a single function. It looked like a set of instructions for how nerves should fire and in what order and with what intensity. An agreement about pain, written in the space between the wand and the boy. As specific and arbitrary as a recipe.

Then Carrow lifted the curse and the boy lay on the stone gasping and twitching, his hands opening and closing, a dark stain spreading across the front of his trousers because the curse had taken everything from him, including that. A girl near the front stepped toward him and Carrow said "Leave him" without looking at her and she stopped.

"Right," Carrow said. "Pair up."

The silence in the Hall changed quality. It had been the silence of fear. Now it was the silence of people understanding what was about to be asked of them.

"You heard me. Every two. Face each other. You're going to practice."

Nobody moved. Alecto stepped forward and began walking through the crowd, pointing at pairs, grabbing shoulders and turning bodies into position. She moved with brisk physical competence, the rings on her fingers catching candlelight as she shoved a tall Ravenclaw girl into place opposite a Hufflepuff boy who looked like he was going to faint.

"When I say practice," Amycus said, "I don't mean wave your wand about and hope for the best. I mean cast the curse. On your partner. You want to know what it feels like to fight for the right side, this is where you start."

Gavin was paired with the Ravenclaw boy — the one with ink-stained fingers who had been counting with his thumbs. Up close, the boy had a narrow face and brown eyes that were trying very hard to be steady and were not quite managing it. A fourth-year. Gavin didn't know his name.

"I'm not going to," the boy said, very quietly, with a strange, careful politeness, as if he were declining a second helping of pudding.

"Yeah," Gavin said. "Me neither."

They stood facing each other with their wands out because Alecto was circling and not having your wand out earned a visit. Around them, students raised wands with shaking hands and pointed them at people they ate meals with. Nothing happened for most of them. The Cruciatus required genuine malicious intent — the desire to cause suffering, not the pretense of it — and most of the students in this room were incapable of producing that even when they were frightened enough to try. Wands moved and the air stayed still and the failure was a kind of mercy, though Carrow was not inclined to see it that way.

"Put your back into it!" He was walking through the pairs now, watching. "I want results. You — Goyle — that's pathetic. Again. Again."

A few managed something. A Slytherin boy hit his partner and the girl staggered sideways, clutched her arm, made a sound that was not quite a cry but close. Not the full curse — something diluted, a fraction of what Carrow had demonstrated — but enough to hurt. The Slytherin boy looked at his own wand as if it had done something he hadn't meant.

Across the Hall, Gavin could see Miren standing three rows forward, paired with a Gryffindor girl. Neither of them was casting. Duncan was a few pairs away from Miren, standing opposite a Hufflepuff sixth-year, and his face held the careful blankness Gavin had been seeing more of since October — the expression of a Muggle-born boy who had learned exactly what attracted attention and was trying with everything he had to produce the opposite. His wand was up. He was not casting. His partner was not casting at him. They stood there like two people in a queue, waiting for something to be over.

Gavin stood opposite the Ravenclaw and did not cast. He was not thinking about the boy. He was not thinking about the room. He was thinking about the third line of the passage he'd been working on — the one where two agreements seemed to occupy the same conceptual space, and whether the relationship between them was hierarchical or parallel, and what it would mean if agreements could nest inside each other the way clauses nested inside sentences, and—

"Not trying, are we?"

Gavin looked up. Carrow was three feet away. His face was damp, the lopsided set of his jaw making him look like he was permanently bracing against a wind from the left. His wand was at his side but not relaxed — held the way a man held a tool he was about to use.

"Sorry, I—"

"Sorry." Carrow repeated the word the way he'd repeat an insult, tasting it. "Sorry. That's nice. That's lovely. What's your name?"

"Tench."

"House?"

"Hufflepuff."

"Right. Tench, Hufflepuff." Carrow raised his wand. His lips were wet. "Let me show you what you're meant to be practicing."

The Cruciatus hit Gavin in the chest and everything that happened next was that his body stopped being his.

Every nerve fired. Not a signal of damage — there was no damage, nothing cut, nothing broken — but the full saturating experience of pain from everywhere simultaneously. His legs gave out because the signal to stand was overridden by the signal that everything was wrong. He hit the stone floor with his shoulder and then his head, and the impact registered as a dull, distant thing underneath the primary thing, which was the pain. It was not like being burned or cut or hit. It did not have an analogy. It was the body's entire capacity for suffering activated at once, every channel open, every nerve reporting the same impossible message, and his mind could not process it because there was nothing to process — no source to locate, no injury to protect, no muscle to brace. His back arched. His hands curled into fists and his fingernails cut into his palms and he could feel that too, the small specific pain underneath the impossible one. His mouth was open. He could hear himself making a sound that was high and continuous and did not belong to him.

The floor was cold under his cheek. That registered. Stone, and gritty, the grit pressing into his skin. His jaw was clenched so hard his teeth were grinding and he could feel one of his back molars shifting in its socket. There was a taste in his mouth — copper, and something chemical behind the copper — and he realized he had bitten through the inside of his cheek.

Then it stopped.

It stopped and his body did not stop. His muscles had been clenched against nothing for however long the nothing had lasted — three seconds, ten, a year — and they didn't know it was over. He lay on the floor of the Great Hall and shook. His jaw ached. His shoulder throbbed where he'd hit stone. His skin was still reporting at full volume, every sensation turned up past where the dial was supposed to go: the cold of the floor, the grit, the fabric of his robes twisted against his ribs, the air moving across the sweat on the back of his neck. Everything was too much and too present and his hands were opening and closing and his left hand was doing something he could not control — a fine, rapid tremor that pulsed through his fingers and up into his wrist as if a wire inside him had come loose and was vibrating.

He got up. Not because he was brave or defiant, not because getting up proved anything about who he was. He got up because the curse was over and lying on the stone floor of the Great Hall while Amycus Carrow looked down at him was worse than standing. His legs shook. He locked his knees and held his shaking left hand against his thigh where the tremor wasn't visible, or less visible, and he stood there and breathed.

"Better," Carrow said, and moved on.

Carrow continued through the room. He hit two more students — a Ravenclaw girl who screamed and a Gryffindor boy who didn't — and then stood at the front again and watched the pairs with his arms folded and his wand hanging loose in his right hand, patient now, the lesson delivered. The Ravenclaw boy opposite Gavin had gone very white. His counting had stopped. His ink-stained fingers were trembling too, though Carrow hadn't touched him.

It lasted another hour. Maybe longer. Time had lost its edges inside Gavin's body after the curse. Students continued to point wands at each other and mostly fail and occasionally succeed, and the occasional successes got quieter as the session went on, the gasps smaller, the staggering more controlled, because the students who could produce the curse were learning what it felt like to hurt someone they knew and the lesson was not the one Carrow intended. Gavin stood and breathed and pressed his left hand against his leg and the metallic taste did not leave his mouth.

When Carrow finally dismissed them — "Think about that next time one of you wants to play artist" — the students poured out of the Great Hall in a dense, quiet mass. Nobody pushed. Nobody spoke above a murmur. The red-haired Gryffindor boy, the first demonstration, was walking under his own power, his jaw rigid and the dark stain on his trousers visible to everyone who looked and seen by no one, because not seeing it was the kindness they could manage. The first-year from the entrance was being steered by the same prefect, her steps mechanical, her face the color of the corridor walls.

The Ravenclaw boy — Gavin's pair partner — passed him at the doors. He nodded at Gavin, a small, precise gesture, like he was closing a parenthesis on something they had shared without sharing it. Then he turned up the stairwell toward Ravenclaw Tower and Gavin went the other way.

His left fingers kept knocking against his robe seam the whole walk back.

---

The Hufflepuff common room was warm and smelled like boiled carrots from the kitchens next door. The fire was burning. Students came through the barrel entrance in ones and twos and filled the low-ceilinged space with the particular quiet of people who had been afraid together and were now safe and did not know what to do with the distance between the two.

A sixth-year girl was crying on the sofa by the fire — not the gasping kind, the slow, leaking kind that looked like it could go on for hours, the kind that came from a place below where crying was useful.
