Two friends sat on either side of her. One had an arm around her shoulders; the other had fetched a cup of tea from somewhere and was holding it near her hands without insisting she take it. A boy in Gavin's year — Collins, the one who played chess in the common room most evenings and always set up the board with the white queen on the wrong square — was sitting on the floor against the wall with his knees up and his wand beside him on the flagstones. He'd put it down and his body language said clearly that he did not want to pick it up again.

Miren came in with Duncan. She had her hand on his arm, not guiding him, just resting there, a point of contact. Duncan's face still held the careful nothing, but his hands were in his pockets, and Gavin could see through the fabric that they were clenched. They sat on the bench near the fire. Miren said something to Duncan that Gavin couldn't hear. Duncan shook his head once, a minimal movement, and Miren didn't push.

Gavin sat in the armchair by the window — the one slightly too far from the fire and slightly too close to the draft that came through the garden-level glass. He pressed his left hand flat against the chair arm and felt the buzz copy itself into the wood, a fine rattling that was visible if you looked and invisible if you didn't.

His jaw ached. His shoulder hurt where he'd hit the floor. His legs had that wrung-out feeling, muscles that had been clenched too hard for too long and were now loose in a way that didn't feel like relaxation but like something broken. The taste of copper sat on his tongue, underneath everything else, a residue. These were the facts of what had been done to him and he noted them the way he would note the symptoms of a flu: present, specific, not interesting.

What he was actually angry about was that it was half past eight and the evening was gone.

The crying sixth-year still hadn't touched her tea. Collins kept his wand on the floor by his shoe as if picking it up too soon might count as volunteering. Miren sat with Duncan and bent her head toward him, listening. Gavin, from across the room, kept doing sums about time: how long until lights-out, whether his hand would steady enough to hold a quill, whether he could prop the manuscript on his knees and work one-handed. That was probably a bad sign.

He wanted to go upstairs and get the manuscript from his trunk. He wanted the passage back in front of him while the shape of it was still warm in his head. Instead he stayed in the chair and watched Miren keep her hand on Duncan's arm and did not go over.

He sat. The fire cracked. The crying girl's friend finally persuaded her to take the tea. Collins leaned his head against the wall and closed his eyes. The draft from the window pressed cold against Gavin's left side and his left hand buzzed against the chair arm and he stared at the fire and thought about overlapping agreements.

---

Miren came to him an hour later.

She crossed the common room with a deliberateness that said she'd been building to this since they sat down — watching him from the bench, watching him sit alone by the draft and not go to anyone, not speak to anyone, not look at anyone for longer than it took to register they were there. She sat in the chair opposite his, the one with the lopsided cushion that listed to the right, and she looked at him and did not speak for long enough that the silence became the opening statement.

"How's your hand?" she asked.

"It's fine. Just a tremor. It'll stop."

"When?"

"I don't know. Probably soon." He moved his left hand to his lap and folded his right over it, which fooled nobody. "How's Duncan?"

"He went to bed." She watched Gavin's face. "He didn't get hit. Carrow went after the Gryffindors mostly, the ones who weren't trying hard enough. But Duncan stood there the whole time and didn't move and didn't speak and I think standing through it was worse for him than getting hit would've been. Because he knew Carrow was looking at the Muggle-borns specifically and deciding whether to make a point, and Duncan was waiting for that, for three hours, and it didn't come, and now he doesn't know if it'll come tomorrow instead."

"Yeah."

"Yeah." She repeated it, flat.

The fire was lower. Most of the students had gone to their dormitories. The crying girl had been led away by her friends. Collins was still on the floor against the wall, either asleep or pretending to be. The common room had that late-evening quality where the warmth turned stale and the firelight moved across the low ceiling in long, uneven strokes and the room felt like it was settling, sinking into itself.

"You weren't there," Miren said.

Gavin looked at her.

"In the Hall. You were standing there and you weren't there. I could see you, Gav. You were three rows behind me. And when Carrow was walking through the room you were somewhere else. Your eyes were open and you were looking at things and you weren't seeing any of it."

"I was—"

"Don't tell me you were fine. Don't tell me you were handling it differently. You were doing the thing you do now where you go inside your head and the rest of us stop being real." She said this without raising her voice. Her hands were in her lap, still. Her body was held in that contained way she had when she was most upset, as if motion would crack something open that she'd decided to keep shut. "I've been watching it for weeks. But tonight I watched you get Cruciated and get up and your face didn't — you weren't angry at Carrow. You were angry at something else. I could see it."

"I know," Gavin said.

"Do you?"

"I know it looks like— Mir, I know. I wasn't. Present." The word came out like something he'd reached for and found at arm's length. "I've been working on something and it's taken up— I can't stop thinking about it. Even when I should be thinking about other things. Even tonight."

"Working on something."

"The manuscript. The one I've been translating. I know you've noticed—"

"The library thing." Her voice got shorter. "The book you've been translating for weeks. The one you disappear to work on until one in the morning. The one you won't talk about."

"It's not that I won't talk about it. It's that I can't— when I try to explain what it actually—" He stopped. Started again. "It's not a book. Not really. The notation in it, it points at whatever spells rest on. No, that's not it. The joins underneath. The part that has to line up before any spell can happen."

"It sounds like a school project."

"It's not a school project."

"It sounds like a school project that you've decided is more important than—" She stopped herself. She did not gesture at the room, at the students who had gone to bed shaking, at the common room that smelled of cold carrots and fear and exhaustion. She didn't need to. The room did the work.

"I'm not saying it's more important."

"What are you saying, then?"

Gavin opened his mouth. The honest answer was too large and too strange and did not have words that would survive being spoken aloud in this room, in this moment. He had found a layer of reality beneath magic. The manuscript had changed his perception and he could see things now that he could not see before and the change could not be reversed and it frightened him and it also felt like the most real thing in his life, more real than the Carrows, more real than OWLs.

What came out was: "I'm saying what I've found matters. I think it matters more than I can explain right now."

Miren looked at him. Her eyes were steady and dark and the anger in them was the kind that came from caring, which was worse than the kind that came from contempt because it meant she was still paying attention.

"We got Cruciated tonight," she said. "All of us. Every student in this school stood in the Great Hall and got tortured by our teacher. Collins can't pick up his wand. There's a fourth-year Gryffindor walking back to his dormitory in pissed trousers because Carrow used him as a demonstration. Duncan's upstairs right now lying in bed wondering if tomorrow's his turn. And you're sitting here telling me your manuscript matters."

"That's not—"

"That's what you're telling me. That is exactly what you're telling me. Because you came back from that Hall and sat in that chair and didn't say one word to anyone and the only thing you're upset about is that it took three hours out of your evening."

She was right. He'd come back, sat by the window, and let Duncan go upstairs without asking him a single question. There wasn't much to say to that.

"Mir—"

"I sat with Duncan for an hour. You were right there. Ten feet away. You didn't come over. You didn't ask him if he was all right. You sat by the window and stared at the fire and I could see you — I could see you were working on something in your head. After that. After all of that."

"I know."

"You keep saying you know. Great. So knowing it, what are you going to do? Keep going? Keep disappearing to that library room until one in the morning while the rest of us actually deal with what's happening?"

"I can't explain it to you because the explanation doesn't work in—" He was doing the thing where he started sentences and abandoned them, reaching for formulations that kept not existing. "What I can see now, since reading it — the way magic fits together underneath, the way the pieces hold — it's not what anyone thinks it is. It's not just a different kind of spell, it's a completely different—" His left fingers were knocking against his thigh. He put his right hand over them, pressing them flat. "I know what it looks like."

"It looks like you've checked out."

The words landed in the space between them and sat there.

"I haven't checked out."

"Then come back in." She said it simply, like it was simple. "Come help with the first-years. Come sit with Duncan. Come do one thing that isn't about the manuscript for one evening. Just tonight. That's it."

"I will. I'll do that. Yeah."

But she had heard the hesitation before the agreement. The half-second where his attention had snagged on the manuscript in his trunk, the passage waiting for him, the problem that was more present to him than the problem in front of him. He saw her register it — her mouth tightening, her shoulders settling into something that wasn't anger anymore but was worse than anger. A kind of tired clarity. The look of someone who had asked a question they already knew the answer to and had gotten the answer they expected.

"Fine," she said. "Leave it."

She didn't slam anything. She didn't raise her voice. She stood and looked at him for a moment with an expression he couldn't read because it was composed of too many things — frustration and worry and disappointment and something underneath all of those that might have been grief, the early stages of it, the kind that came when you watched someone leave a room they were still physically inside. Then she turned and walked to the dormitory corridor and the common room absorbed her departure the way it absorbed everything this year — quietly, without comment, with the fire still burning and the draft still pressing through the garden-level glass.

Gavin sat in the chair. His left fingers kept giving small jumps against his knee. The copper taste had faded to a residue, barely there, the body's diminishing record of what had been done to it.

He thought about going after her. He thought about knocking on the dormitory door and trying again: not a spell, not really, more like the buried set of instructions every spell used whether anyone knew it or not. He got as far as the bit where the Cruciatus and a Levitation Charm seemed to run on the same hidden workings, and stopped.

He stayed where he was.

He sat until the fire burned low and Collins opened his eyes and looked at Gavin across the dim common room with an expression that was just exhaustion, just a boy who had been on the floor for too long, and then Collins picked up his wand — carefully, with two fingers, as if it might be hot — and stood and went to bed without speaking. Then Gavin was alone.

He went to his dormitory. He lay in bed. He did not get up and take the manuscript out of his trunk.

His left fingertips kept snagging on the blanket. The dormitory was dark. He could hear Duncan's breathing from the next bed over, too even, too controlled, the breathing of someone who was awake and managing it.

Gavin listened for a minute, then turned onto his side facing the foot of the bed where the trunk sat and started worrying at the passage again: the overlap, the nesting, the part he had nearly had before Ernie opened the door. If that meant Miren was right about him, it didn't make the passage let go.

He lay in the dark and did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 7

Every half hour Gavin had to stop reading and press his fingers against his thighs until the joints loosened.

The sub-collection was colder in December. The chill came out of the limestone itself, dry and mineral, nothing to do with the weather outside, and it made his left hand tremor harder — the same fine shake the mass detention had left behind, sharpened now until the manuscript's notation blurred when he held the pages up to the wall's glow.

He sat on the floor with his back against the shelves, the manuscript spread across his knees, his notebook open beside him on the stone. The passage had been defeating him for three days.

The notation was doing something he hadn't seen before. The earlier sections — the ones describing the First Art's principles, magic as agreements that could be renegotiated, the substrate beneath spells — had used symbols that moved in pairs and small groups, showing direct correspondences between concepts. This section's symbols moved in larger patterns. Slow, layered, interconnected. When he translated one cluster and wrote the meaning down, the act of comprehension seemed to shift the patterns around it, as though the page were adjusting to his understanding. He wasn't sure if the adjustment was in the manuscript or in his perception. The distinction had been getting less clear for weeks.

He'd been translating the section using Babbling's framework — structural invariants, repeating patterns, mapping known elements onto unknown positions — and it worked, but slowly. Each evening he got a few more sentences into English, or something near English. The translations sat in his notebook in his own bad handwriting, crossed out and rewritten and crossed out again.

The section was about origins. Not the First Art's principles, which the earlier passages had covered, but its source. Who — or what — had created the substrate that Gavin was learning to perceive.

He'd been translating the key concept as "practitioners," which he'd known was wrong even as he wrote it. The beings the manuscript described did not practise the First Art the way a wizard practised spells. The relationship was different at a level that English kept flattening.

He tried the passage again. The symbols rearranged themselves in their slow configurations as he read, and this time — on his fourth attempt at this particular cluster — something aligned. Not a dramatic shift. More like the sensation of a sentence snapping into meaning when you'd been reading it as nonsense for days. The structural invariants held. The correspondences checked.

He wrote in his notebook: *Their relationship to the substrate was not functional. It was —*

He stopped. The word he wanted sat somewhere between "constitutive" and "fundamental" and "identical," and none of those words were right.

He tried again: *The substrate was not something they used. It was something they were.*

It was still wrong. Too simple. The notation showed a relationship more nuanced than identity. The beings were not literally made of the substrate the way a table was made of wood. It was more like — and he knew the analogy would break, because analogies always broke here — it was more like the relationship between a fish and water, except the fish was also water, except the water was also the fish's thoughts and its social structures and its architecture and its history, except there was no moment where the fish had been separate from the water and then started using it. The separation didn't exist. Hadn't ever existed.

He wrote:

*Think of it as: magic was not their tool. Not their environment. Their medium of existence. As fundamental and unconscious as air is to humans, except air is something separate from the body that breathes it. For them, the separation did not exist.*

He looked at what he'd written. It was the best he could manage and it was still inadequate. The notation was patient. It had been designed by an intelligence that assumed the reader would eventually understand, and Gavin was not the reader it had been designed for.

He'd taken to calling them the Builders in his notes. The manuscript didn't name them. They were referred to with a notational structure that implied something closer to "those who made the medium specific" or "the ones who gave the substrate its current local configuration." Neither of those was a name. The manuscript had been written for an audience that already knew who they were, the way a Hogwarts textbook didn't bother explaining who Merlin was. Gavin was reading a text written for readers who no longer existed, in a room that those readers had probably built.

He worked through the next section. The notation described the Builders' activities — their works, their constructions — in terms that the Babbling framework could only partially map. They had made stable configurations of the substrate that persisted. What a wizard would call enchantments, though the word reduced something vast to something small. When a wizard enchanted an object, they applied a spell to a thing — a layer on a surface. The Builders worked with the substrate itself. They adjusted the underlying agreements about what a thing was and how it related to everything around it. A wizard enchanted a ceiling to look like the night sky. The Builders could have edited the rules that defined what a ceiling was, what "looking like" meant, what the night sky was permitted to be.

Gavin sat with this for a while. The wall's faint glow was steady — a luminescence he'd been reading by for months without much thought. It had a depth to it now that it hadn't had before his perception changed. He could sense the substrate structure underlying the glow, a faint architecture that produced the light — something stable and intricate and very old, set down long before the castle was built above it. He'd assumed it was some early enchantment, a Founders-era spell that had persisted through centuries of ambient magical reinforcement. It wasn't. It was a substrate configuration. One of the Builders' works. He was sitting inside their work, reading about them by their own light.

Everything Gavin had learned in five years of magical education — every spell, every potion, every transfiguration — operated in a thin top layer on something these beings had shaped the way he might doodle on parchment in Binns's class. The whole system that wizards used was a residue. Left-behind infrastructure. Nobody remembered who had built it, the way you didn't think about who had built the road when you walked on it.

His hand shook. He pressed it flat against the cold stone until the tremor passed, and then he kept reading.

---

The next section was shorter, and it took longer.

Gavin worked through it symbol by symbol, checking each translation against the structural invariants, writing possible meanings in his notebook and scratching most of them out. Outside the sub-collection — up through corridors and staircases, past the seventh-floor entrance that only sometimes connected to the same hallway — it was getting dark. In December the daylight was gone by four. Down here there was no way to know.

After two hours he had a sentence he was reasonably sure about:

*They are not here.*

Three words of English content for a full passage of notation, because "here" was doing work that English couldn't match. "Here," in the manuscript's terms, was not a location. It was a category of existence — a specific mode of being that included occupying physical space, existing on a planet, maintaining a particular relationship to the substrate in its current local form. The Builders were "not here" the way a dog whistle was absent from a room full of human ears: still sounding, just pitched past the range of the listener.

The manuscript was precise about one thing, and Gavin read it three times to make sure his framework was giving him the right reading.

They had not died.

Whatever had happened to the Builders, the manuscript treated them as continuous. The notation had separate structures for cessation — he'd seen them in earlier passages describing the collapse of specific substrate configurations — and none of those structures appeared here. The page handled the Builders the way it handled anything still in force: as an ongoing fact.

They had left.

He sat with the word. "Left" was wrong. Everything was wrong in his translations, but "left" was specifically wrong in a way that nagged at him. Leaving implied a direction — from somewhere to somewhere else. A departure through space, the kind of thing you could draw on a map. The notation showed something else: a hinge turning inside reality itself. Its representational movement — the slow shifting of symbols that depicted a *from* and a *to* — gave him a *from* he could understand. This planet. This substrate. This mode of existence where you had a body and occupied a position and knocked against physical things.

The *to* was there on the page. The notation could show it. The symbols moved through a configuration that represented a destination, or a state, or a new mode of being — Gavin could see the notation doing it, could watch the symbols arrange and rearrange in a pattern that meant *where they went*.

He couldn't follow it. The meaning was there and his mind slid off it the way his eyes slid off an optical illusion — perceiving the shape but unable to resolve it into sense. The symbols kept folding into a state with no distance in it, only changed conditions. The Builders had stepped clear of location altogether and become a different kind of thing, one the manuscript could hold steady on the page and Gavin's human cognition could not track.

They had relocated. He wrote the word in his notebook knowing it was inadequate. It still suggested trunks and routes and arrivals. What the page showed was more like one set of rules giving way to another while they passed through intact.

He put the manuscript page down and pressed both hands against the floor. Both were shaking — his left from the persistent damage, his right from something else. From holding a page that had rewritten what he thought the world was.

The sub-collection room was three metres by four. The shelves were cut into the limestone and most of them were empty. The ceiling was low. The light glowed steadily from the walls, old and stable and made by beings who no longer existed here in any sense that Gavin could track. The entire magical system that ran the castle above — every portrait that moved, every staircase that shifted, every self-lighting candle, every ghost that drifted through walls as a sustained agreement between death and the substrate's rules — was built on top of what they had left behind. Leftovers. The residue of a civilization that had decided to become something else, and had gone, and what remained was what remained, and nobody knew.

He picked the manuscript up again. There was still more.

---

The last section he translated that night was about what the Builders had done before leaving.

They had set wards.

That was Gavin's word, and it was as inadequate as everything else, but it was the closest thing in wizarding vocabulary. In class — in the version of Ancient Runes that was still being taught, the version that had nothing to do with the notation in his hands — wards were protective spells. You set them around a house, a room, a doorway. They could be keyed to specific people, configured for specific threats, maintained with periodic renewal. The concept was familiar. Every student knew what wards were.

What the Builders had set was not familiar.

The wards covered the planet.

Gavin worked through the passage twice. The notation was unambiguous. It wrapped the whole globe in one continuous structure, running over oceans and under continents alike. Planetary scale was not a grand undertaking to the Builders but an ordinary exercise — the equivalent, maybe, of a wizard warding their house before a holiday.

He sat on the cold floor and worked through the next part of the passage, and this was where the translation gave him the most trouble, because the concept underlying the wards' function was the hardest thing he'd tried to render into English.

The wards bent notice. They took Earth and slid it out of whatever counted as a valid target for attention. The planet still turned inside them — wizards and Muggles and flobberworms and all the ordinary mess of life carrying on — but any awareness coming from outside found nothing for its grip to catch.

He tried several translations of the key passage:

*They remove Earth from a category of detection.*

*They subtract this planet from the set of things that can be perceived.*

*They edit the rules of attention so that Earth is not the kind of thing attention can land on.*

The third was closest, and it was still wrong around the edges. The wards rewrote the rules of notice itself. Inside them, Earth kept all its ordinary traffic — weather, wars, schoolchildren trying to levitate feathers. Outside, attention passed over the planet as cleanly as a hand over polished stone, because Earth no longer fit the shape of a thing that could be seized by a look.

Like pulling a folder from a filing system and leaving the papers where they were. The loss was in the index.

Gavin closed his notebook. He looked at his hands, which had stopped shaking at some point during the last section, the way a headache sometimes vanished when your attention was somewhere else. He checked his watch. Quarter past nine. He'd been in the sub-collection for five hours. His knees had gone past aching and into a numb stiffness that he'd have to walk out, and his lower back felt welded into its position against the shelf.

He put the manuscript and the notebook into his bag and stood up. The standing wasn't graceful. He bent and straightened and bent again until his legs remembered how to hold him. He stretched, and the stretch was clumsy and ordinary and human, and his body was a body in a castle on a planet, and the planet was inside a structure set by beings who had used the substrate as a medium of existence before becoming something else and going somewhere he couldn't follow, and the structure was designed to make the planet unnoticeable, and the question he hadn't translated yet — because the section hadn't covered it, or because he hadn't reached the right passage — was: unnoticeable to what?

The wards existed. Wards warded against something. The manuscript had described what the wards did with careful precision and had not yet described what they protected against. Or maybe it had, somewhere in the pages he hadn't translated, in the sections he hadn't decoded, in the slow patient configurations of symbols that kept rearranging themselves for a reader who was not the reader they'd been designed for.

He walked out of the sub-collection through the sequence of stone rooms — three of them, connected by low archways, each emptier than the last. The limestone gave way to the castle's regular masonry at the seventh-floor corridor, and the torches here were burning at half-strength, which was standard after nine o'clock in December though nobody could say whether it was conservation or the Carrows tightening the atmosphere.

Gavin walked past sleeping portraits and the occasional suit of armour standing at attention in its alcove, and every one of these things — the spell-animated paintings, the enchanted metal, the torches' self-regulation — drew on a system the Builders had put down. He could feel it now, faintly, the way he'd been able to since the breakfast in the Great Hall when his perception had first shifted. The substrate ran underneath everything, quiet and continuous, and the castle's spells rode it the way ink rode paper. Portrait eyes tracked him because somebody had once taught paint a narrow trick. The armour held its stance on memory alone. Even the torches were only fire following an old instruction. And all of it drew on groundwork a vanished civilization had laid down the way you might lay paving stones — functional, durable, unremarkable to the people who had done it.

The whole castle was recent and local, threaded through something older and much larger, and the species that lived in it was using the residue of a departed civilization without knowing that the residue was residue.

The corridors were empty. He passed a window on the sixth floor and the glass was cold enough to feel from two feet away. Outside, the grounds were dark under December cloud cover, the distant edge of the Forbidden Forest a darker line against the sky. Snow sat on the mountains beyond the lake. The world looked the way it always looked at this hour, at this time of year — a Scottish castle in winter, nothing visible that suggested anything beyond what the eye reported — and it was wrapped, all of it, in a structure that made it invisible to things Gavin couldn't name yet, and the structure was the last act of beings who had become something else and gone.

He kept walking. The stairwell between the fifth and fourth floors was the coldest part of the route, always, because the window on the landing didn't close properly and the draught cut through robes and jumper alike. His left hand tremored in the cold. He tucked it under his arm and took the stairs carefully, because the steps here shifted position after midnight and it was nowhere near that yet, but a fall would still be a stupid way to end the evening.

---

The common room was warm. That was the first thing, always — the warmth of the Hufflepuff common room after the castle's corridors, the heat from the fire and the adjacent kitchens bleeding through the walls. It smelled like bread tonight, bread and something with onion. The barrel entrance closed behind him and the cold began to drain from his robes and his fingers and his feet.

The round room with its low ceiling and warm-toned walls and the plants growing in the window boxes — it looked the same as it had looked every night for five years. Earthy, enclosed, lit by candles and firelight that moved and flickered in ways the sub-collection's steady glow never did. Students were scattered in the usual places. Third-years working at the big table by the window where the plants grew in their indirect light. A loose group of younger students near the fire, quills out, textbooks open. Someone had left a Gobstones set half-played on the floor by the hearth, the stones sitting in a constellation that would make sense to whoever came back to finish the game.

Miren's chair was empty — she'd gone up already, or she was somewhere in the castle doing DA work that Gavin knew about in broad terms and didn't ask about in specific ones. They hadn't talked much since the argument. Not fighting, exactly. Just careful. Duncan's usual corner of the sofa was also empty. He'd been going to bed earlier and earlier as the term wore on, and Gavin hadn't found a way to ask about it that didn't feel like prying into something Duncan was deliberately managing by himself.

He dropped his bag by his usual chair and sat down. The fire found his face first, then his hands. His left hand tremored faintly against the armrest.

He should eat something. He'd missed dinner again.

"Excuse me?"

A first-year was standing next to his chair. She was small — conspicuously small, the kind of eleven-year-old who hadn't hit any growth spurt yet and barely reached Gavin's shoulder even when he was sitting. Her school robes were new but already looked slept in, the hem dragging on one side where a stitch had come loose. She was holding her Charms textbook against her chest with both arms, and in her right hand she gripped a quill she'd chewed until the feather end was a flat, damp mess.

"Sorry to — I mean, I was wondering if you could help me with — it's the Levitation Charm." She said the last part quickly, the way you said something embarrassing — get it out, get it done. "I can't get it to work."

"The — yeah, sure. Sit down."

She sat in the armchair across from him and opened her textbook to the Levitation Charm chapter. Her name was written on the inside cover in careful, rounded handwriting: *Ffion Pryce*. Under the name she'd drawn a small picture of a cat. The cat was terrible — enormous head, no whiskers, body like a sausage — but she'd coloured it in carefully with what looked like Muggle crayons, orange and white stripes, and had written *Mr Paws* underneath in tiny letters.

"So what happens when you try it?" Gavin said.

"Nothing. The feather just sits there. I've been trying since October and everyone else got it weeks ago and I just —" She stopped and chewed on the ruined quill end.

"Right. Can I see you try?"

She pulled a practice feather from her bag — white, crumpled from being carried around — and set it on the low table between their chairs. She held up her wand, which was short and pale and looked like willow.

"Wingardium Leviosa." Swish and flick.

The feather sat there.

"Okay," Gavin said. "The wrist movement looks right to me. I think. Let me —" He took out his own wand and tried to demonstrate the flick, and immediately realized he wasn't sure about the precise angle. He'd learned this spell four years ago and hadn't thought about the mechanics since. His own Levitation Charm worked — it just worked, the way tying a knot worked, without thinking about each step. "The flick might need to be sharper? Like more of a snap at the end. And — make sure the emphasis is on the right syllables. Win-*gar*-dium Levi-*o*-sa."

"That's what Professor Flitwick says."

"Yeah. So. Right. Do that."

She tried again. Nothing.

"Okay, um." Gavin rubbed the back of his neck. He was not good at this. Teaching required being able to break down something you did automatically into separate steps, and he couldn't find the steps. The spell worked when he cast it. He didn't know why it worked and didn't know why it wasn't working for her. "Try — this is going to sound thick, but try not thinking about the feather. Think about the space above the feather. Where you want it to be. Like, aim your — I don't know, your wanting? — at the empty air where you want the feather to end up."

Ffion looked at him with the expression of an eleven-year-old receiving advice she suspected was nonsense but was too polite to say so.

"I know it doesn't make sense," he said. "I'm not great at Charms, honestly. I just — that's what worked for me. Sharp flick, right emphasis, and sort of really clearly want the feather up and not down."

She adjusted her grip on the wand, chewed the quill, and set her jaw in a way that made her look briefly older.

"Wingardium Leviosa."

The feather twitched. It shifted on the table but didn't rise.

"That was something," Gavin said. "Again. Same thing but sharper on the flick."

"Wingardium Leviosa."

The feather rose. Slowly, wobbling, uncertain — but up. Three inches. Five. It hung there, shaking slightly in mid-air, held by a connection between intention and substrate that Ffion couldn't see and Gavin could. He could see the arrangement underlying the charm — the fit between the caster's intention and the substrate's current state that made "up" a valid direction for a feather to travel — but it did not look self-contained anymore. The charm sat in the room like a tap screwed into pipework hidden in the walls. Ffion's wand movement had caught on rules the Builders had laid down long before feathers or schoolbooks or first-years. The feather was not just going up. It was borrowing old buried machinery to do it.

"Do that again," Gavin said.

Ffion blinked at him. "Did I do it wrong?"

"No. Sorry. No. Just again."

She lifted her wand. "Wingardium Leviosa."

The feather rose two inches and dropped.

Gavin leaned forward before he meant to. The older structure had been there again — a brief clean hitch into something larger, there and gone before he could get a proper look at where the little classroom charm bit into the buried works.

"Again," he said.

Ffion didn't cast this time. She looked at him instead, the quill end paused halfway to her mouth. "Is something wrong with it?"

With it. As if the feather could be wrong, or the spell, or the room. Gavin opened his mouth and nearly told her that the whole planet had been edited out of being noticed and that the spell she was struggling through was riding on the leftovers. What came out was, "No. Nothing's wrong. I just thought I saw — no, sorry. It's fine. You had it."

She kept looking at him for a second, uncertain now in a way she had not been when she'd asked for help. Then she tried again. The feather rose faster this time, higher, steadier. She held it for close to twenty seconds before her focus broke and it drifted down.

"I did it."

"Yeah. You did."

"I actually —"

"Keep going. It'll get steadier."

She grinned, though there was still a faint crease between her eyebrows now, and immediately tried again. The feather rose. It hung. It held.

"Thank you. I've been — everyone else got it ages ago and I thought I was just..." She trailed off. The sentence didn't need finishing. "Thanks," she said, and gathered her things and went back to her table near the fire and put the feather down and cast the charm again. It rose. She made a small, private sound of satisfaction, a quick breath through her nose, and Gavin looked away because the moment belonged to her.

He sat in his chair and watched the fire. Someone had left a pair of socks drying on the hearth guard and they gave off the faintly sour smell of damp wool left too close to the heat. The common room was quiet in the way it got after ten — settling, soft, the scratch of quills and the murmur of people not wanting to go to bed yet because the fire was here and the dormitories were cold.

He pulled his notebook back out.

He turned to a clean page and wrote: *Earth is under wards.*

He stared at it. It looked stupid immediately. Like the opening line of a bad prophecy, missing only the dramatic capitals. He wrote underneath it: *Attention bent away.* Then: *Built by —*

He stopped. Builders was his word. Not here was his word. Left was his word. Every usable word on the page was wrong in a slightly different way, and the right ones were still sitting in the manuscript in a notation nobody else could read.

His left hand tremored once against the paper. He flattened it there until the shaking eased. The fire cracked and shifted. The bread smell drifted from the kitchens through the wall. At the table near the hearth, Ffion Pryce was working through her Levitation Charm again, the destroyed quill clamped between her teeth, Mr Paws the terrible crayon cat face-down on the table beside her textbook. The feather rose. It hung. It fell. She put it back and tried again.

Gavin looked at the two and a half lines in his notebook and shut it. There was no version of them he could say to Miren tomorrow that would not sound mad, and no version he could hand to Babbling that would come with proof attached. He didn't go back to the sub-collection. Not tonight. Tomorrow the manuscript would be there, in the same small cold room, in the same patient notation that kept rearranging itself for a reader it hadn't been designed for. The rest of the questions — what the wards protected against, why the Builders had thought a planet needed hiding, what existed outside the wards that made concealment worth the work of a civilization that could reshape reality as a medium of thought — would wait in the pages he hadn't reached, in the slow configurations of symbols that showed things his mind couldn't quite resolve, in the quiet of a room lit by a light that a vanished civilization had left on when they stepped through a door that wasn't a door into whatever they'd become.

He closed his eyes. The fire was warm. Somewhere in the common room, a feather rose and settled and rose again.


# Chapter 8

The notation in this section was denser than anything he'd encountered before. The patterns interlocked at scales his translational framework could barely hold — each decoded element revealing relationships to a dozen others, each of those connecting to a dozen more, until the structure he was building in his mind felt like trying to hold the shape of the entire castle at once when all you could manage was a single corridor.

He'd come back the following night and been at it for three hours. Or four. Time moved differently in the sub-collection — no windows, no change in the luminescent light from the walls, no sounds from the castle above. The stone rooms existed outside the rhythms of school life, and the longer he worked in them, the more that separation felt like a feature, not an accident. The sub-collection had not been designed for human scholars sitting on the floor with their legs going numb. If it had been designed at all, it had been designed for something that didn't need chairs.

The manuscript lay across his knees, its not-parchment surface faintly warm against his robes. Gavin shifted his weight and felt the cold of the flagstones through the thin fabric, sharp enough to remind him he had a body that was not especially comfortable. He'd brought a quill and scratch parchment for notes, but he hadn't written anything in over an hour. The notes he'd been taking were useless — approximations of approximations, English words circling concepts they couldn't hold.

The ward structure was the first thing the translation gave him. He'd known, from the section on the Builders, that wards existed — planetary-scale agreements set before the departure. But the earlier section had described them in passing, the way a description of a house might mention the foundation. This section was the foundation itself.

The wards were agreements, like everything in the First Art, but agreements at a scale that made the substrate beneath a Levitation Charm look like a scribble in the margin of a vast book. They interlocked across the planet's surface in patterns his mind could only hold in fragments — he could manage a local section the way you could manage a single floor of the castle, but the whole structure slipped away every time he tried to see it at once. The Builders had not constructed the wards the way wizards constructed spells. They had negotiated them into existence, convinced the substrate itself to register Earth in a particular way — or rather, to stop registering it. To agree that the planet was not present in a category of perception that the notation could represent but that Gavin's language could not name.

Not hidden. Not shielded. Unregistered. The distinction mattered in ways he could feel pressing at the edge of his understanding but could not quite articulate. A shield implied an attack it defended against. Registration implied something that perceived. The wards did not defend against perception — they edited the conditions under which perception of Earth was possible, so that nothing outside them could perceive the planet the way nothing perceives something that has never existed. The planet was still here. The wards made its being-here a local fact rather than a universal one.

He sat with that for a while. The stone was cold under his legs. His left hand was trembling — it did that now, the Cruciatus aftereffect that came and went, worse at night, worse when he was tired. He pressed it flat against the floor until the cold numbed the shaking, and continued.

The notation's representational movement changed.

The interlocking patterns that described the ward structure were still present, but they had thinned. Not uniformly — in specific areas, the relationships between agreements had weakened, the way fibres in a rope thin before they snap. The thinning was not recent. Gavin's translational framework could not map the notation's time-representation cleanly to years, but the scale was centuries. Many centuries. The wards had been designed by beings who maintained them as part of their existence, the way humans maintained breathing, and the beings had left, and nobody had breathed for the wards since.

He worked through the degradation section slowly. Each decoded passage made the picture worse. The thinning was not linear — it compounded. Areas where the wards had degraded became less capable of supporting adjacent areas, which degraded faster, which weakened the sections next to them. A cascade failure in slow motion. Not imminent by human time — still years or decades away from critical thinning — but already past the point where the original structure could recover on its own. The wards needed maintenance the way a cathedral needed maintenance, and there were no builders and no tools and no one who knew the building was there.

Except him.

He kept translating. The next section was where the notation broke down.

The Builders' notation system could hold substrate relationships with brutal precision — agreements, dependencies, the rules under spells. It could map any magical concept Gavin had seen. At the boundary pages it hit something it could not render. When Gavin tried to follow the boundary patterns into the still center of the page, his eyes lost focus so violently they watered. The faint sense of substrate he'd learned to feel under every magical thing simply stopped there, cut off cleanly enough to make his stomach drop.

The notation represented this by failing where it touched it.

Gavin had turned four pages of the manuscript before he understood what he was looking at. The notation on these pages was sparse — a few patterns at the margins that described the ward boundary, the innermost edge where the wards met what was beyond them. The centre of each page was not blank. The material was the same slightly warm, not-parchment surface throughout. But the notation had stopped moving. The representational shifts that characterised every other section of the manuscript — the constant, living rearrangement of patterns showing relationships between ideas — were absent here. Worse: when he let his gaze rest there for more than a second, the warm material under his thumb went cold and the room tipped half an inch to the left. He had to look away and swallow before it settled.

And at the edges of that stillness, Gavin could read what the Builders had managed to encode. It was not intent or hunger or anything that could notice Earth the way a mind noticed. It was simply the state of things where substrate had never taken hold. The wards were the coastline. Beyond them was sea.

At the weak points it bled through. The nearest patterns lost precision first. Clean relationships went cloudy, then smeared broad enough to make his molars ache when he tried to track them. Another of those brief, nauseating sways ran through the room. His quill slid off his knee and clicked against the stone.

The Earth-side notation failed in order. A line that had meant persistence went soft. The branch connected to it lost its anchors. A cluster of symbols he had already translated twice stopped resolving into meaning at all. He knew what he was looking at because the manuscript kept showing it to him: substrate rules becoming approximate, then unstable, then absent.

He tried once to keep looking. Pain drove straight in behind his eyes. For one second the room went dead: no wall-light, no old stone enchantments, no tiny dormant charge in his own wand. It all slammed back only when he jerked his head away.

Full failure was contact. Let the wards go and the substrate came apart on touch: exact, then ragged, then gone. The thought left a copper taste at the back of his tongue.

All of magical life — every spell, every enchantment, every magical creature, every ward and charm and curse that had ever been cast or ever would be — depended on the substrate. The substrate depended on the wards. The wards depended on maintenance by beings who had left and were not coming back.

Gavin put the manuscript pages down.

The room was very quiet. The luminescent walls gave off their steady, sourceless light. The air was dry and cold and tasted of limestone. Somewhere above him, a thousand students and a hundred staff members were sleeping or patrolling or plotting resistance against a dark wizard they believed was the worst thing in the world, and none of them knew that the world itself was a thin place held together by agreements that were coming apart.

He tried to take a breath and the breath did not work. It went in but it went in wrong, too fast, too shallow, and the next one was worse. His hands were on the stone floor and they were shaking — both of them now, not just the left — and the luminescent walls blurred at the edges of his vision, and he thought very clearly: I'm hyperventilating. And then he stopped thinking clearly and just breathed wrong for a while.

The stone floor was cold against his palms. Then against his knees, because at some point he'd tipped forward. The manuscript pages had slid off his lap and lay on the floor beside him, their surfaces still faintly warm, still shifting at the edges with representational motion that continued whether anyone was reading or not. When he blinked, the still center of the boundary page stamped itself on the backs of his eyes as a flat grey coin, and every time it appeared his chest clenched again.

He stayed on the floor until his breathing dragged itself back into rhythm. He had not calmed down. His body had simply refused to keep misfiring forever. The shaking eased from violent to a steady tremor that lived in his hands and in the muscles of his thighs. His face was wet. He had not known he was crying.

He pressed his cheek against the stone and felt the cold of it and stayed there for a while longer. The room was the same. The walls glowed. The air was dry. Nothing had changed except that he now knew what was going to happen, on a timescale too long for urgency and too short for comfort, and he was the only person who knew it, and he was fifteen and his best subject was Herbology and he got Acceptables in Ancient Runes.

After a while he sat up. He wiped his face on the sleeve of his robe. He gathered the manuscript pages carefully, because his hands were still shaking and the pages were the only copy of the only description of the only problem that mattered, and if he damaged them there was no one to consult and no replacement and no second chance.

He put them in the leather folder he used to carry them through the castle. He stood up. His knees ached from the stone. His left hand was trembling badly enough that he had to press it against his side to hold the folder steady.

He walked out of the sub-collection and through the shifting seventh-floor corridors and down six flights of stairs and through the barrel-entrance to the Hufflepuff common room and past the embers of the common room fire and into his dormitory and put the folder in his trunk and got into bed without undressing and did not sleep.

---

The next day he went to classes because not going to classes attracted attention, and attention under the Carrow regime meant questions, and questions meant answers he did not have. He sat through double Charms and Herbology and then Ancient Runes. He took notes in none of them. In Herbology he repotted a Flutterby bush and got soil under his fingernails and thought about the wards. In the corridors between classes, students moved around him the way water moves around a stone in a stream, and he let them.

Professor Babbling's classroom was on the third floor, small and lined with reference texts that predated the school's current crisis by centuries. The class that day had been comparative Futhark systems, and Babbling had taught it the way she taught everything this year — carefully, within the syllabus, without tangents, because tangents could lead to questions and questions could lead to the Carrows taking an interest in what was being taught in Ancient Runes.

The other students filed out after class. Gavin stayed in his seat.

Babbling was at her desk, sorting student work into two piles. She had a system — green-ink comments for content, red for notation errors — and her handwriting was small and precise even on the worst papers. She'd taken off her reading glasses and they hung on their chain against her cardigan, which was the same dark grey wool she seemed to wear every day, its cuffs ink-stained past rescue. There was a half-eaten ginger biscuit on a saucer by her elbow that she appeared to have forgotten about.

She looked up when she realised he hadn't left.

"Mr. Tench." She set down her quill. "Is there something I can help you with?"

He'd meant to start with the translational framework — to anchor the conversation in something she'd already helped him with, to build from the known to the unknown. Instead he said, "The wards round Earth are coming apart."

Babbling looked at him for a long moment. Then she said, "Close the door, please."

He closed the door. When he turned back, she had moved the student papers to one side and was watching him with an expression he couldn't quite read — academic interest and concern and something else, something more cautious.

"Sit down," she said. "And start from the beginning. Not from the conclusion."

So he did. He sat in the chair closest to her desk and told her about the section of the manuscript that described the ward structure — the planetary-scale agreements that made Earth unregistered, the interlocking patterns, the system the Builders had left in place when they departed. He told her about the degradation — the centuries-long thinning, the cascade failure, the absence of maintenance. He told her about the thing beyond the wards — not a creature, not anything with a mind, just the state outside the boundary pages where the notation went dead and the room tipped under him.

He self-corrected twice. Three times. He kept reaching for the right words and finding that they did not exist in English or in any runic vocabulary she'd taught him. He said "it's like" and then revised. He said "not exactly, more like" and then revised again. He watched her face for signs that the meaning was landing, and what he saw instead was the gradual settling of a professional judgment.

When he finished, Babbling was quiet for a while. She picked up the ginger biscuit, looked at it, put it back down on the saucer.

"Mr. Tench," she said. "I would like to ask you several questions, and I would like you to answer them honestly."

"Yeah. Of course."

"How many hours per week are you spending on this manuscript?"

He hesitated. "A lot."

"How many."

"Maybe... twenty. Thirty, some weeks."

She nodded, as if the number confirmed something. "And your other coursework? Your OWL preparation?"

"It's — I know it's slipping. But this is more —"

"I am not asking as a rebuke. I am asking because the information matters." She paused. "Are you sleeping?"

"Some."

"That is not an answer."

"Four hours. Sometimes five."

Babbling's hands were folded on the desk. The ink stains on her fingers were dark in the classroom's lamplight. "Mr. Tench. What you have described to me is a cosmological model. A fascinating one. The ward-structure concept, in particular, is unlike anything in the established runic literature, and I would be very interested to examine the relevant notation under different circumstances." She hesitated, and Gavin watched her revise something internally, pulling back from whatever she'd been about to say. "However. The interpretation you are placing on this model — that the wards are real, that they are currently degrading, that a condition beyond them poses an existential threat to magical life — that is an extraordinary claim."

"I saw what it does —"

"Let me finish." Her voice was kind but had the edge of precision that did not allow interruption. "Ancient manuscripts frequently contain cosmological speculation. The Sumerian cuneiform records describe catastrophes that never occurred. The Ogham prophecy tablets — which I spent seven years studying — predict the end of magical civilisation approximately once per century. Speculative eschatology is one of the most common genres in pre-medieval magical writing. It is entirely possible that the manuscript you are translating contains a real description of an ancient ward system and a speculative, theoretical model of its potential failure. These are not mutually exclusive."

"It's not speculative. The notation shows —"

"The notation shows what your translation renders." She said it gently. "I gave you a framework for approaching the cipher, and it is a good framework — I believe that — but every translational framework introduces interpretive latitude. I am not questioning your diligence. I am questioning whether a single student working alone with an imperfect translation tool can draw the conclusion you are drawing from notation that, by your own description, becomes representationally ambiguous precisely at the point where the most dramatic claims are made."

Gavin opened his mouth and closed it.

"I think," Babbling said, more softly, "that you have been working very hard, under conditions that would be stressful for anyone, and that the manuscript has become a place where some of that stress has collected." She reached for the teapot on the shelf behind her desk — it was always there, always warm, charmed to stay that way — and poured a cup without asking whether he wanted one. She slid it across the desk to him. "This has been a terrible year. The things that are happening in this school — the detentions, the atmosphere, the uncertainty — these produce anxiety that has to go somewhere. And sometimes it goes into the work that occupies us most deeply, and the work begins to feel more urgent than it is, because it is carrying weight that belongs elsewhere."

The tea was milky and too sweet. He drank it because she'd given it to him and because the warmth of the cup was the first pleasant thing he'd felt since the sub-collection floor.

"Could you look at the section yourself?" he asked. "The ward section. If I brought you the pages —"

"No." The word was quiet and final. "I cannot involve myself further with research that falls outside the approved syllabus. Not this year." She looked at him, and what was in her face was not unkindness but the particular sadness of a person choosing self-preservation over curiosity. "Do not bring those pages to me, Mr. Tench. It would put both of us at risk, and I cannot pretend otherwise."

"Yeah," he said. "I understand."

"I would recommend sleep, Mr. Tench. And some attention to your other subjects. Your OWL marks will matter, whatever the manuscript says about the rest." She picked up the ginger biscuit and ate it, finally, as if the conversation had used up whatever restraint had been keeping her from it. "Close the door behind you when you leave."

He closed the door behind him. The third-floor corridor was empty, afternoon light slanting through the tall windows, dust floating in it. He stood there for a moment with the taste of someone else's tea in his mouth and the understanding that the most qualified person he could reach thought he was projecting.

---

He found Miren in the common room after dinner. She was on the floor by the fire with a Muggle Studies textbook open in her lap — Alecto Carrow's version, the one that described Muggles as subhuman — annotating it in the margins with corrections. She'd been doing this since September, filling the margins with the actual facts she knew from her mother, as if quiet factual resistance in the margins of a propaganda textbook could change something. Gavin did not know if it could. But Miren did it anyway, every week, in small precise handwriting that looked nothing like her usual scrawl.

She had mud on her shoes from somewhere — the greenhouses, or the grounds. Her hair was in its usual plait, though strands had come loose around her face, and she was biting the end of her quill in a way that suggested the annotations were costing her something she would not admit to.

"Hey," she said when she saw him. Her voice was careful — the tone she'd used with him since the argument in November, warm but keeping a millimetre of distance. "You look awful."

"Can I talk to you about something?"

"You're already talking to me."

"I mean — something specific. Something I need you to hear."

She looked at him for a moment, reading something in his face. Then she closed the propaganda textbook and set it aside. She turned toward him, and he saw the shift — the way she went from casually present to fully focused, her whole body going still in the way that was more intense than most people's engagement. When Miren decided to listen, she listened with everything.

"OK," she said. "Talk."

He sat down on the floor next to her, cross-legged, close enough to the fire that he could feel it through his robes. The common room was mostly empty — a few first-years playing Gobstones in the corner, a seventh-year asleep on the sofa with a textbook over her face, one shoe off and one shoe on. Private enough.

He told her about the Builders first. The civilisation that had used magic as a medium of existence, not a tool. She'd heard fragments of this before, from the argument in November, but he hadn't been coherent then and she hadn't been willing to hear it the same way. This time he went slowly. He told her about the departure — not death, relocation, to somewhere the manuscript's notation could represent but that his translation could only approximate. He told her about the wards — the planetary-scale agreements that made Earth unregistered, invisible to something outside them that the Builders had understood and protected against.

Then he told her about the wards failing. The centuries of degradation without maintenance. The cascade failure in slow motion.

Then he told her about the thing outside.

He struggled here, the way he'd struggled in Babbling's office. The same words that had failed him there failed him again — not a creature, not a force, a condition, an environment — and he watched Miren's face for the moment when the scale of it landed. What he saw instead was attention, steady and fierce, tracking everything he said without giving him anything back.

When he finished, the fire had burned lower. The first-years had gone to bed. The seventh-year was still asleep on the sofa, her shoe still half off.

Miren was quiet for a while. She pulled a loose strand of hair behind her ear. Then she said, "So. The world's ending."

"Not — not ending. The wards are. If they fail completely, then... yeah. Magic stops working. Everything that depends on magic stops existing. But it's not —" He caught himself. "It's not soon. Decades, maybe longer. But it's happening, and nobody knows except —"

"Except you."

"Yeah."

The fire cast moving shadows across her face. The common room smelled of earth and warm wood and the faint undercurrent of whatever the kitchens had been cooking — boiled potatoes, something with cabbage. Everything around them was warm and specific and small.

"OK," she said. "Can I ask you something?"

"Yeah."

"What'd you get on the last Runes assessment?"

He stared at her. "What?"

"Your Ancient Runes mark. End-of-term assessment. What was it?"

"I — Acceptable. Why does —"

"And the one before that?"

"Acceptable. Maybe a Poor on the practical. Miren, what does that have to do with —"

"Just — hang on." She held up a hand, not to silence him but to hold the space for what she was putting together. "The manuscript. The notation. You said even Babbling couldn't read it without giving you a framework first. She said it was pre-Futhark, pre-everything."

"Yeah."

"And you've been translating it. You. On your own. With a framework she gave you for a completely different notation system."

"It's not — the framework was a starting point. I've built on it. The notation teaches you how to read it, that's part of how —"

"Gav." She said his name the way she said it when she wanted him to hear what she was actually saying, not what he was hoping she was saying. "You're middle of the class in Runes. That's just true. And now you're asking me to believe you've cracked something no one's cracked, with a method that wasn't built for it, and the proof is that months alone with this book have made you see exactly what the book says is there. I can't test that. Babbling can't test it. Nobody can unless they let the same thing happen to them. So what am I supposed to stand on here except you?"

She was not angry. That was the worst part. She was not dismissing him out of frustration or spite. She was looking at him with the careful, painful attention of someone trying to find a place to put her weight and finding none.

He opened his mouth and found nothing in it. From anywhere outside his own head, the manuscript was a closed loop: the change it caused was the only proof that the change was real.

"I can see the substrate," he said at last. "Underneath spells. Under the lights in here. It's there."

Miren's expression tightened, not with contempt but with fear. "I believe you think that. I'm not taking the piss. I do."

That hurt more than if she'd laughed. A log shifted and sent a spray of sparks across the hearthstones where they glowed for a moment and went out. The common room stayed stubbornly ordinary around them — the round walls, the plants in the windows catching only indirect light, the sleeping seventh-year, the Gobstones abandoned in the corner by the first-years.

"I don't know what to do," he said.

Miren's face changed. The careful analytical expression shifted into something softer, more worried, closer to the way she'd looked at him before November. She reached over and took his hand — the left one, the one that was trembling — and held it without saying anything.

"I think you need to stop," she said quietly. "Not forever. Just for a while. Sleep. Do some homework. Come to dinner. Be a person for a bit."

"I can't stop."

"You can. You're choosing not to."

"If what I'm reading is right —"

"Then making yourself ill tonight doesn't help," she said. She squeezed his hand and let go. "It just means the only person who thinks he can do anything about it is too wrecked to think straight."

He wanted to argue. Instead he looked at the dying fire, at the seventh-year asleep with her shoe half off, at Miren's textbook waiting where she'd set it down. The room had kept going while his whole world split open. That felt, suddenly, like an accusation.

"OK," he said.

"OK you'll stop?"

"OK I hear you."

She looked at him for a long time. The fire was dying, reduced to coals that pulsed with heat. The common room had gone dim.

"I'm worried about you," she said. "That's all. I'm not — I just want you to be all right."

"I know."

"Are you going to bed?"

"In a bit."

She stood up. She picked up the propaganda textbook and tucked it under her arm. At the foot of the stairs to the girls' dormitory, she turned back.

"If it's real," she said. "If the wards and the — all of it. If it's actually real. Then someone needs to fix them. And it can't just be you, Gav. You know that, right? You can't carry something like that alone."

"Yeah," he said. "I know."

She went upstairs. He sat by the dying fire for a long time. The coals ticked as they cooled. The common room settled into its nighttime quiet — the creak of old wood, the distant sound of the kitchens cleaning up, the low hum of the earth around the walls that he'd never noticed before this year.

Both of them had been kind. That made the loneliness worse. If they had laughed, or told him to stop being stupid, he could have gone upstairs angry. Instead he'd been handed tea and worry and a grip on his shaking hand, and none of it changed the fact that he was alone with it.

They were both wrong, too. He knew because the boundary pages had left a mark on him: when he let the memory of them back in, part of the common-room dark went flat and depthless, and the small everyday hum of magic in the room vanished there. His stomach dropped every time it happened. The outside did not think about him. It did not think at all. It was simply there.

He went to bed. In the dark he listened to his dormitory mates breathing in their sleep. The sound was ordinary enough to hurt. They had enough to be frightened of already without this on top of it. Each time he shut his eyes, the dead center of the page came back behind them. Not as an image. As a place his thoughts skidded off and could not cross. The skin along his forearms tightened with cold, though the dormitory was stuffy with sleeping bodies. Somewhere above and beyond, the wards still held. Thinner than yesterday. Thinner than the day before that. Nobody else in the room knew it.

Gavin lay on his back and stared at the underside of his bed canopy and went over it again because there was nothing else to do. Nobody was going to help him. He was not good enough to do it alone. It was still going to be him, because there was nobody else and the manuscript was the only thing he'd found that even hinted at what to do. After a while he closed his eyes. He did not sleep. Much later, without noticing when it happened, he did.


# Chapter 9

In the nights that followed, the exercise stayed simple. The manuscript described it in three lines of notation that Gavin had decoded weeks ago: perceive the agreement governing the candle's flame, hold the perception steady, and adjust. *Adjust* was the word he used because the manuscript's actual term sat somewhere between *renegotiate* and *edit* and *speak differently to* — three English words where the original used one symbol that contained all of them and none of them exactly. He had been trying the exercise every night for the past two weeks. The candle burned the way candles burned.

He sat on the stone floor of the sub-collection with his back against the wall, the manuscript open across his lap. The room was cold — December cold, the deep mineral kind that came up through the stone itself and settled into his legs and the base of his spine. The sub-collection was always colder than the rest of the castle, and this deep into winter the chill had a permanence to it, as if the limestone had made a final decision about temperature and would not be moved. The room's own light — faint, steady, a luminescence from the walls that had nothing to do with torches or spells — was enough to read by. But the exercise needed a target. Something small whose rules he could see.

He could see them. That was the part that made him want to put his head through the wall. The flame's flicker was built on agreements — heat and air and fuel and the specific relationship between combustion and the magical substrate that kept fire in a magical building burning by the rules wizards expected. He could see the edges of those agreements now, dim and structural, layered beneath the visible flame like a second architecture. When he let his focus settle past the light, the substrate showed itself as pressure, rules being applied, deep-water weight pressing against his ears in the dark.

He could not touch any of it.

He tried again. He narrowed his focus on the flame, let his perception drop below the heat and light into the structure underneath, and reached with something the manuscript called *intention made structural*. He had no better understanding of the phrase than the first time he'd read it. He reached for the agreement like a missing word, one he knew he knew, sitting just behind his tongue, almost there, almost —

The candle flame burned. The sub-collection walls glowed their ancient glow. The stone was cold against his back and cold under his legs.

Nothing happened.

He sat back and pressed the heels of his hands against his closed eyes until the pressure built colours. His left hand trembled — the Cruciatus tremor from November, the nerve damage that surfaced in the cold or when he was tired or when he tried the exercises. He pressed his palm flat against the stone floor until the trembling settled to something he could ignore.

The manuscript had been designed for someone else. Gavin filed this with his shoe size and his Charms grade: a fixed fact about himself that was not going to change because he wanted it to. The exercises assumed a mind that was already partway to understanding the substrate, that needed only structure and practice to complete a transition it was nearly ready for. Gavin's mind was not nearly ready. Gavin's mind was adequate at History of Magic and passable at Ancient Runes and mediocre at the rest. The manuscript was trying to teach something that required a capacity he did not have, and his response to this was to keep trying because the alternative was to stop, and if he stopped then nobody was doing this at all and the wards would go on thinning and nobody would notice until noticing was the same as dying.

After an hour the headache came on. Pressure built behind his eyes until the manuscript's symbols seemed to press back from the page. The headaches had started the previous week, the first time he'd held the substrate perception long enough for something behind his forehead to protest. The sensation was less like pain than like something being fitted too tightly, as if the inside of his head had been measured for a thing that was slightly too large for it.

He sat with his eyes shut until the pressure eased from impossible to bearable, then packed the manuscript into his bag. It was half past eleven. He walked back to Hufflepuff through corridors that were empty and dark and cold. His footsteps echoed in a way that made the castle feel twice its size. He passed the third-floor stretch where someone had scratched DUMBLEDORE'S ARMY into the stone and someone else — a Carrow, probably, or a student who had decided cooperation was safer than solidarity — had gouged through the letters with something heavy. Both sets of marks were still visible, the words and the violence done to them, and nobody had repaired the wall.

The barrel entrance to Hufflepuff was warm against his knuckles when he tapped the rhythm. Inside, the common room smelled of earth and warm wood and the faint yeast-and-honey scent of whatever the kitchen elves were baking below. The last coals of the fire gave the round room an orange glow that turned the copper pots on the shelf into small, warm suns.

He climbed the dormitory stairs and sat on his bed with his notebook and translated one more passage by wandlight — ward geometry, the relationship between the wards' physical structure and their substrate resonance — before his eyes gave out. He understood perhaps a third of it. He wrote guesses in the margins, crossed out two, wrote new guesses over the old ones, and fell asleep with his quill still on the pillow.

---

The days had a shape he could have drawn with his eyes closed. He woke at six in a dark dormitory, dressed in the cold — his tie knotted badly, his robes never quite straightened — and went to breakfast in a Great Hall that was emptier each week. Students had been leaving all term. Families were pulling children out. Muggle-borns disappeared into the registration apparatus and sometimes did not come back. Nobody counted the empty seats aloud. Counting meant knowing how many were gone, and knowing meant sitting with the number, and nobody wanted to sit with the number.

He ate toast. He drank pumpkin juice. Miren sat next to him and Duncan sat across, and the three of them occupied their section of the Hufflepuff table in waiting-room silence, close by default, their attention elsewhere. Duncan tore his bread into smaller pieces and lined half of them along the edge of his plate. He did this most mornings now, taking a portion, dividing it, eating the smaller division. The weight he had lost since September showed in his sagging robes and in the sharpness of his wrists where the bones stood out against the skin. He wore his collar buttoned to the top, even in the Great Hall's warmth, as if loosening it would expose something underneath that he was keeping covered.

"Did you finish the Herbology essay?" Miren said one morning. She was eating porridge and tearing bread into small, precise pieces, her hair in the tight plait she wore on days she had things to do after class. She had been running DA logistics — Gavin didn't know the details and she didn't offer them — and on those mornings the plait was a signal, a small, practical flag.

"No," Gavin said.

"It's due before lunch."

"I know."

"No, wait." She put two fingers on his wrist before he could reach for his satchel. "Don't do that thing where you say you know and then vanish."

"I wasn't vanishing."

His Charms notes were open beside his plate. At some point while she was talking he had picked up his quill and written three lines of manuscript notation in the margin where the date should have gone. Miren turned the page toward him.

"This," she said. "I'm talking to you and you're writing whatever that is."

He looked down at the symbols as if someone else had put them there. "Sorry. I'll do the essay at lunch."

"You said that yesterday about Transfiguration."

"That was different."

"How?"

He had no answer that worked outside his own head. The Herbology essay was about the medicinal properties of Fanged Geraniums and the Fanged Geraniums were not going to matter if the wards failed and the essay was not going to affect whether the wards failed. None of it mattered except the manuscript, and saying that out loud only made people look at him the way adults looked at a boy who had mistaken obsession for importance.

Duncan kept his eyes on the bread. "Sprout asked where you were," he said. "Yesterday."

Gavin looked at him. Duncan's voice had been so quiet the words seemed to arrive from somewhere behind the table. "I was in the library."

"No, you weren't," Miren said. She did not raise her voice. "I checked."

"I was there first. Then I moved."

"Fine. Then don't move today." She reached into the crook of his arm, pulled the Herbology textbook halfway out of his satchel, and thumped it onto the table between the toast rack and the pumpkin juice jug. "Lunch. Here. With the book open."

"I said I'd do it."

Miren held his gaze for another second, then let go. "Good."

The warning bell rang through the Hall. Benches scraped back. Owls swung through the rafters in a late flutter and somebody at the Gryffindor table dropped a goblet hard enough to make half the room look up. Gavin shoved the Herbology text back into his bag, feeling the weight of it beside the manuscript pages, and followed the Hufflepuffs out.

In Charms he lasted three lines. Flitwick was teaching nonverbal casting, a fifth-year OWL requirement, and Gavin couldn't get his Silencing Charm to work without the incantation.
