He had managed it last month. Now the spell kept sliding off the edge of his intention before it reached his wand, as if his mind had found a new track and the old one was closing behind it. When he looked down, his neat copied notes had already tilted back into the angular marks of the First Art's cipher. His wand work was deteriorating steadily, the decline so gradual he could only see it in hindsight, the coordination between intention and spell and wand movement losing its precision because his intention was learning to operate at a level where wands were irrelevant, and the transition was making him worse at the thing he'd been merely mediocre at before.

History of Magic was the easiest class to disappear in. Binns droned and the room dissolved into grey inattention and Gavin translated undisturbed for forty-five minutes, his notebook filling with notation and guesswork. The boy beside him — a Hufflepuff called Patterson, who spent every Binns lecture arranging his Chocolate Frog cards in rows on his desk, sorted by some system involving colour-coding and tiny pencil marks on the backs — glanced at Gavin's page once, saw the unfamiliar symbols, and went back to his cards without comment. Patterson had freckles and bitten nails and a way of mouthing the names on the cards as he sorted them, his lips forming a silent catalogue. The habit was no stranger than Gavin's own work, and neither of them remarked on what the other was doing.

Ancient Runes was the only class he still attended with both eyes. Not because Babbling's lectures connected to the manuscript — they were working through standard Futhark, exercises he could have done in half the time — but because the framework she had given him months ago was still producing results, and occasionally a concept from her lecture would land against a problem he'd been carrying and something would shift. The cipher's structural invariants, the patterns she had taught him to look for in unknown notation — these were the tools that let him grind forward a sentence at a time.

Babbling did not look at him differently this term. She did not mention the manuscript. She called on him in class, marked his essays with her usual terse comments, and treated him as a student who was adequate at Runes and nothing more. If she remembered the notation he had shown her in October — the notation she had recognised as unprecedented, pre-Futhark, possibly pre-anything — she did not let the memory show on her face or in her voice. Gavin respected this. He also felt, faintly, the specific loneliness of being forgotten by the one person who had seen what he had found and chosen to look away.

---

The Transfiguration mock OWL was on a Thursday, the last week before the Christmas break. McGonagall had announced it with the clipped efficiency of someone who disapproved of the timing but lacked the authority to change it. The mock exams had been compressed into the final two weeks of term — pushed forward, rushed, because nobody was certain, this year, that the OWL examiners would come at all in June.

Gavin revised the night before. He set the manuscript aside for two hours and practised Switching Spells at his desk — teacup to rat, cushion to quail, the standard exercises. The teacup went passably. The cushion was poor. He forgot to attempt the pincushion because he'd drifted into a passage about ward-substrate coupling and lost the time.

The examination room was the Transfiguration classroom with the desks pushed to the walls, students standing at individual stations with their targets lined up: a teacup, a cushion, a wooden box. McGonagall stood behind her desk with her marking parchment and her quill and the dark-green robes she wore every teaching day. The left cuff of her sleeve was frayed — a single thread hanging loose from the hem, visible when she moved her hand to write. It was the kind of small disrepair that McGonagall would not have tolerated in September, and its persistence into December said something about the year that a speech could not have said.

The Switching Spell was the second task. Gavin raised his wand, visualised the transformation — teacup to rat, standard OWL exercise, he had done this a dozen times in practice — spoke the incantation, and watched the teacup collapse into a lump of grey-brown clay.

It was not a rat. It was not a malformed rat or a rat with the wrong ears or a rat that had retained some ceramic quality. It was a lump of wet clay the size of his fist that sat on the desk and did nothing because it had never been alive and never would be. It was formless and slightly damp and it looked like something left behind by a process that had given up halfway through.

The Ravenclaw at the next station — a girl with ink-dark fingers and a quill tucked behind her ear that she seemed to have forgotten was there — glanced at his desk, saw the clay, and turned back to her own work with the careful speed of someone who did not want to be caught looking.

Gavin stared at the clay. He knew what had gone wrong. He had reached for the Switching Spell and his mind had gone to the substrate first — to the layer of agreements beneath transfiguration, the grammar that made the spell possible — and the two intentions had collided and produced neither. The clay was what happened when you tried to speak two languages at the same time and got nothing comprehensible in either. A collapsed meaning. A thing that was not a teacup and not a rat and not anything.

His wand felt wrong in his hand. Not physically wrong — same length, same weight, same wood — but the relationship between his intention and the wand's response had shifted, subtly, the way a familiar door handle felt different when you'd been turning it with the other hand. The wand worked by channelling intention through a specific grammar. Gavin's intention was learning a different grammar. The two were beginning to interfere with each other, and the interference produced clay.

He moved on to the third task — a Vanishing Spell on the wooden box — and managed to make it transparent rather than invisible. Half marks at best. He was fairly sure he didn't care.

After class, McGonagall asked him to stay. She looked at him over her spectacles — a look that in September had been her standard assessment of insufficient effort but that had softened, over the term, into something closer to concern. The particular concern of a teacher who had watched too many students come apart this year and had attributed each case to the obvious cause.

"Your practical work has deteriorated significantly since September," she said. McGonagall spoke in clean, measured bricks of language, each word placed precisely. She did not soften her sentences and she did not rush them. "I am aware that this year has placed extraordinary pressures on all of our students."

"Yes, Professor."

"If you would find it helpful to speak with Madam Pomfrey, or to arrange additional revision sessions —"

"I'm fine." He'd said it too fast. "Thank you. I'll — I'll revise more."

She looked at him for a moment. The frayed thread on her cuff trembled in the draught from the window. Then she nodded and turned back to her marking, and Gavin left.

The corridor outside was cold. Somewhere ahead of him he could hear Gryffindors talking about Christmas plans, their voices carrying the specific forward-leaning energy of people counting days until something good happened. Gavin walked toward Hufflepuff and the sound faded behind him.

He did not feel ashamed of the clay. That was the thing he noticed as he descended the stairs — the absence of shame where shame should have been. A year ago, failing a mock exam badly enough to be spoken to afterward would have sat in his stomach for days. Not because he cared deeply about Transfiguration but because he had always cared about adequacy, about sitting in the middle of every distribution, exceptional at nothing but terrible at nothing either. The clay should have bothered him. It didn't. It sat in his memory like a fact about the weather — real, registered, not actionable. His problems were elsewhere and larger and also not going well, and the total was that he was failing at everything: bad at the magic other people did, bad at the magic only he could see, bad at being present for the people who needed him present. He was spread across two kinds of failure and competent at neither.

He went to the sub-collection anyway. The candle burned. He stared through it until the pressure behind his eyes spread into his teeth. Nothing moved. He packed up and went to bed.

---

The Hufflepuff common room emptied early that December. Students went to bed when they could because sleep was the one place the Carrows couldn't reach, and by half past ten most nights the round room held nothing but the fire and the potted plants and the quiet clatter of the kitchens through the south wall.

Gavin stayed up. He worked at the round table near the hearth, the manuscript pages spread beside his notebook, and the common room's warmth was better for translation than the sub-collection's stone cold. He could sit here with his candle and the fire's last coals and work until one or two in the morning and the only cost was tiredness, and tiredness was a thing he could carry.

Duncan stayed up too. Not every night, but often enough that Gavin had stopped tracking when it started. Duncan would appear in the armchair by the hearth after the others had gone, a textbook on his knee, and sit there. The textbook was always open. Duncan's hands were always still on the pages. The pages did not turn.

Tonight was one of those nights. Past midnight, the fire burned down to embers that ticked as they cooled. The room was lit by Gavin's candle and the last orange glow from the hearth, and the shadows in the corners were deep and close. Through the south wall, the kitchen sounds continued — a house-elf's footsteps, the faint clink of something being stacked, the rhythms of work that went on regardless of the hour.

Duncan was wearing the green jumper. It was a Muggle thing, knitted, the colour of moss on a north-facing wall. The left elbow had a patch in a slightly different green where it had worn through and been mended, the stitches visible if you looked, uneven in a way that said *hand-sewn by someone who cared more about warmth than neatness.* He wore it every evening when his robes came off. It was too big for him now. The sleeves covered his hands entirely, the cuffs bunched around his fingers, and the body of it hung loosely on his frame in a way that remembered a shape Duncan no longer filled.

Gavin was working through a passage about substrate maintenance — the same passage he had been fighting for three nights. The notation was recursive, referring to itself, folding inward like a sentence that contained its own grammar. He was writing his fourth attempt at the same clause when Duncan spoke.

"Do you think about food much?"

Gavin looked up. Duncan was watching the embers, or the space just above them, the thin column of heat rising from the coals.

"What?"

"The food here. It's fine. It just always tastes —" He moved his hand, a small searching gesture. "Copied. Like someone ate the real thing, wrote down what it was like, and the elves followed the notes exactly. But it's still the notes. You know?"

Gavin put his quill down. "I've never really thought about it."

"No, it's — forget it. Stupid thing to say."

"It's not stupid."

Duncan looked at him briefly, then back at the fire. "My mum does this chicken thing. She puts it in a big tin with lemon and potatoes and slides it in the oven and the fat runs down off the chicken onto the potatoes and the bottoms go crispy and the tops go soft from the juice. She doesn't weigh anything. She doesn't time it. She just puts it in and checks it when she feels like it." He was pulling at the cuff of his jumper, rolling the fabric between his thumb and forefinger, a small repeated motion. "My dad sets the table wrong every Sunday. Every single time. Forks on the wrong side, spoons where the knives go. He thinks it's hilarious. Mum shouts at him. He does it again the next week."

A coal fell through the grate. The soft sound of it — a whisper of ash, a small settling — was the loudest thing in the room.

"Then she puts the tin right in the middle of the table and everyone just eats out of it because no one can be bothered doing extra washing up."

Gavin sat with this. The kitchen was in the room now — the table with the wrong knives, the tin, the steam, the shouting over forks that meant everything was still ordinary enough to shout about forks. A Sunday in a house where magic belonged to school and chicken belonged to home, and the distance between the two had once been a simple train journey.

"That sounds good," Gavin said. It came out flat and obvious, the kind of thing you said when someone had offered you something real and you were holding it with the wrong hands.

"Yeah." Duncan closed his textbook. He did not mark the page. He had not been reading it. "I was going to go home for Christmas. The form came back approved — all stamped, everything official." He said *official* carefully, as if the word no longer meant what it used to. "But I've been thinking about whether —"

He stopped there. The rest of the sentence stayed inside him.

"Whether what?"

Duncan looked at him. His face had thinned since September in specific, countable ways — the jaw sharper, the skin beneath his eyes darker, the softness gone from his cheeks. He had the face from photographs of hard years: sharpened jaw, shadowed eyes, every softness gone. What remained belonged to someone who had been fifteen for a very long time.

"Whether it's safe. For them. Whether going home means someone starts watching the house. Whether they're already watching and me going back makes it worse." His hands were in the jumper's pockets, hidden. "Whether I'd come back. If I went."

"You should talk to Miren," Gavin said. "She keeps track of this stuff — who's gone home, who's come back. She'd know if —"

"I know. I know she would." Duncan's voice was steady. Steadiness was the thing he had chosen when all the other options had closed. "I'm not asking you to fix it. I'm just." He looked at the fire. The embers were dimming, the orange fading toward grey. "I miss the chicken."

Something shaped like a laugh came out of him, hollow at the centre, a reflex his body made because what it was replacing was worse.

Gavin looked at Duncan's hands, buried in the green wool. He knew about the shaking. He had seen it in November, after a Muggle Studies lesson that Duncan had come back from walking stiffly, his face the particular blank of someone who was holding everything behind his expression. Gavin had seen the tremor in Duncan's fingers when he'd reached for a glass at dinner. Duncan had seen Gavin see it. Neither of them had said anything. They were both carrying the same year in different parts of their bodies — Gavin's left hand, Duncan's hidden hands — and the damage was different in origin and in weight but identical in what it meant: that this year was leaving marks that would not come off when the year ended.

He should say something more. Ask what had happened in Muggle Studies. Ask why Duncan kept his hands in his pockets even here, by the fire. Ask whether he wanted Miren fetched now, not tomorrow, not when it would be easier to pretend this conversation had not happened. The questions came up one by one and each one felt wrong before it reached his mouth, too late or too obvious or too much like pretending he had only just noticed.

Duncan waited without looking at him. Crockery clicked through the wall. Gavin put two fingers on the edge of his notebook and took them off again. By the time he found words, they were the wrong ones.

"Duncan," he said.

Duncan waited.

"The chicken thing. With the potatoes — the crispy on the bottom. That really does sound good."

Duncan's mouth shifted at the corners, there and gone before it settled into anything.

"Yeah," Duncan said. "It is."

He stood. He tucked the Potions textbook under his arm — the textbook he hadn't read, whose pages hadn't turned, which he carried the way you carried something that had once meant you were a student. His socks were visible below the hem of his robes as he climbed the stairs — Muggle socks, thin stripes of red and blue, the kind that came in plastic packets from shops that sold everything for a pound. The green jumper hung loose across his shoulders.

Gavin was alone in the common room.

The fire had died to ash. Through the wall, very faintly, the kitchen sounds went on — crockery, footsteps, the distant steady rhythms of preparation for Christmas morning still hours away. The potted plants on the windowsill were dark shapes in the candlelight, their leaves curled toward the indirect light they wouldn't get until dawn.

He looked at his notebook. The passage he had been translating was still there in his cramped handwriting, four attempts at the same clause stacked on top of each other:

*The structure requires maintenance at intervals incompatible with the lifespans of its current inheritors.*

He had written it differently each time and each time it had meant the same thing. The wards were designed for beings who lived longer than humans. The maintenance cycle assumed a continuity of attention that did not exist on Earth. The gap between what was needed and what was available — in lifespan, in capacity, in the sheer cognitive architecture required to do the work — was the same gap Gavin sat inside every night when he tried the exercise and produced nothing.

He picked up his quill. He dipped it. He read the next line of notation and began the slow, wrong, necessary work of turning it into something his mind could hold.

Outside, snow fell on the Hogwarts grounds, and the castle was quiet, and the wards — invisible, immense, older than the school and thinner than they had been yesterday and thinner than they would be tomorrow — held.


# Chapter 10

A few hours later Gavin woke to the sound of pipes gurgling in the walls. The kitchens beneath the Hufflepuff dormitories ran early on Christmas morning, and the warm-water pipes that cut through the stone behind his headboard ticked and knocked as they heated. It was the only sound. The other four beds in the room were empty except for Malcolm Preece's, and Malcolm was still asleep, his blankets pulled up to his eyebrows with one foot sticking out at the bottom, bare and pale.

There was snow against the window. Not the charmed interior snow that sometimes drifted through the common room, but real snow piled against the ground-level glass, dimming the light to a blue-grey wash. The fire had gone out overnight. Gavin's breath showed when he sat up.

At the foot of his bed, three things. His mother's parcel, wrapped in brown paper and tied with the same string she used to bundle newspapers for recycling, her handwriting neat and small on the tag. A package from Miren, soft, wrapped in pages torn from the Daily Prophet — she'd used the employment section, which was the only part that didn't carry war news these days. And a card from his aunt in the cream-coloured envelope she always used.

He opened his mother's parcel in bed, sitting cross-legged with the blankets over his shoulders. A jumper — hand-knitted, dark blue, too large. She always knitted them too large because she was convinced he was growing faster than he was. A tin of fudge with a note that said *Merry Christmas love Mum and Dad, the cat knocked over the tree again, Dad fixed it with a Sticking Charm so now we can't move it until June, we love you.* The fudge smelled like toffee and butter and the kitchen at home, and he ate a piece before he was fully awake. It was perfect. His mother's fudge was the only thing in his life that was consistently, reliably perfect.

He put on the jumper. It hung past his wrists, and the neck was wide enough that it kept slipping off one shoulder, but it was warm and it smelled like the wool basket in the sitting room and he was not going to take it off today.

The common room, when he came down, was almost cosy. Someone — Sprout probably, or one of the house-elves who had always liked the Hufflepuffs best — had strung fairy lights around the barrel entrance and along the mantelpiece. They blinked in a pattern that was almost regular but not quite, one strand amber and the other white, and a few of them had given up entirely and just glowed a steady dim orange. A garland of holly ran along the window ledges, pinned into place wherever it would hold. The room smelled sharp and green under the warm kitchen reek. Someone had spent part of Christmas morning on this instead of sleeping late, and that sat in the room as plainly as the fire.

The fire was built up high, logs as thick as Gavin's arm, and the heat of it reached the armchairs by the round windows. A small tree stood in the corner, its branches heavy with conjured ornaments — some of them moving, little gold birds that flapped and resettled and flapped again, shaking glitter down onto the tree skirt — and beneath it a handful of parcels that hadn't been collected yet.

Miren was already there, cross-legged on the rug in front of the fire, still in her pyjamas with a jumper pulled over. She had a mug of tea resting on her knee and was reading a letter that Gavin knew from the handwriting was from her mother — the same round, careful script he'd seen on the packages Miren's mum sent every month, always wrapped in brown paper and smelling faintly of shea butter. Miren's plait was coming undone on one side, loose strands curling near her ear. She looked up when he came down the stairs.

"Happy Christmas," she said.

"Happy Christmas."

"Your mum do the jumper?"

He looked down at himself. "Yeah."

"It's enormous."

"She thinks I'm growing."

"Are you?"

"Not really."

Miren set the letter aside and reached behind her for a package. "Here. From the box my mum sent."

The package was light. He sat down on the rug across from her and unwrapped it — more Daily Prophet, the Quidditch scores this time. Inside was a paperback, well-thumbed, the cover creased and cracked at the spine. *The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy* by Douglas Adams. The pages were yellowed and soft at the corners, and someone — Miren's mother, probably — had dog-eared several of them and underlined a passage near the back in pencil.

"It's Muggle," Miren said, watching his face. "My mum's favourite. She's read it about a hundred times. She said if you're going to be weird and antisocial you might as well be weird and antisocial with a good book."

"Your mum said that?"

"More or less." She took a sip of her tea. "It's about a bloke whose planet gets demolished to build a bypass."

"A what?"

"A bypass. Like a road. A Muggle road. Just read it."

He turned it over in his hands. The back cover had a coffee ring on it and a price sticker from a shop called Blackwell's, half peeled away. He ran his thumb along the worn spine and the book fell open to one of the dog-eared pages. "Tell your mum thanks."

"Tell her yourself, I'll give you her address." Miren pulled her knees up. "She worries about you. She's never met you and she worries about you. I don't know how she does it."

"I've got yours," he said. "Hang on."

He went back upstairs and came down with his gift for her, wrapped in parchment because he'd forgotten to buy wrapping paper and then the Hogsmeade weekends had been cancelled and he'd never found another source. The shape was obvious — a lumpy rectangle — and when she unwrapped it, she held up a hand warmer made from two pieces of felt sewn together with large, uneven stitches, filled with something that shifted inside when she tilted it.

"It heats up," he said. "You tap it with your wand."

She tapped it. Nothing happened for about four seconds, and then the left side went suddenly, aggressively hot — hot enough that she shifted it to her other hand with a sharp intake of breath — while the right side stayed cool.

"It does that," he said. "The charm's a bit — I couldn't get it to distribute evenly. It's meant to warm up all at once but the felt's thicker on one side and the heating charm sort of pools there."

"It's very warm on this side."

"Yeah. That side works."

She turned it over and pressed it between both palms. The heat spread slowly, not quite reaching the edges but radiating through the centre in uneven waves. A faint smell of singed wool rose from it and then faded. "I like it," she said. She kept holding it while the stiffness went out of her fingers, and Gavin could feel his face getting hot because she was going to use it and he hadn't meant it to be something anyone had to make allowances for.

"I'll fix it," he said. "After Christmas. The felt needs to be the same thickness all the way through, I think that's the problem."

"Don't. My room's freezing." She tucked it into the pocket of her pyjama bottoms and left her hand there, wrapped around it.

Duncan came down fifteen minutes later, moving with the careful deliberation he'd had since October, one hand on the banister, each step considered. He was wearing a jumper that didn't fit him anymore — too loose across the shoulders where he'd lost weight, the cuffs hanging past his knuckles — and he had the particular expression Gavin had come to recognise: a kind of watchful stillness, as though he'd forgotten it was a day when nothing bad was scheduled to happen and his body hadn't caught up yet. He carried a small cloth bag and his wand was in his other hand, though he put it in his pocket when he saw them.

"Happy Christmas," Miren said.

"Yeah. Happy Christmas." He sat down on the sofa nearest the fire and pulled his feet up underneath him. His socks were mismatched — one grey, one dark green — and he tucked his hands into his sleeves in the way he'd started doing when he didn't want people to see them shake.

They exchanged gifts. Duncan gave Miren a set of Gobstones he'd found in the bottom of a common room drawer and cleaned up — he'd polished them until they caught the firelight, amber and green, and they were better than anything available in the castle this year. He gave Gavin a bar of Honeydukes chocolate that he said he'd been saving since September, which might have been true. The wrapper was faded and the chocolate had bloomed slightly, a pale film across the surface, but it was Honeydukes and it was real.

"Thanks, Dunc."

Duncan nodded. He was looking at the fire.

Miren gave Duncan the scarf she'd been knitting for weeks — Gavin had watched it grow in the common room, Miren's needles clicking while she argued about DA logistics or complained about Alecto Carrow's propaganda assignments. It was purple and thick, long enough to go twice around Duncan's neck.

"It's lovely," Duncan said, wrapping it around his neck.

"Good," Miren said. "You looked frozen all week."

"I was frozen all week."

He wound the scarf up over the lower half of his face, leaving only his eyes showing. "Better."

His hands, Gavin noticed, were steadier today. Or maybe it was just that the scarf gave them something to hold.

They sat for a while. The fire popped and settled. The fairy lights blinked their not-quite pattern. The sounds from the kitchen below shifted from preparation to production — a low clatter of pots, the rush of steam through the pipes, the faint warm smell of roasting meat rising through the stones. Gavin opened his aunt's card, which contained a long account of her garden and a wish for a happy term, and he set it on the arm of the sofa and did not think about how distant her world sounded from his.

---

The Great Hall at midday was a strange sight. The four house tables stretched the length of the room as they always did, built to seat a few hundred students each, but the people present would have fit comfortably around two. Thirty, maybe, scattered across the benches in no particular house order because there was no point keeping it. A Gryffindor fourth-year sat between two Hufflepuff girls. A cluster of Slytherins had colonised one end of the Ravenclaw table because it was closest to the fire. A pair of first-years from Gavin's own house sat at the Gryffindor table and nobody said anything about it.

The enchanted ceiling showed white sky and snow falling in thick, slow flakes. The snow met the actual warmth of the hall and dissolved into nothing about six feet above their heads, so there was a visible line where winter stopped and the room began.

Sprout was there, and Flitwick, and Slughorn, who had brought his own wine and was already pink-cheeked by the time the food appeared. McGonagall sat with her back very straight, wearing a tartan scarf over her robes, and she had placed a cracker at every seat, which must have taken her half the morning given how far apart the students were sitting. Snape was not present. The Carrows were not present. Their absence was the day's first and best gift.

The food appeared the way it always did — platters of turkey and roast potatoes and sprouts and bread sauce, tureens of gravy, bowls of cranberry jelly that wobbled when you reached past them — and for a few minutes the only sounds were serving spoons and the particular industrious quiet of people filling their plates. The food was good. The food was always good at Hogwarts, even this year, even under occupation, because the house-elves cooked the way they'd always cooked and nobody had thought to interfere with them.

Duncan took seconds of the potatoes before he'd finished his firsts, spooning them onto the edge of his plate with his head slightly down, not looking to see if anyone noticed. Gavin noticed. Miren noticed. Neither of them said anything. There was something in the way Duncan ate — deliberately, with concentration, taking more because there was more and nobody was going to stop him — that was not about hunger. It was about the fact that this was a table where he was allowed to eat as much as he wanted without calculating what it would cost him.

Crackers. Gavin pulled one with Miren and lost — the snap sent a burst of purple smoke across the table and left him holding the short end while Miren got a paper crown and a small hand mirror that shouted compliments when you looked into it. "Lovely eyebrows!" it shrieked, and she turned it facedown on the table.

"Yours are nice too," Gavin said.

"Shut up."

Duncan's cracker produced a joke that he read aloud in a flat voice: "Why did the wizard bring a ladder to the pub? Because he heard the drinks were on the house."

"That's the worst one I've ever heard," Miren said.

"Last year's were worse," said the sixth-year across from them — Keir Dunbar, who Gavin knew mostly from Quidditch tryouts that neither of them had made and from the common room, where Keir could usually be found playing chess against himself and cracking his knuckles one at a time, methodically, left thumb to right pinkie. He was doing it now, working through his left hand between bites of turkey. He had a red paper crown on and it sat crooked over his dark hair. "Last year I got one that said 'What do you call a sleeping Kneazle? A catnap.' And the prize was a thimble."

"A thimble?"

"A thimble. I've still got it. Use it for nothing." He reached for the gravy. "At least nobody's here to ruin it this year."

"Keir," Miren said, quietly.

"I'm just saying." He poured gravy over his turkey. "It's nice, isn't it? Just eating. Without — you know."

Nobody answered that directly. Flitwick, at the staff table, was trying to charm a cracker into singing a carol and not quite managing it — the cracker produced a series of strangled squeaks and then went silent. The Gryffindor fourth-year laughed at something one of the Hufflepuff girls said. At the far end of the hall, Slughorn poured himself another glass and said something to McGonagall that made her almost smile.

Then Keir leaned forward and put on a face. Gavin recognised it before the first word came out — the jaw pushed forward, the eyes narrowed to mean little slits, the shoulders hunched up as if cruelty were a physical posture you climbed into.

"Well?" Keir said, in a voice that was Amycus Carrow's voice — the thick nasal rasp of it, the vowels flattened and shoved through his teeth. "What was I just teaching you, then? Nothing? Useless lot." He jabbed a finger across the table. "You there. On your feet."

He was pointing at Duncan.

Duncan's fork stopped. Just for a fraction of a second — his shoulders drew in and his face became the blank, careful surface he wore in the corridors now, the one that meant he had gone somewhere behind his own eyes to wait — and then it passed and he was smiling, the measured smile he used for everything, and Keir saw it and the impression died.

The silence at the table was not the silence of awkwardness. It was the silence of people who had been reminded of something they were trying, for one day, to set down.

"Sorry," Keir said. His voice was his own again. He was looking at his plate and his paper crown had slipped down over one ear. "That was — sorry. I didn't mean to —"

"It's fine," Duncan said. "It was a good impression."

"It wasn't, though."

"No," Duncan said. "It was."

The table was quiet for a long moment. Miren put her hand on Duncan's shoulder — briefly, lightly, the way you'd check whether a surface was hot. Duncan didn't move. The cracker joke lay on the table between his plate and Gavin's, and nobody picked it up.

Then Flitwick's cracker finally sang. The sound was thin and wobbly, a tuneless rendition of "God Rest Ye Merry Hippogriffs" that came out of the cracker in a reedy falsetto and died halfway through the second verse, and one of the first-years at the Gryffindor table clapped, and the moment passed. Not gone — moments like that didn't go — but it moved sideways into something they could sit with. Gavin ate his sprouts. They were overcooked and tasted like every Christmas he'd ever had here, and they were perfect.

---

After the meal they went back to the common room and played Exploding Snap until a card spat sparks into Miren's fringe and she accused Duncan of shuffling maliciously. Duncan said he wasn't nearly coordinated enough for malicious shuffling, and Miren said that sounded exactly like something a malicious shuffler would say, and they argued about it with the low-stakes pleasure of people who did not care about the answer. Gavin's left hand trembled when he held the cards — the fine, intermittent tremor that had lived there since November, coming and going without pattern — and he steadied it against his knee when it was his turn and didn't think about why.

They played Gobstones with Duncan's cleaned-up set until the stones squirted something that smelled powerfully of boiled cabbage, and then they stopped playing Gobstones. They tried the hand warmer again and it worked a little faster this time. Miren held it against the inside of one wrist and then the other, and when the left side started getting too hot Duncan told her to swap hands before she scorched herself again.

Miren read bits of the Hitchhiker's Guide aloud to prove that Muggle books were funnier than wizard ones. She might have been right. There was a section about a whale that Gavin found funny in a way that sat uneasy in his chest — something about the scale of it, the smallness of a creature against a vastness that didn't know or care — but he laughed anyway, because Miren was doing voices for the characters and Duncan was eating a piece of Gavin's mother's fudge without asking, which was, in its way, the best thing that happened all day. The automatic easiness of someone reaching for your food because he had forgotten, for a few hours, to be careful about what he touched and took and occupied.

The light through the snow-packed windows shifted from blue to grey to the amber of a winter evening lit mostly by fire. Keir Dunbar was in the corner with his chess set, playing both sides and cracking his knuckles between moves, the paper crown still on his head. Two first-years had fallen asleep on the sofa nearest the fire, one of them still wearing her cracker crown, which had slipped down over her nose.

Gavin sat in the armchair by the window and read. He got to page forty of the Muggle novel and the words started sliding past his eyes without catching, and he let them. It didn't matter. The fire was warm and the room smelled like the kitchens below — roasting things, bread, something sweet that might have been the treacle tart the elves made on holidays — and Miren was explaining to Duncan why Exploding Snap was a game of pure luck, and Duncan was holding his ground for once, his voice slightly louder than it had been in weeks, and the fairy lights blinked on and off in their imperfect pattern.

He did not go upstairs to the trunk where the manuscript waited. He did not think about the notation that had been rearranging itself in the margins of his sleep for weeks, the shifting structures that pressed at the edges of his attention even when he wasn't reading. He did not think about the agreements beneath spells or the civilisation that had left or the wards that separated everything from what pressed on the other side. He thought about the chocolate Duncan had given him and the fudge his mother had made and the jumper that was too large and how Duncan had eaten fudge without asking and how good that was. He thought about what a bypass was and why you'd demolish a planet to build one. He thought about the whale.

He was fifteen and sitting with his friends on Christmas Day and the war was somewhere else and the other thing, the larger thing, was also somewhere else, and for one day both of those distances held.

---

He woke in the dark.

The dormitory was cold again — the fire had burned down to ash and the pipes had stopped their ticking. Malcolm Preece was breathing steadily in the bed across the room, the slow rhythm of someone deeply, unconcernedly asleep.

Gavin lay still for a moment, trying to locate what had woken him. No sound from the corridor. No footsteps, no voices, no distant disturbance. The castle was as quiet as it ever got at this hour — the deep-night quiet where the stones themselves seemed to settle into their own weight, and the only sounds were structural. Wind against the window. The packed snow cracking faintly against the glass as the temperature dropped.

He sat up and looked at the window. Nothing. The ground-level view showed the banked snow against the glass, backlit by the faint luminescence the castle's exterior stones gave off at night. Through the frost he could see the suggestion of the grounds — the lawn, the dark weight of the forest's edge, the white blankness beyond.

He got out of bed. He didn't decide to. His body was already moving, feet on the cold stone, reaching for the jumper his mother had sent. He pulled it on over his pyjamas, found his shoes by the bed, and padded out through the dormitory and the common room — empty now, the fire banked to red coals, the fairy lights still blinking at nobody — and up the spiral stairs to the corridor above.

The barrel entrance let him out near the kitchens. The corridor was dark and he walked by wand-light, his footsteps louder than they should have been on the flagstones. He wasn't going anywhere in particular. He was following something that was not a sound and not a sensation in any category he could name but something between — a directional pull that had been in his body since the manuscript had begun changing how he processed things. It had been there for weeks, faint, like a draught from an invisible gap in a wall. Tonight it was less faint.

He found a window on the first-floor corridor, not far from the passage that led to the greenhouses. Tall, narrow, arched, the glass old enough to have ripples in it. It looked out over the grounds toward the lake. The snow had stopped falling. The sky had cleared.

He pressed his hand flat against the glass. The cold went through his palm and into the bones of his wrist and he held it there.

The sky was the same sky he had looked at for five years. The same stars, the same winter constellations in the same positions. Orion was up, tilted at the angle he always assumed by late December, the three belt stars sharp and clean. Sirius burned low near the trees. The grounds below were white and blue and silver and motionless, the snow unbroken except where someone had walked to the greenhouses earlier in the day and the footprints had already half-filled.

And there was something else.

The stars were where they ought to be. What changed was the window. The old glass clicked under his hand, once, twice, dry and brittle. The lead strips between the panes gave a faint shiver. It ran through his palm and up his wrist into the roots of his teeth, and his jaw clenched on it before he understood what he was feeling.

The glass held. So did the stone around it. He could feel the frame and the wall behind it taking strain through his palm, braced and locked, but not without effort.

He had read about the wards in the manuscript. He had translated the section — slowly, badly, losing half the nuance to his own inadequacy — about the structures the Builders had put in place before they left. Vast interlocking agreements that made Earth undetectable. On the page it had been information. Here it was load.

The corridor air changed. The hairs on his forearms lifted under the wool. His ears kept trying to clear and failing. Frost feathered across the inside of the pane in thin white veins, racing out from the places where the pressure met the glass. Far off, somewhere down by the greenhouses, there came a soft run of ticks as ice cracked along the roof panes.

Nothing in it turned toward him. It did not pause at the fact of a boy with his hand on a window. The pressure lay against the wards like floodwater against a gate: full weight, no malice, no need to notice what was holding it back. His lungs shortened anyway. Each breath came shallow. The corridor felt smaller than it had a minute ago.

His hand was numb. He pulled it from the glass and the skin of his palm blazed, then went dead. Red marks from the lead lattice striped his hand though he had not been pressing hard enough to leave them. When he bent his fingers they moved a fraction late, stiff and white.

The stars were the same. Orion was the same. The grounds below were white and still. Somewhere in the castle behind him his friends were sleeping — Miren with the hand warmer on her bedside table, probably cool by now because the charm had a half-life of about two hours, and Duncan in the purple scarf because the dormitory was cold and the scarf was warm, and Malcolm Preece with his blankets pulled to his eyebrows and his one foot sticking out. The fudge tin was on Gavin's trunk with three pieces gone. The book about the demolished planet was face-down on the arm of the chair where he'd left it.

The wards were thinner than they had been.

He didn't know how he knew. The manuscript had described degradation in notation he could barely follow — terms that had no English equivalents, structural relationships he had to hold in his head in four dimensions and watch collapse back to three. But the knowledge was in his body now. The window frame had a little give under his hand, not enough to shift, just enough to tell him where the strain was, and he could feel the same give in the wards. They were still holding, just less completely than before. The margin between what held and what pressed had narrowed. Not by much. Not enough to see or measure or prove to anyone. But the day he had just lived — the fudge and the crackers and the sprouts and the fairy lights and the jumper that was too big and the book and the game and the scarf and the whale — all of it sat on one side of a barrier that was thinner tonight than it had been last month, and would be thinner next month than it was tonight, and nobody in the warm sleeping castle knew.

He stood at the window for a long time. The sky did not change. The pressure did not change. The stars were beautiful and precise and ancient and they did not know either.

He pulled the curtain across the window and went back to bed, but the feel of that give stayed in his hand.


# Chapter 11

In January the quill was still the same one he'd been using since October. A school quill, cheap, the vane split on one side where he'd pressed too hard during Babbling's mid-term assessment. He'd brought it to the sub-collection because it didn't matter — the exercise didn't require a good quill — and because he'd failed with it so many times that switching to a different one would have felt like superstition.

He set it on the stone shelf and sat back. The air in the sub-collection was cold and dry and smelled of limestone, the way it always did. Since Christmas he'd also been unable to miss the slight give in the castle's magic, the sense that the wards were holding with less to spare than they had before. The walls gave off their faint luminescence — not torchlight, not Lumos-light, something older that came from the stone itself and cast no shadows. The manuscript lay open beside him, its notation shifting slowly on the not-parchment surface, but he wasn't reading it tonight. He'd memorized the relevant passages weeks ago. What the notation described wasn't complicated. The execution was.

The substrate around the quill was visible to him the way it had been visible since November — a structural quality in the air, not a glow or a shimmer but a kind of commitment. Everything in the room had it. The shelves, the stone, the air itself. The agreements that governed what things were and how they behaved, the rules that said stone was hard and air was transparent and quills were visible. He could see them the way you could see grain in wood once someone pointed it out. You couldn't unsee it afterward.

The agreement governing the quill's visibility was the one he'd been working on. Not because visibility was easy — nothing about this was easy — but because it was legible. The rule that said this object was visible, that light would interact with its surface and reflect into eyes and produce the experience of seeing a quill, had a shape he could perceive. A structure. A kind of spatial grammar around the quill that specified the terms of its participation in being seen.

For six weeks he'd been trying to loosen it.

The technique, if you could call it that, was not a spell. There was no wand movement, no incantation, no magical energy involved. What the manuscript described was a cognitive operation — a way of engaging with the substrate directly, the way you might reach into the grammar of a sentence and alter a rule so that the sentence still worked but worked differently. A Disillusionment Charm cast invisibility onto an object. What Gavin was trying to do was soften the rule that made visibility apply. He was not trying to hide the quill. He was trying to change the terms under which the quill participated in being seen.

The distinction was, he understood by now, the entire point of the First Art. But understanding the distinction had not helped him accomplish it. Every night for six weeks he'd sat in this room and reached for the structure and tried to adjust it and nothing had happened. The agreement stayed firm. The quill remained stubbornly, completely visible. He'd tried approaching the rule from different angles, at different speeds, with different degrees of focus. He'd tried thinking about it in the manuscript's notation, in Babbling's translational framework, in plain English. Nothing caught. The substrate was there, he could see it, and it would not move for him.

Tonight he did not expect anything different. That was maybe the relevant thing. He was too tired to hope. It was past midnight. He had Transfiguration at nine and would fail whatever exercise McGonagall set because he'd been failing all of them since autumn. His left hand had the tremor tonight — the one from the Cruciatus, which came and went without pattern, a faint judder in his ring and little fingers that made his handwriting even worse than usual. He was cold. The sub-collection was always cold but January cold was different from November cold, a settled permanence in the chill, as though the stone had given up pretending it would ever be warm.

He looked at the quill. He looked at the agreement around it. He reached.

It was the same nothing as every other night. The substrate was there, structured and visible and completely indifferent to his effort. He held the reach for a while — the way you'd hold a stretch past the point of discomfort on the chance that something might give — and felt the familiar blankness of a tool that didn't engage. The manuscript said the First Art was natural, that the Builders had practiced it the way humans breathed, that it was not a learned skill but a way of being in relation to the substrate. But Gavin was not a Builder. He was a fifteen-year-old Hufflepuff with bad Transfiguration marks and a split quill and hands that shook.

He let go and sat back against the stone wall, which was the temperature of January in the Scottish Highlands filtered through six feet of castle. The quill sat on the shelf, solidly and irritatingly visible.

He should go to bed. He was going to feel terrible in Transfiguration. McGonagall had started giving him the look she reserved for specific disappointment — not the general kind she distributed across the class when nobody got the exercise, but the individual kind that meant she'd expected something from him once and had recently revised the expectation downward. He didn't want to see that look on four hours of sleep.

He rubbed his face. His fingers were numb. The tremor in his left hand had settled into a steady rhythm, the faintest vibration, like a string that someone had plucked and forgotten about.

He reached for the substrate one more time, the way you checked a locked door a second time even though you'd just checked it, more habit than hope. The agreement around the quill. The rule that specified its visibility. He could see its shape — something like a weave, if you could weave with obligation instead of thread. The terms that bound this particular arrangement of feather and dried ink to the category of things that light acknowledged.

He didn't push. He didn't try harder than he had any other night. He was too tired for effort. What he did was hold the reach and let his attention rest against the structure of the agreement the way you'd lean against a wall — not trying to move it, just being present against it. The manuscript's notation for this operation, as near as he could translate it, was something like releasing a held emphasis. Not force. Something closer to forgetting to insist.

The agreement softened.

It happened without transition. One moment the rule had the structure he'd been looking at for six weeks — committed, definite, the quill existing firmly in the domain of the visible. The next moment the structure was different. The edges of the agreement blurred outward as though the rule had relaxed a grip it hadn't known it was holding. The quill was still there. Still on the shelf. Still a quill. But the terms of its visibility had changed. The weave was the same weave, but looser, the obligations between light and surface and eye widened to something approximate where they had been exact.

Gavin stared at it.

From his perception — from the substrate-aware perception that the manuscript had built in him since November — the quill looked exactly the same except for the loosened rule around it. He could see the object and the changed agreement at once: the cheap quill on the shelf, and around it the rule gone slack like a knot tugged loose. Both were real, both were there, and only one of them belonged to ordinary sight.

But when he tried to see the quill the way he would have seen it a year ago — before the manuscript, before any of this — when he tried to look at it with just his eyes, the way Miren would look at it or Duncan or McGonagall or anyone — the quill was wrong. It was there, but less there. The light from the walls brushed its surface and slid off, as if the feather had forgotten how to catch it. It wasn't invisible or transparent. It was something that had no word. The quill was present in the room but didn't hold its place inside sight. His eyes wanted to slide past it. When he forced his gaze to stay, the quill wavered at its edges like something seen through old glass.

He was still looking at it when the pain arrived.

It started behind his eyes — a hard spreading pressure, as though something inside his skull had tried to wedge itself into a space too small for it. His vision smeared. The faint stone-luminescence of the sub-collection stretched sideways, the walls running together at their edges, and he couldn't pull the room back into focus. He blinked and his face was wet and he touched his upper lip and his fingers came away dark.

A nosebleed.

He tilted his head forward and pinched the bridge of his nose. Blood ran between his fingers and dripped onto the stone shelf next to the quill. The quill that he had altered. That he had actually, genuinely altered — not with a spell, not with his wand, but by loosening the agreement that governed its participation in being seen.

His vision stayed bad. The sub-collection's stone walls smeared when he moved his head, and the manuscript's notation on the open page swam into illegibility. His brain was protesting. The First Art taxed cognitive architecture, not magical energy — the manuscript had been specific about this. The human mind could engage with the substrate, but the engagement cost something structural. The Builders' minds had been built for this the way lungs were built for breathing. Gavin's was not.

He sat on the floor of the sub-collection with his head forward and blood spotting the knees of his trousers and thought: he'd done it.

The feeling sat low and strange in his chest. He had crossed a line. Theory had turned into practice. He could edit the substrate. He could change the rules. The thing the manuscript described was real and he could do it and the crossing was — the manuscript was right about this too — irreversible. He could not go back to being the person who had only read about the First Art. He was now the person who had practiced it.

His nose bled for another ten minutes. His vision cleared slowly, in stages, like a lens being adjusted by someone with an unsteady hand. When he could see properly again the quill was still altered. The loosened agreement held. He hadn't been sure it would — the manuscript was unclear on whether edits persisted or decayed — but the quill sat on the shelf in its changed state, less visible, less committed, as though the rule had accepted its new configuration and found no reason to return to the old one.

He picked it up. It felt the same in his fingers. The same weight, the same split vane, the same slightly waxy texture of a cheap school quill. The substrate didn't change how things felt to touch. It changed how they participated in the agreements that governed reality. Visibility was one agreement. Solidity was another. The quill was still solid because he hadn't touched that rule. He'd only loosened visibility.

He put the quill in his pocket. He cleaned the blood off the shelf with his sleeve, which didn't clean it so much as spread it into a faint pinkish smear on the stone. He closed the manuscript, folded it carefully into the leather wrapping he'd cut from a ruined bag, and put it back on its shelf in the innermost room. Then he climbed the cold stairs out of the sub-collection and into the seventh-floor corridor.

The corridors connected tonight in the straightforward pattern, the one that took ten minutes instead of thirty. The castle was silent — past midnight, no patrols, the Carrows asleep or at least out of the upper floors. Gavin walked through the dark corridors and the substrate around him was the same as always, the agreements governing stone and air and torchlight steady and undisturbed, and for a moment the fact that he had changed one of those agreements felt improbable, as though it had happened to someone else in a different part of the castle. But the quill was in his pocket. The blood was on his trousers. His head ached in a way that was new, specific, unlike any headache he'd had before — a dull wrongness behind his eyes, as though something back there had been rearranged and hadn't settled yet.

He was on the third-floor staircase when he realized he was smiling. A small, private, stupid smile that had nothing behind it except the fact that something had worked. He stopped smiling when he noticed it, because it felt wrong — too easy, too clean an emotion for what had just happened. But it kept trying to come back.

The Hufflepuff common room was almost empty when he reached it. A fourth-year girl he knew vaguely — Leah something, she sat two rows behind him in Herbology and was always losing her dragon-hide gloves — had fallen asleep on one of the sofas with a Potions textbook on her chest. The fire had burned down to a low orange mound that gave off more light than heat. The room smelled the way it always did: earth, warm wood, and underneath that the yeast-and-onion drift from the kitchens next door, where the house-elves were baking tomorrow's bread.

He went to bed. His hand still had the tremor. His nose had started bleeding again, faintly, a slow warmth on his lip that he pressed his sleeve against. He lay in the dark with the quill in his pyjama pocket and the barrel ceiling low above him and listened to the dormitory's sounds — the steady breathing of one boy, another's occasional mutter from whatever he was dreaming, the small settling noises of the castle adjusting itself in the cold. He knew he should care about the nosebleed, about the headache, about the visual distortion that had taken most of an hour to clear. He knew these were the costs. But the quill was in his pocket and it was altered and he had done it.

He slept. He dreamed about the agreement around the quill, or about something adjacent to the agreement, the way dreams were adjacent to the things that produced them. When he woke up his pillowcase had a brown stain where his nose had bled in the night.

---

He found Miren after Herbology the next day. She was in the common room, sitting cross-legged on the floor near the fire with a spread of parchment around her and a quill behind her ear that she'd clearly forgotten was there. There was ink on her left thumb and on the side of her hand where it dragged across the page. She was writing in the tight, rapid shorthand she'd developed for DA supply logistics — a notation that nobody else in Hufflepuff could decipher, which was less by design than because her handwriting under pressure was atrocious.

"Hey," he said.

She looked up. "Hey." She studied his face for a moment — she did that now, a quick check, her eyes doing a sweep that took in his colour and his posture and whatever it was she was looking for. "You've got something on your —" She gestured at her own upper lip.

He wiped his nose. Dried blood came away on the back of his hand. "Yeah. Nosebleed."

"Another one?"

"It's fine." He sat down beside her, careful not to sit on the parchment spread. She moved one sheet out of his way without looking at it. "Can I show you something?"

Miren set down her quill. The logistics parchment could wait, apparently — she made the choice to be present when someone asked her to be present and then figured out the scheduling later. She had a smudge of soil on her neck from Herbology that she hadn't noticed, and a scratch on her forearm from the Venomous Tentacula that Professor Sprout had them repotting this term. Sprout kept insisting the repotting was a normal part of the fifth-year syllabus, but three students had been bitten last week and Gavin suspected the Carrows had just stopped funding replacement plants for the less dangerous species.

"All right," Miren said.

He pulled the quill from his pocket.

From his perception — from his — the quill was the same as last night. The substrate agreement around it was still loosened, the visibility rule relaxed in its new configuration. He could see the change the way you could see a door standing open that used to be closed. Still a door. Just differently positioned.

He held it out to her.

Miren looked at the quill. He watched her look at it. He watched her eyes try to settle on the object and not quite manage — the gaze sliding sideways, catching, sliding again. She squinted.

"What've you done to it?"

"I've changed it. Not with a spell. With —" He stopped. Started again. "You know the thing I've been working on. The substrate. The agreements."

"The First Art thing." Her voice was careful. Not dismissive. Miren had decided somewhere around Christmas, he thought, to stop arguing about whether the First Art was real and start treating it the way you'd treat a friend's religion — with respect for the belief if not for the content. It was kind of her. It was also exactly wrong. "What about it?"

"I changed the quill. Last night. I edited the — the agreement that governs its visibility. Not a charm. Not a spell applied to the object. I changed the underlying rule."

Miren looked at the quill again. She reached out and took it from his hand. Turned it over. Held it up to the common room's warm firelight. The quill wavered in her grip, its edges indistinct, the split vane blurring into the air around it. Her fingers were solid around something that didn't look entirely solid.

"It's blurry," she said.

"Yeah, but —"

"It's like a Disillusionment Charm. A not-great one. Like..." She tilted her head. "Like a second-year's, maybe? It's still pretty visible. You can see the shape."

"It's not a Disillusionment Charm."

"I know you're saying that. I'm telling you what it looks like."

"And you're right. That's the problem. From your — from where you're seeing it, it does look like that. It looks exactly like a bad Disillusionment Charm. But what I did is different. A Disillusionment Charm applies an effect on top of the object. What I did — I changed the rule underneath. The quill isn't hidden. It's less... it's less subject to being seen."

He could hear himself. He could hear how it sounded. The difference he was trying to make — paint on the wall versus the wall itself, which felt to him like the whole point of all this — sounded, spoken aloud in the Hufflepuff common room, like a boy explaining why his homework was actually better than it looked.

Miren turned the quill over one more time. She was trying. He could see her trying.

"Less subject to being seen," she repeated.

"I know it sounds —"

"No, I'm listening. I'm just — Gav, what's the difference? Actually. If the result looks the same and a Disillusionment Charm does the same thing —"

"It doesn't do the same thing. It does a thing that looks the same. A Disillusionment Charm puts a coat of paint on the quill that makes it hard to see. What I did is — I went into the wall the paint's on and changed the wall so paint doesn't stick the same way. The quill isn't painted invisible. The rule that connects it to visibility is... wider. Softer."

He watched her face. She was listening with the focused attention she gave to things she was determined to understand, her head tilted slightly, one hand resting on her knee. But what she was hearing was a distinction without a difference, because the evidence — the only evidence he could offer her — looked identical either way. The blurry quill. The blurry quill that looked exactly, precisely, maddeningly like a bad Disillusionment Charm.

"Can you show me the difference?" she asked. "Like, is there a test? Something that would work differently on a Disillusionment versus what you're saying?"

He opened his mouth. Closed it. Thought.

"A Finite Incantatem would remove a Disillusionment Charm," he said slowly. "It wouldn't affect what I did. Because what I did isn't a spell. There's nothing to cancel."

Miren's eyes sharpened. That was a test she could work with. "So cast one. Cast a Finite on it. If the blurriness stays, you're right."

"I can't. I mean — I can, but it wouldn't prove anything. You'd just see a still-blurry quill and you could say the Disillusionment was layered, or that my Finite was weak, or —" He stopped. It just went in a circle. Every way of proving the difference needed the other person to see the substrate, and there was no way to hand someone that sight without first putting them through the same change the manuscript had already made in his head. The proof was behind the same locked door as the sight.

Miren was quiet for a moment. She handed the quill back to him.

"I'm not trying to..." She paused. Restarted, which was unusual for her. Miren didn't usually need a second run at a sentence. "I don't think you're making it up. I think you believe it. And maybe you're right, I don't know. But what I can see is a blurry quill, Gavin. That's it. That's all I've got."

"I know."

"And you got a nosebleed doing it?"

"Yeah."

"So." She looked at him. He could see the expression assembling itself on her face — concern layered over frustration layered over something else, something that might have been grief if grief could be that tired. She'd been wearing some version of this expression since Christmas. "You stayed up all night, you gave yourself a nosebleed, and you made a quill a bit blurry. From where I'm sitting."

"From where you're sitting. Yeah."

"I'm not being —"

"I know. You're not being anything. You're telling me what you see."

"Right."

They sat with that for a moment. The common room fire crackled and shifted, a log settling into the grate. The second-years in the far corner were playing Exploding Snap — the soft slap of cards and the occasional small bang that made everyone flinch slightly this year, because loud noises meant different things now. The kitchen smell had shifted to something with cinnamon in it, probably whatever the elves were testing for Saturday's pudding.

"How's the DA stuff going?" he asked, because he didn't know what else to say, and because she was his friend and her life was not a sounding board for his demonstrations.

Miren glanced at the parchment spread on the floor. "Fine. Seamus wants to do another graffiti run but Neville thinks we should wait until after the Slytherin match because the Carrows'll be watching the corridors." She rubbed the Herbology soil off her neck, noticed the ink on her thumb, and let that go. "We're low on dittany again. Hannah's been trying to nick some from the hospital wing but Pomfrey's started locking the supply cupboard, so." She shrugged. The shrug carried more weight than it looked like it should — the specific weight of someone whose supply logistics had life-or-death stakes and who was fifteen years old and keeping track of them in illegible shorthand.

"Do you need help with —"

"No." Not sharp. Just certain. She'd said it this way before. "I need you to sleep and eat and stop bleeding from your nose. Those are the things I need from you."

He put the quill back in his pocket. "Okay."

"Okay." She picked up her logistics quill — the one she'd recovered from behind her ear — and turned back to her parchment. He sat beside her for a while, not talking. She wrote, her hand moving fast, the scratch of quill on parchment steady and purposeful. After a few minutes she paused and looked at him sideways.

"Is your vision doing that thing again? Where you look past things?"

"No. I'm just sitting."

"All right." She went back to writing. He sat there until his head started to ache again and the edges of the room went soft, the fuzziness at the margins of his vision that meant the cost of last night was not finished with him yet. Then he got up and went upstairs.

---

The dormitory was empty. The other boys were at dinner. He could hear the Great Hall's muffled rumble distantly — two floors up and sideways, the institutional clink and scrape of a school meal, the low hum of a student body that was smaller and quieter than it should have been. He should eat. He would eat later, probably. Or not. Food had become something he forgot about and then remembered when his hands shook from something other than the Cruciatus remnant.

He sat on his bed and took the quill out of his pocket.

It sat in his hand, less visible than a quill should be. He could still make out the split vane and the dried ink at the nib. The loosened agreement around it was perceptible to him as a changed texture in the substrate — like a patch of softer ground in a field, a place where the rules gave more than they did elsewhere.

He held it up to the dormitory light. The quill wavered in the glow. Its edges were soft. Looking at it felt like looking at something through water, something present but not holding still in the right way. He set it on the bedspread to free a hand for his handkerchief and immediately lost it. His eyes slid to the blanket fold beside it. He had to pat the coverlet twice before his fingers found the shaft.

He stared at the quill for a moment, then pulled a bit of spare parchment from the nightstand drawer. Fine. He'd do a practical test. He put the parchment on his knee, dipped the nib in the ink bottle, and tried to write his name.

The G came out badly. By the a his gaze had slipped off the nib again and the letters climbed uphill across the page. He stopped with Gav skewed toward the corner, the ink line frayed where he'd pressed too hard while trying to keep track of where the point actually was. That was one consequence. He had turned a cheap school quill into something nearly useless for homework.

His nose started bleeding again. A warm trickle ran down his left nostril. He found the handkerchief in his bedside drawer — already stained brown from this morning, the Scourgify he'd done before breakfast apparently not up to the task — and pressed it to his face. The blood was bright against the cotton. His vision blurred again, softly, at the edges. The dormitory's shapes — bed, trunk, nightstand, someone's Quidditch magazine on the floor, a sock that had missed the laundry pile — went liquid, as if the room were seen through rain on a window.

He was still sitting there with the handkerchief to his face when the room tightened.

The round window clicked in its lead frame. Every hair on his forearms lifted. The wall sconce opposite his bed thinned from a steady amber tongue to a blue needle edged in orange. The change was small, maybe two seconds, but the whole dormitory felt narrower in that interval, as if the stone had drawn half an inch closer to his ribs. On the bed the altered quill lost another degree of itself. The shaft did not just blur. Its middle seemed to go missing, nib and vane hanging in the air with barely enough quill between them.

Then the pressure eased. The sconce swelled back to amber. The window went still. The quill settled into its usual half-seen state on the blanket.

Gavin wiped his nose and looked at the quill. The wards had thinned again. It was not enough for anyone else in Hufflepuff to wake up and say the room felt wrong. It was not enough to leave a mark you could point at tomorrow morning. But the room's magic had registered it, and the first thing he'd changed had registered it worst of all.

He cleaned the handkerchief with a Scourgify that mostly worked — his wand still did ordinary spells, and he was still mediocre at them — and the worst of the blood vanished, leaving a faint pinkish ghost on the cotton. Then he set the quill on the nightstand instead of putting it back in his pocket. If the wards shifted again, he wanted the loosened thing where he could see what happened to it.

He turned on his side. Through the round window he could see the January dark, the grounds invisible except for the faint light from Hagrid's cabin far below and the deeper black of the Forest beyond it, its edge just visible against the sky. The quill sat on the nightstand, not quite holding still in the sconce-light. He watched it until sleep took him.


# Chapter 12

Carrow was peeling an orange when they came in. He stood behind his desk with his sleeves pushed up past the forearms, digging his thumbnails into the rind and dropping strips of peel onto the wood in damp curls. The classroom smelled of citrus and, underneath it, the flat mineral odour that never left the stones anymore — scorched air, old copper, the residue of whatever had happened in here during the last lesson. The fifth-years filed in and sat along the walls.

The desks had been shoved against the stone since November, clearing the center of the room into an open floor that Gavin had stopped thinking of as a teaching space and started thinking of as a place where things happened to people. Students sat on the flagstones with their backs to the wall, schoolbags between their knees, wands out because you needed your wand out for Dark Arts or Carrow noticed. Gavin took his usual spot near the back left corner, where a torch bracket cast enough shadow that Carrow's gaze tended to skip past him. It wasn't reliable. Nothing was reliable. But the shadow felt like something, and this year Gavin took what he could get.

Duncan was already seated three places to his left. He'd been reading something when Gavin came in — not a textbook, something smaller, a folded piece of paper he'd been holding close to his face in the way of someone with a private thing in a public place. He put it away when the room started filling, tucked it into his trouser pocket with a quick, practised motion. It was a letter from home, probably. Duncan's parents wrote through the Muggle post, which got screened at the gates, and the letters that made it through were rare enough that Duncan carried them in his pocket for days. Gavin had seen him refolding one once, creasing the paper along the same lines with his thumb until the fold was soft as cloth.

Duncan set his wand across his knees and sat very still. He was thinner than he'd been at Christmas, and Christmas had been thin. His school robes sat on his frame the way a sheet sits on a chair — you could see the shape underneath but the fabric didn't touch where it was supposed to. His fair hair needed cutting and he hadn't cut it, because cutting it meant going to the fourth floor, where the barber portrait worked, and the fourth floor was near Alecto Carrow's office. Duncan had learned the building's geography as a system of risks, and his shaggy hair was a map of routes he'd stopped taking.

A girl from Ravenclaw — Lisa Turpin, who always sat near the door and kept her bag half-packed as if she might need to leave in a hurry — was the last one in. She pulled the door shut behind her, and the sound of it closing was the sound of something sealing.

Carrow finished his orange. He ate it in sections, pushing each one whole into his mouth and chewing with his lips open, the wet sounds audible in the quiet room. When he was done he wiped his hands on his robes, leaving damp smears on the black fabric, and looked up at the class the way you look at a shelf of supplies to decide which one you need.

"Right," he said. "Last week we did control. How your intent shapes the curse. Who remembers?"

Nobody answered. This was normal. Answering in Carrow's class meant being noticed, and being noticed meant being used.

"Nobody. Brilliant." He picked at something between his teeth with a thumbnail. "Means I've got to show you again, don't I? Can't have you lot going soft. The Dark Lord's got standards, even if you haven't."

He came around the desk. His gait was heavy, slightly bowlegged, the walk of a man who took up space because taking up space was the thing he was best at. He scanned the room. Gavin watched his eyes move from face to face with the casual assessment of someone sorting objects by usefulness.

"Flett."

Duncan's hands went still in his lap. That was the only visible change — his fingers, which had been lightly pleating the fabric of his trousers, stopped moving and pressed flat against his knees.

"Up. Come here."

Duncan stood. He picked up his wand and walked to the center of the room, and Gavin watched the walk — each step measured, each foot placed carefully, nothing hurried, nothing reluctant, nothing that could be read as defiance or fear. Duncan kept his weight even and his body quiet and watched the floor in front of him. He stopped in the center, turned to face Carrow, and waited with his wand at his side.

Carrow looked at him. There was a moment — brief, almost invisible — where something in Carrow's face shifted, and Gavin thought it might be uncertainty — not compassion, not hesitation, but the quick, involuntary calculation of a man who knew he was performing for an audience he couldn't see — Snape, the Dark Lord, whoever Carrow imagined was judging whether he was hard enough for the job he'd been given. Then it was gone. Carrow bit a piece of skin off his thumb, a habit Gavin had seen before, and raised his wand.

"The thing about the Cruciatus," he said, addressing the room, "is that most of you don't mean it. You wave and you say the word and nothing happens because you haven't got the bottle. That's what intent is. You've got to want it. You've got to find the part of them that you reckon deserves it — the bit that's wrong, the bit that shouldn't be here — and you push the curse at *that*." He looked at Duncan. "Some of them make it easy."

He flicked his wand.

Gavin saw it at two levels.

At the level everyone in the room could see, red light hit Duncan in the chest and Duncan's body stopped belonging to him. His legs folded and his back arched and he hit the floor with the boneless, graceless impact of something dropped from a height. His wand clattered away across the stone.

At the substrate level — the level Gavin had been learning to perceive since the manuscript had restructured his cognition, the level under spells and inside them — something else happened. The rules governing Duncan's nervous system were there, running quietly, the ordinary agreements that said nerve signals corresponded to physical states, that pain mapped to damage, that the body's alarm system was tethered to reality. The Cruciatus folded those rules. Smoothly. The ordinary agreements gave way to something simpler: maximum signal everywhere, no referent, pain with no source and no location and no purpose except its own continuation. Gavin could see the substitution directly now, as plainly as he'd seen the visibility rules around the quill. It was a single edit. One agreement suspended, another imposed in its place.

Duncan screamed.

The sound filled the room from wall to wall. Gavin felt it in his ribs. It started in Duncan's chest and came out through his throat as raw noise, shapeless, nothing Duncan was choosing to make. His fingers scraped the flagstones. His heels kicked against the floor with a rhythmic, involuntary motion, the muscles in his legs firing and firing in response to signals that had no origin in anything that was actually happening to his body. His back arched again, lifting his shoulders and hips off the stone while his head stayed down, the tendons in his neck standing out like cords, and his mouth was open wider than Gavin had thought a mouth could open — the jaw at a wrong angle, the lips pulled back, the teeth visible.
