Gavin sat against the wall with his bag between his knees. His left hand was shaking — the intermittent tremor from his own Cruciatus, months ago, which came back at unpredictable moments and was there now, his fingers tapping against the leather of his bag in a rhythm he wasn't choosing. He watched Duncan's face and he watched the substrate beneath the curse and they existed simultaneously in his perception, the human reality and the mechanical reality, and neither cancelled the other out.

The sound Duncan was making changed. It broke into shorter bursts as his diaphragm spasmed, and in the gaps between bursts Gavin could hear the smaller sounds the scream had been covering — Duncan's nails on the stone, the wet click of his jaw clenching and releasing, the scuff of his shoes. Someone in the room was crying. Gavin didn't look to see who. Lisa Turpin had her face turned away and her hands were pressed flat over her ears and her bag was still half-open at her feet.

The curse held — five seconds, then eight. Carrow stood over Duncan with his wand pointed down and his face had settled into the expression of a man doing something that required effort but not doubt. The satisfaction was not theatrical. It was the plain, workmanlike satisfaction of a person who has been told he is good at this specific thing and has chosen to believe it.

Gavin looked at the substrate again.

The edit was clean. Simple. One rule suspended, one substituted. Structurally, it was the same kind of operation he had performed on the quill's visibility — looser even, cruder, a blunt overwrite rather than the careful loosening he'd attempted in his single successful practice. If he reached into the substrate beneath the curse and restored the original agreement — put the page back, cancelled the substitution — the pain would stop.

Could he do it?

He didn't know. His one successful edit had been on a stationary object with no competing intent, and it had cost him a nosebleed and an hour of blurred vision. This was a live curse held in place by Carrow's will, and Carrow was three metres away with his wand out. The gap between making a quill slightly less visible and disrupting an active Cruciatus Curse in a room full of witnesses was real. It might be unbridgeable.

He didn't try.

The thought was not a decision. It was an absence — a place where a decision should have been and wasn't. His hands stayed on his bag. His wand stayed across his knees. He watched Duncan's body spasm on the cold floor and he saw the substrate structure that was making it happen and he sat against the wall and did not move. The uncertainty was real — he probably couldn't do it — but the uncertainty was also a kind of shelter, and the shelter was comfortable, and the comfort was the thing Gavin couldn't look at while Duncan was still screaming.

Carrow lifted the curse. Duncan stopped making noise. His body kept moving for several seconds — involuntary shaking in his legs, his fingers opening and closing on the stone, the aftermath of a nervous system that couldn't believe the signals had stopped. He lay on the floor with his cheek pressed to the flagstone. His breathing was fast and shallow and audible across the room. His eyes were open and they were not looking at anything.

"See?" Carrow said. He sounded the way he'd sounded after the orange — casual, finished, moving on. He wiped his wand hand on his robes. "That's what intent looks like. Now. Pair up."

The room shifted. Students stood, shuffled toward each other, began the routine that had become familiar enough to be merely awful. You found someone you could trust to say the word and withhold the intent, someone who would aim the wand and produce the minimum — a sting, a twitch, nothing like what Carrow had just demonstrated. The pairing happened quickly and without discussion, partnerships established weeks ago through the grim arithmetic of who would hurt you least.

Gavin stood. His legs worked fine. Everything worked fine. He paired with Michael Corner, a Ravenclaw who had bitten the inside of his cheek raw over the past months and who always tasted blood during these exercises. They took turns. Gavin's Cruciatus produced a faint shudder in Michael's arm, nothing more, because Gavin could not make himself mean it and the spell knew. Michael's produced a cramp in Gavin's shoulder that faded in seconds. They repeated it twice because Carrow was watching, and both times the result was the same — an echo of a curse, an intention refused.

On the floor between the paired students, Duncan had not been told to get up. He lay where he'd fallen, his breathing slower now but still audible, the wet sound of someone whose mouth was full of something he hadn't swallowed. His lip was bleeding. He'd bitten through it during the curse, or his face had hit the stone when he fell — Gavin couldn't tell which, but the blood was there, running down Duncan's chin in a dark line, collecting in the hollow of his throat, dripping onto the flagstones in a small pool that was almost black against the grey stone in the torchlight. Students stepped around him as they paired up. They stepped carefully. Nobody touched him.

"Get up, Flett," Carrow said eventually, not looking at Duncan, sorting through papers on his desk. "You're in the way."

Duncan got up. The movement took a long time. He got his arms under him, locked his elbows, pushed himself up to his hands and knees. His arms were shaking with a fine, persistent tremor. He got one foot under him, then the other, and stood, and swayed, and steadied himself. He walked to the wall with the same careful, measured steps he'd used to walk to the center of the room, except now his body was unreliable and the careful steps were also effortful, each one a negotiation between his intention and his legs' willingness to participate. He sat down against the wall and picked up his bag and held it in his lap and did not look at anyone.

His lip was already swelling. The blood had crusted on one side and was still wet on the other, and the swelling pulled his mouth into a lopsided shape that made him look both younger and older than fifteen — younger because of the vulnerability, older because of the damage.

The bell rang. Gavin packed his bag. He walked out of the classroom behind three other students and did not look at Duncan and did not look at the blood on the floor and his hands were steady except for the left one, which shook with its own old damage, separate from everything that had just happened.

***

He went to the sub-collection instead of the common room.

He did not want to be in the common room, where Miren would ask what had happened in Dark Arts and where Duncan would arrive eventually and sit in his armchair and say he was fine. He did not want to be around anyone. He took the seventh-floor corridors that rearranged themselves — today the route was short, three turns and the staircase that wasn't always present and the door that looked like a wall to anyone who hadn't walked this way before — and then he was in the small stone room with its cold air and its mineral smell and its light that came from the walls themselves, older than the castle's torch system, older than anything in the building except possibly the manuscript itself.

He sat at the stone shelf he used as a desk and took the manuscript from his bag and opened it to the page he'd been working on and looked at the notation and could not make himself read it.

He was running through the practical question step by step, using the same method Babbling had taught him for translation problems.

The Cruciatus operated through a substrate rule substitution. One agreement suspended — nerve signals correspond to physical states — and another imposed — maximum signal everywhere, no referent. It was a crude edit, a blunt instrument at the substrate level. Structurally simpler than the visibility adjustment he'd made to the quill. The edit itself was not the problem.

The problem was the live cast. Carrow's will held the substitution in place. Disrupting it would mean reaching into the substrate beneath an active curse, maintained by a caster with a wand, and restoring the original agreement while the caster was actively sustaining the replacement. He had done one substrate edit, on a quill, in an empty room. The distance between that and this was the distance between lifting a stone and holding back a river.

He probably couldn't have done it.

But he hadn't tried. He hadn't reached for the substrate. He hadn't tested whether Carrow's intent would resist. He'd let the question sit there until Carrow lifted the curse and the question became irrelevant. In the sub-collection he looked at his hands on the stone shelf and could still see them on the bag in his lap, staying where he'd put them.

He became aware of the cosmic rationale beginning to assemble itself at the edge of his thoughts. The wards. The thing beyond them. The scale of what was actually at stake, the framework that made Duncan's suffering — real, specific, happening to a person Gavin knew — into a smaller thing inside a larger thing, a local cruelty inside a planetary emergency. The framework where spending cognitive resources on disrupting one curse in one classroom was a misallocation when the substrate itself was degrading at a scope that made human cruelty look like weather in a single room. The framework was there, available, and it might even have been true.

He shut it down. The argument kept trying to finish itself anyway — wards, scale, triage — and he shut the manuscript with both hands, hard enough that the sound cracked in the little room. Then he pushed it to the far end of the shelf and sat with his hands trapped under his thighs until the neat sequence of thoughts broke apart.

His nose started bleeding.

It came without warning, without practice — he hadn't been doing anything with the substrate, hadn't tried any exercises. It was just a nosebleed. The blood dripped onto the stone shelf next to the manuscript, and it was the same dark colour as Duncan's blood on the classroom floor, which was an obvious connection and a useless one. He tilted his head forward and let it drip and did not wipe it because his handkerchief was in his bag and his bag was on the floor and reaching for it felt like an effort his body was not prepared to make.

He thought about the letter Duncan had been reading before class. It was something from home — the Muggle post, screened at the gates, most of it confiscated. The letters that got through were handled with the careful attention of a person holding something that might be the last of its kind. There was an entire life folded into that letter — a kitchen in Sheffield, parents who wrote on regular paper with regular pens, a world where Duncan was not a Muggle-born in an occupied school but someone's son who'd gone away for the year and would come back. Gavin had watched the person who carried that letter convulse on a stone floor, and his dominant thought — not the only thought but the one with the most weight, the one that had occupied the most space in his skull — had been whether the substrate edit was technically feasible.

The nosebleed slowed. He wiped his face on his sleeve and looked at the manuscript page. The notation moved in its slow, representational way, configurations shifting to show relationships between concepts he was beginning to read, and he could not engage with it. Every time his attention settled on the patterns, the pattern from the classroom surfaced instead — the clean substitution that had turned Duncan's nervous system into an instrument of its own torture.

He put his forehead down on the stone shelf. The surface was cold. The air smelled of limestone and old dust. The light from the walls was steady and unconcerned. He sat like that for a long time with his forehead on the cold stone and the blood drying on his lip and did not do anything that could be called thinking, because the thoughts that were available to him — the practical question and the moral question and the question of what kind of person sits and watches — did not lead anywhere he could go.

After a while he picked his head up and wiped his face again and opened the manuscript and made himself read. The notation resolved slowly, requiring the sustained attention that crowded out other thought, and the crowding-out was the point. He read. The translation problem absorbed him. The image of Duncan on the floor did not disappear, but it shifted to the edge of his attention. He kept running into it anyway. He worked on the ward-substrate coupling problem until the light from the walls dimmed — it did that sometimes, following a cycle that had nothing to do with the castle's rhythms — and then he packed the manuscript and went back down through the rearranging corridors, which took longer this time, twenty minutes instead of five, the castle not cooperating.

***

Miren was in the common room when he came through the barrel entrance. She was standing near the notice board, talking to Ernie Macmillan, who was holding a rolled-up piece of parchment and nodding at something she'd said. She saw Gavin and broke off from Ernie and came over.

She had a bruise on her jaw. It was fresh — purplish and dark, the size of a thumb, on the left side below her ear. It hadn't been there at breakfast. She didn't mention it. Ernie had glanced at it while they were talking and hadn't mentioned it either, which meant it was DA-related and therefore not discussed in the open common room.

"I need you to do something," she said. She sat down across from him at the study table where he'd dropped his bag. "Lookout. Tonight. An hour, probably less."

"What's it for?"

"Supply run. Healing paste, dittany, some bandages. Neville's lot have a delivery coming through the passage near the greenhouses and someone's got to watch the east corridor while they bring it in."

"Can't someone else do it?"

"Everyone else is already doing something." She said it flatly, without emphasis. It was a statement of logistics, not a plea. "It's an hour, Gav. Stand at the corner by the Charms classroom and if you see anyone coming, send red sparks. That's it."

He wanted to say no. The task itself was easy. That wasn't the problem. He knew what would happen. He would stand at the corner and his attention would slide away from the corridor and toward the coupling problem he'd been working on, and he would miss something or react late and it would be worse than if no one had been watching at all. He knew this about himself. He knew it with the specific certainty of a person who had been watching his own attention wander for months and could no longer pretend it was accidental.

"Yeah," he said. "All right."

Miren looked at him for a moment. He didn't know what she saw — the dried blood in his nostrils, probably, and the ink on his jaw where he'd been resting his chin on his hand in the sub-collection, and whatever his face was doing. She didn't ask about any of it.

"Ten o'clock. The junction by Charms. I'll come get you."

She went back to Ernie. They bent over the piece of parchment together. Gavin watched them for a moment and then stopped watching because the parchment was DA business and he had not earned the right to know what was on it.

Duncan was in the armchair by the fire. He was sitting as he sat now — pulled in, knees drawn up, his body occupying as little space as possible. His lip was swollen and dark on one side, the cut splitting the skin in a line that would scar if it wasn't treated, and it probably wouldn't be treated because the healing paste was being smuggled and hadn't arrived yet. He had a book open on his knees but he wasn't reading it. He was looking at the fire with the fixed, inward gaze of someone whose attention was not in the room.

Gavin got halfway out of his chair. Duncan lifted a hand to his mouth, touched the swelling there, and winced. The sound from the classroom came back whole. Gavin stopped hard enough that the heel of his shoe scraped the floor. He stood there with one hand on the strap of his bag, not saying anything, until the moment had gone too long to be casual. Then he sat down again.

He opened his bag and took out the Charms essay he'd been writing and turned it over to the blank back and started sketching notation. The coupling problem was there, where he'd left it — the ward-maintenance principles assumed a uniform substrate density across the planetary surface, but the local density around Hogwarts was anomalously high, thickened by centuries of magical activity, and the coupling equations collapsed unless he accounted for the differential. He'd been trying to model it all week. The notation kept resolving into configurations he couldn't interpret, which might mean the differential was too complex for his current notation skills or might mean his initial assumptions were wrong. He worked on it until ten o'clock, filling the back of the parchment with symbols that Babbling would have recognized as notation and nobody else in the room would have recognized as anything.

Miren came back. "Ready?"

They left through the barrel entrance and went up the stairs in near-darkness. The castle was quiet — the Carrow curfew meant most students were sealed in their common rooms by nine, and the corridors had the hollow, over-large feeling of a building that should have been full of people and wasn't. Their footsteps were loud on the stone. Water was running somewhere inside the walls — the thaw, snowmelt seeping through the outer stonework — and the sound made the castle feel less like a school and more like a thing that was slowly, imperceptibly eroding.

Miren walked ahead, moving with the quick, quiet confidence of someone who had done this before, who knew which corridors had monitoring enchantments and which flagstones squeaked and where Filch ran his patrols on Friday nights. She'd been doing this work for weeks, probably months — the supply runs, the lookout shifts, the small logistical acts of resistance that didn't make anyone a hero but kept people fed and healed and hidden. The bruise on her jaw was part of it, the cost of a specific incident she would not discuss.

They reached the junction. Miren pointed. "There. You can see down both directions. Anyone comes — Carrow, Filch, Snape, anyone — send sparks. Don't wait to identify them."

"Got it."

"I'll be at the greenhouse passage. Twenty minutes, maybe less."

She turned and walked away down the corridor. Gavin watched her go — the practical plait, the quick stride — and then she was around the corner and he was alone at the junction with his wand in his right hand and the east corridor stretching in two directions, lit by torches at wide intervals that left dark gaps between them.

He watched. He scanned the corridor in both directions, listened for footsteps, kept his wand ready. The stone walls were damp — he could feel the cold moisture in the air, could see the faint sheen of water on the nearest wall where it caught the torchlight. The sound of dripping was louder here, closer to the castle's outer shell.

Three minutes passed. He watched the corridor and it was empty and the silence pressed on his ears and the coupling problem was right there, sitting at the front of his mind with the patient insistence of something that would not be ignored.

He held it off for another minute. He counted flagstones. He tracked the torch flames. He tried to anchor his attention to the physical fact of the corridor — the cold, the damp, the dark at either end — and for sixty seconds it worked.

Then the coupling problem expanded. If the density differential was logarithmic rather than linear, the notation's collapse into unreadable configurations might not be a failure — it might be a description of a fundamentally different kind of relationship, one that his linear assumptions couldn't accommodate. The ward-maintenance principles were designed for a civilization that lived in the substrate as naturally as humans lived in air, and their mathematics assumed a perceptual range that Gavin didn't have, and the notation might be collapsing because it was trying to show him something his mind wasn't shaped to receive yet. If he approached the differential as a perceptual limit rather than a mathematical error—

He heard something and his body jerked and his hand tightened on his wand. The corridor was empty — just a torch guttering, shadows rearranging themselves. His heart was beating hard in his ears.

He went back to watching. He held the coupling problem at bay for another thirty seconds before his mind slid back to the differential. If he reframed the logarithmic—

Then he heard footsteps. Real ones, not a torch or a shadow or the water in the walls. Someone walking down the east corridor from the direction of the Astronomy Tower, an unhurried pace, steady and confident.

He raised his wand. His left hand was shaking — the tremor, which had been quiet all evening, was back — and he transferred the wand to his right hand, which was steady, and aimed at the ceiling and sent up red sparks.

He was late. The sparks were late by the two seconds it had taken him to register the footsteps as real rather than imagined, to pull his mind out of the coupling problem and back into the corridor where his body was standing and his job was being done. Two seconds, and two seconds mattered, because the sparks lit the corridor and the person walking was close enough now that the red light showed his face — a seventh-year boy with a DA coin visible against the front of his jumper, his hair dark and unwashed, a scratch across one cheek that suggested his evening had included something more strenuous than walking. He was one of Neville's people, not a threat.

The runner stopped, pressing himself flat against the wall until the sparks died. Then he looked at Gavin.

"You the lookout?"

"Yeah. Sorry. I didn't — sorry, I thought you were—"

"I'm going to the greenhouse end." The runner looked at him for a moment longer — long enough for Gavin to see the expression on his face, which was not anger but something more tired than anger, the look of someone who has learned to factor unreliable help into his plans. Then he moved on down the corridor, and Gavin was alone again.

He kept watching. This time he held his attention on the corridor by force, keeping his eyes fixed on the dark at both ends, and the effort was physical — a pressure behind his eyes, a strain in his forehead, the specific sensation of holding a door shut against something pushing from the other side. The coupling problem pressed and pressed and he kept the door shut and the twenty minutes passed and nobody else came.

Miren came back from the greenhouse end. She had a bag over her shoulder that hadn't been there before, heavy enough to pull the strap tight against her jumper.

"Any trouble?" she asked.

"No."

She looked at him. He waited for her to mention the sparks — the runner would have told her, or she would have seen the red flash against the corridor ceiling from the greenhouse end. He thought she knew. Her eyes stayed on his face for a beat longer than the question required.

She didn't say anything about it.

"Come on, then."

They walked back to the common room in silence. It wasn't easy silence. Miren kept half a pace ahead of him and never looked round. She'd asked him for one hour of attention. He'd lost the corridor to a notation problem after three minutes. The bag strap had dug a dark line into her shoulder. He couldn't tell whether she was angry, exhausted, or simply done talking about it, and he did not ask.

The common room was nearly empty. Duncan was still in the armchair by the fire. His book had fallen shut on his knees and his hands were loose at his sides. His eyes were closed now, and Gavin couldn't tell if he was sleeping or just holding still because holding still was easier than anything else.

Miren dropped the supply bag by the notice board for Ernie to deal with and went over to Duncan. She sat down on the arm of his chair and said something Gavin couldn't hear — quiet, practical, a few words. Duncan opened his eyes and said something back — two words, maybe three. Miren rested her hand on his shoulder and left it there, and it was a plain gesture, nothing dramatic, the kind of thing you did for a person who was hurting when you had no cure. She was good at it. She had been good at it all year, at the small acts of presence that did not fix anything but meant that the unfixed thing was witnessed. She was good at this and Gavin was not good at this and the difference between them was not a matter of talent but of where they were each pointing their attention.

Gavin went upstairs. He sat on his bed and opened the manuscript to the coupling problem and worked on it. After ten minutes his nose started bleeding again, a slow seep that he caught with his handkerchief, tilting his head forward so the blood fell on the cloth and not on the pages. He kept working. The notation shifted under his attention, configurations resolving and collapsing, the logarithmic differential still resisting his attempts to frame it in terms his mind could process.

In the common room below, one floor down and a spiral staircase away, Miren sat with Duncan by the fire. Gavin was on his bed with the manuscript, blood on his handkerchief, the ward-substrate coupling problem absorbing what the guilt had not. These things happened in the same building, on the same night, and the gap between them felt structural, as real to Gavin as the substrate itself: one floor of stairs and something else he had no measurement for.

He worked until his vision blurred and the notation dissolved into shapes he could not read. Then he lay back on his bed with the manuscript on his chest and his robes still on and the bloody handkerchief balled in his fist and did not sleep.


# Chapter 13

Gavin hadn't slept. The Astronomy Tower was the coldest place in the castle and he hadn't brought a cloak.

The stone floor leached heat through his robes as he leaned back against the parapet and pulled the cloth tight around his chest. Wind came through the gaps between the crenellations in bursts that flattened his hair and then died and came again. He'd been up here for a while. An hour, maybe. Long enough that his hands had stopped just being cold and had moved into the numb stage where the fingers didn't bend right when he tried to make a fist.

March in Scotland. The thaw had started but hadn't committed — patches of snow still on the mountains, the grounds mostly mud with green pushing through in places, the lake half-frozen and half-not in a way that looked like a decision the water couldn't make. From up here the Forbidden Forest was a dark shape pressed against the hills, and below it the lawns sloped down to Hagrid's cabin, which was dark. Hagrid might be in the forest tonight. Gavin didn't know. He hadn't paid attention to Hagrid's schedule in months. He hadn't paid attention to most things in months.

He'd come up because the sub-collection was too small. The walls of those cut-stone rooms had started to press. Fourteen weeks of working there — the limestone smell in his clothes, the faint glow of stones that predated everything else in the castle — and the air had begun to feel used. After he'd lain on his bed with the manuscript until the notation blurred into shapes, he'd shoved it back into his trunk and kept walking. Out of the dormitory, out of the common room, up the stairs past the library landing. Up more stairs. Past the Dark Arts classroom, dark now, the door closed. Up again. The Astronomy Tower because it was the highest place he could reach, because there was sky here, because the walls opened.

A telescope mount jutted from the parapet near his left shoulder. Cold iron, bolted into stone. He touched it without thinking and then left his hand there because the metal was cold enough to feel sharp and the sharpness was something real and immediate and unrelated to anything he'd been translating. The manuscript was in his trunk, four hundred vertical feet below him, and he could feel the distance like a taut thread in his awareness. That was new — the sense of where it was when he wasn't with it. Not a pull, exactly. More like being aware of a sound source even when you weren't listening to it.

The sky was clear. No moon worth noticing — a thin paring low over the western mountains, already going down. The stars were the hard, specific kind you got when there was nothing between you and them and the nearest city light was forty miles south. The constellation Gavin always looked for — Orion, the only one he actually knew — was high and tilted over the forest, three belt-stars in a line.

He looked up.

And the wards were there.

Not suddenly. Not with a flash or a sound or anything dramatic. They were there the way the stars were there — present all along, perceivable now because something in him had adjusted, except it wasn't his eyes that had adjusted and it wasn't darkness they'd adjusted to. His substrate perception had been deepening since January, since the quill, since the nosebleeds started. At Christmas he'd felt the wards as pressure — something leaning on glass — and in the sub-collection he'd caught edges of them, fragments, like hearing a few notes of something you couldn't quite place through a wall. But up here, on the tower, with the sky open above him and no stone ceiling or castle architecture between his perception and what was overhead, the wards came clear.

They were enormous.

That was the first thing. They extended farther than the sky — not across it but through it, occupying a layer of reality that the sky didn't know about. His brain did its best: it rendered what his restructured cognition was perceiving as something between structure and weather, something that had spatial qualities because spatial was how his mind handled large-scale pattern. The wards arced overhead in interlocking curves that went past the horizon in every direction. They weren't lines, exactly, but if he'd had to draw them on a piece of parchment they'd have been lines — sweeping, intersecting arcs, each one an agreement about what Earth was and what could notice it.

Back against the parapet, mouth slightly open, he looked at something no one else in the school could see.

Beautiful wasn't what he'd expected. The manuscript had described the wards as structures, functional, engineered, and he'd pictured something utilitarian — a fence, a wall, a shield. They were nothing like that. They were patterned in ways that reminded him of the interference shapes you got when you dropped two stones in still water and watched the ripples cross, except each ripple was itself a structure of extraordinary intricacy and the patterns where they crossed were more intricate still. They nested. A large curve contained within it smaller curves that mirrored the large one's shape at a different scale, and those contained smaller ones, and at whatever resolution his perception could manage there were more — more detail, more structure, more. It was like looking at a snowflake and realizing the snowflake was the size of a continent.

He didn't have words for what they were made of. They weren't made of anything in the way a wall was made of stone. They were agreements — the manuscript's term, the best his translation could manage — rendered in the substrate itself. Each arc was a statement about visibility and attention and category, woven into the grammar beneath magic with a precision that made wand work look like a child scratching letters in dirt. The Builders had done this. Beings for whom magic was air had sat down — or whatever they did instead of sitting — and written, into the deepest level of how reality organized itself, a set of interlocking agreements that said: this planet is not here. Do not notice it. There is nothing to notice.

And it had worked. For however many thousand years, it had worked.

Gavin pulled his knees up to his chest and wrapped his arms around them. The wind pushed at him and he barely registered it. His hands were numb. He was cold in the specific, bone-deep way you got cold when you'd been sitting on stone in March without a cloak for too long, and the cold wasn't important because the wards were beautiful and they were failing and he could see where.

The thin places. He'd known about them from the manuscript — the sections he'd translated in December, the passages about degradation and maintenance protocols designed for a civilization that had left. But knowing was words on a page. Knowing was concepts he'd assembled from imperfect translation and then panicked about on the sub-collection floor. Seeing was different. Seeing was worse.

The thin places weren't holes. They were areas where the interlocking curves had weakened — where the nested agreements had become less forceful, less definite, the way a path worn through grass wasn't a gap in the grass but a place where the grass had thinned. The ward structures in these areas still existed but with diminished conviction. The patterns were still there. They just weren't saying what they said as firmly.

He could see three from the tower. Two were large — spanning what felt like enormous tracts of the ward surface, though his sense of scale was unreliable because the wards weren't operating at a scale where human distance measurements meant much. One was above the Forbidden Forest and slightly east, an area where the intersecting arcs had separated from each other enough that you could perceive what was between them. The other was further north, barely at the edge of what he could resolve, a wide zone where the nested structures had degraded unevenly — some scales still strong, others thinned to almost nothing, like a rope with half its strands frayed through.

The third was small. It was almost directly overhead.

He looked at it. It was a place where the ward patterns had worn smooth, like the letters on an old gravestone that the rain and the wind had been at for centuries. The agreements were still legible. Still present. But they'd lost their precision. The grammar that said *this planet is not here* was, in this small patch, saying something more like *this planet is probably not here*. The filter was thinner. Not gone. Thin.

And through the thin places, he could sense what was beyond them.

He'd expected, if expected was the right word for something he'd been dreading for months, that perceiving it would be different from reading about it. He was right. It was different. But not in the direction he'd feared. He'd been afraid of a presence. Something that would notice him noticing it, something looking back, something with intention. A monster, even though the manuscript said it wasn't one. You could be told something wasn't a monster and still picture a monster because that was what human minds did with threat. They gave it a face and a direction.

It didn't have a face. It didn't have a direction.

What was beyond the wards wasn't a thing. It was a condition.

The first time he let himself lean into it, the skin under his fingernails went dead. The stars above the thin place split into separate distances his eyes couldn't hold together, some seeming close enough to touch and others so far his sight slid off them, and the tower gave a quick sick sideways tilt that made his stomach clench. The stone had not moved. His sense of where he was had.

He swallowed. His ears stayed blocked, the way they did on a fast descent, but there was no relief-pop waiting on the other side. Air went in and out of him and still left a raw panic in his chest, as if breathing had stopped being a complete answer. On this side of the wards, the substrate had rules. Magic operated according to a grammar. Agreements were possible. Things could stay what they were because there were structures underneath them making that statement hold.

Through the thin place overhead, he could feel those structures tail away by degrees. Not a break. A thinning. Like climbing higher and higher until the air still existed but no longer had enough in it for lungs to use: words going short, fingers clumsy, balance giving bad reports. Except it wasn't air. It was the substrate itself becoming less applicable, less able to support the fact of Earth. The agreements thinned, and with them the conditions that let magic and life remain legible to themselves.

The manuscript had used two analogies that kept resurfacing whether he wanted them or not. The first was the gap between wizards and flobberworms, not predator and prey but a scale so lopsided the smaller thing fell below the shape of notice. The second was altitude. Not weather battering the wards. Not something trying to break in. Just the breathable layer ending farther down than it used to, the livable band narrowing century by century while the upper air stayed exactly what it had always been. The high air didn't want to suffocate you. It was simply where breathing quit working.

With his knees against his chest and his numb hands clutching his own elbows, Gavin felt the truth of that shift from translated words into something his altered perception could actually touch. It was worse than he'd imagined because the condition beyond the wards was indifferent. If it had wanted to destroy Earth, that would have been terrible but comprehensible. There would have been a relationship, however predatory, and relationships implied shared categories. But this was worse. It occupied its own terms, and out there the terms that included Earth did not obtain.

Only the wards kept the question from arising. They said *nothing here* and the condition, because it didn't care enough to look closely, because close looking wasn't even the right category, accepted the statement. But the wards were old and no one had maintained them and the statements were getting quieter.

His nose kept running in the cold. The tremor in his left hand made the wool at his sleeve jump in little jerks. Wind shoved at the tower, and the thought came in flat and miserable: the adults couldn't help with this.

Not because they were unwilling. Because they couldn't perceive it. You couldn't fix what you couldn't see, and you couldn't see this without the First Art, and the First Art required months of cognitive restructuring through a manuscript that existed in a single copy in a sub-collection that didn't appear on any map. The adults were busy with a war. Dumbledore was dead. Snape was whatever Snape was. The Ministry had fallen. The Order of the Phoenix was scattered and hiding. Every competent witch and wizard in Britain was occupied with a war about blood purity and political power and none of them — not one — knew the whole place might just stop working.

Gavin was not smart enough.

That was the thing that settled in his stomach like swallowed ice. He'd known it before — Miren had pointed it out, gently, and she'd been right. His Ancient Runes marks were mediocre. His Transfiguration was worse. He'd failed his mock OWL. The manuscript had been designed for minds categorically different from his, and even with the cognitive restructuring he was still Gavin Tench, average at everything, exceptional at nothing, a kid whose best Switching Spell turned teacups into lumps of clay. He'd managed one successful First Art edit — a quill that was slightly less visible — and it had given him a nosebleed that lasted twenty minutes and blurred his vision for an hour. The wards were a planetary-scale structure built by beings who used magic the way humans used oxygen, and the maintenance protocols in the manuscript called for interventions of a sophistication so far beyond what he'd achieved that the gap between his ability and what was required was not meaningfully different from infinity.

At fifteen he didn't know enough. He couldn't do enough. The problem was too large and he was too small and the people who might have helped were killing each other over something that would be irrelevant if the wards failed.

He stayed where he was.

Time passed. He wasn't tracking it. The stars moved in the slow way stars moved — Orion's belt shifting westward, the paring of moon gone now behind the mountains, the sky rotating around its pivot point. The wind died and came back and died again. His body was cold in a way that had gone past discomfort into a kind of background fact. The stone under him was stone. The iron telescope mount was iron. His robes were damp from the wind and the beginning of frost that was forming on every cold surface.

The ward-structure hung overhead, beautiful and degrading. The three thin places. The condition beyond, patient and indifferent, above the line where breath worked.

He could have gone inside. He didn't.

---

The sky began to change.

It happened the way dawn always did — slowly at first, the deep black overhead lightening to a dark blue that was almost black but not quite, the stars fading from the east as the light came up behind the mountains. The ridgeline caught the first change: a thin line of grey along the peaks, then something warmer creeping down the western faces as the sun found the mountains from below the horizon.

The ward-architecture was still there. It hadn't disappeared — it wasn't optical, had nothing to do with visible light — but Gavin's attention loosened. The part of his perception that rendered the ward structures grew tired the way any sense grew tired when you held it at maximum effort for too long. The curves receded. They were still present if he reached for them, a faint architecture of agreements arcing overhead, but they stopped dominating his awareness and the morning came in to fill the space they left.

The morning.

March dawn in the Highlands. The sky went from blue-black to blue-grey to a pale, clean blue that promised nothing warmer than what was already here. Frost had settled on the parapet during the night — Gavin noticed it now, thin and white along the edges of the crenellations, on the iron of the telescope mounts, on the stone floor around him where his own body heat hadn't reached. His robes were stiff with it. His trousers were damp and cold against his legs. He was very cold and he noticed that now, finally, the way you noticed cold when the thing that had been overriding it went quiet.

Below him, the grounds resolved in the growing light.

The lawns were patchy — dead brown and muddy where the last snow had melted, green in strips where the grass was coming back, churned up near the Quidditch pitch where somebody had been walking back and forth for no reason anyone could see. The lake was a flat grey mirror, still partly frozen at its northern edge where the sun didn't reach until afternoon. The Whomping Willow was a bare skeleton across the water, leafless, its branches motionless in the still air. The greenhouses caught the early light and threw it back in irregular flashes, their glass dirty from a winter's worth of weather.

Hagrid's cabin. Smoke was coming from the chimney now — thin, grey, the kind you got from a fire that had burned low overnight and was being coaxed back to life. The pumpkin patch beside it was bare dirt, nothing growing yet, but the soil had the dark, wet look of ground that was thawing and would be ready for planting soon. A light came on behind the curtains. Fang barked once — a deep, muffled sound that carried up through the morning air.

The Forbidden Forest was waking. The trees at its edge were still bare but there was a different quality to them in the March light — the branches not quite empty, buds present though not open, a thickening at every tip that would be leaves in a month. Birds were starting up. Not many. A few early songs from the forest edge, thin and territorial, staking out the morning the way birds did when spring was still a question rather than an answer.

Gavin watched a crow land on the Quidditch pitch and walk across the mud with the deliberate, important stride of a bird that had business. It stopped, tilted its head, jabbed at something in the ground, came up with nothing, and walked on.

Below the tower, in the courtyard, the first students appeared.

Two Ravenclaws — blue-trimmed scarves, walking close together, heading toward the Great Hall. One said something and the other laughed, the sound carrying up in the way sound carried on cold mornings, thin and clean. A first-year came out of the entrance hall and crossed the courtyard at a trot, small inside a coat he was wearing over his robes, late for something or cold or both. A group of older students followed, talking, their voices a murmur from three hundred feet up.

Gavin watched them.

They didn't know. None of them knew. They were worried about the Carrows and the war and whether Harry Potter was alive and whether the DA's last message drop had been discovered and who would be called up in Dark Arts today and what was for breakfast. Those things were real. Duncan's bruises were real. The Cruciatus was real. Those fears had names and timings and bodies attached to them.

Then the things below him he couldn't bear to lose started crowding in at once. The Hufflepuff common room, too warm and smelling of bread through the wall. The plants in the windows doing fine on bad light. Miren arguing Quidditch standings nobody could use this year. Duncan, before he'd learned to sand himself down, muttering about Sheffield Wednesday and his mum's horrible thing with tinned peaches. The first-year Gavin had coached through Levitation in December, face going bright when the feather finally rose. The boy in the courtyard now, kneeling on cold stone to tie his shoe. Fang barking once from Hagrid's cabin. The crow on the pitch walking like it owned the place.

He loved this world. Not the big idea of it. The exact bits. Dirty greenhouse glass. Ink on a student's cheek. Socks that didn't match. Toast eaten on the run. Weeds in the flagstones because nobody had time for weeds.

The boy in the courtyard got his shoe tied, shoved himself upright with one hand on his knee, and went inside for breakfast.

Gavin's grief landed there, in the inventory. Not for himself first. He'd be dead too if the wards failed, him and his manuscript and his mediocre understanding and his nosebleeds and his one blurry quill. But the grief was for the boy who'd tied his shoe, for the Ravenclaws who'd laughed, for the first-year with the feather, for Miren and her arguments, for Duncan whose worst problem should have been the Carrows because the Carrows were the kind of problem that ended. It was for his parents writing letters about the garden and the cat. For Hagrid stoking his fire. For every person in the Great Hall eating breakfast under the enchanted ceiling with no idea what was over the real one.

He still wasn't smart enough. Fine. That hadn't changed. But nobody else even knew there was work to do. Waiting for an adult to notice was useless. Trying to explain it again would only burn time and turn it back into rubbish in his mouth. He would go to class. He would keep his head down. He would hand in what he could and let the rest rot. Every hour he could steal would go to the manuscript and the maintenance protocols and the parts he still couldn't read. He would learn enough to do something, or wear himself out trying, but he was done waiting for somebody better to turn up.

More students crossed the courtyard. The sun cleared the mountain ridge and the grounds went from grey to the specific gold-brown of early Scottish spring — mud turning amber, the green patches of grass brightening, the lake catching light and throwing it in a line toward the forest. A door opened somewhere below and the breakfast sounds drifted up — faint, muffled, the clink of tableware and the low communal murmur of people eating together.

Gavin wiped his nose on his sleeve. His fingers left a damp streak on the wool. He looked up one more time. The ward-structure was there, faint now in the morning light but perceivable if he reached — the arcs and intersections, the nested agreements, the three thin places with their worn-smooth grammar. The condition beyond, patient as geology. The small patch almost directly overhead where *this planet is not here* had become something less certain.

He stood up. His knees cracked. His lower back ached in the specific way stone made you ache when you'd sat on it for most of a night, a deep bruised feeling that went into the hip joints. His feet had gone past numb into the territory where he wasn't sure they'd hold him and he stood slowly, one hand on the parapet, waiting until the pins-and-needles started and he could feel the stone under his soles. The telescope mount was warmer now under his other hand — sun-warmed on its eastern face, the iron going from cold to cool.

He went down the stairs. The spiral staircase was dim after the open tower and he went slowly, one hand on the wall, feeling the stone under his fingers change from the wind-smoothed outer blocks to the rougher interior masonry. The castle was waking around him. Footsteps in a corridor below. A door. The particular morning sounds of a school that was occupied and frightened and still, despite everything, functioning as a school.

He reached the bottom of the stairs and stepped into a corridor that smelled like breakfast. Eggs. Toast. The warm-bread smell that meant the kitchens were going and the house-elves were doing what they did and the day was starting the way days started.

A second-year he didn't know passed him, walking fast, tie loose and shirt untucked, a piece of toast in one hand. The boy glanced at Gavin — his rumpled robes, his frost-damp hair, whatever his face looked like after a night on a cold tower — and kept going. The boy had a smudge of ink on his cheek. His socks didn't match. He was eating his toast in the efficient, distracted way of someone who had somewhere to be and hadn't given a single thought that morning to what was overhead.

Gavin watched him disappear around the corner and then walked toward the Hufflepuff common room. He'd need to change into dry robes before class. He had Charms first period and a Potions essay due tomorrow that he hadn't started. Those things were still facts. Smaller ones. Schoolwork was camouflage now, something to keep him moving through the day without drawing notice while he spent the real hours somewhere else.

He went down the stairs toward the barrel entrance. The corridor near the kitchens smelled like bread, warm and specific and alive. Through a doorway he caught a glimpse of a house-elf carrying a platter of sausages, small and serious-faced, absorbed in the work of feeding people.

The barrel entrance was ahead. He could hear voices inside — Hufflepuffs getting ready for the day, the sounds of a common room doing what common rooms did in the morning.

He tapped the barrel in the right rhythm and went in. The warmth came first — the fire up, the room heated, the contrast with the tower so sharp that his face prickled and his hands began to ache as the blood came back. Students at tables, eating, talking. A sixth-year was grumbling about a Herbology assignment. Someone laughed at something someone else had said. A copy of the *Daily Prophet* lay on a side table, its headline about the war — always about the war.

He went to his dormitory and changed into dry robes. His fingers were clumsy with the buttons. His left hand shook. He dressed and picked up his Charms textbook and his wand, which he kept forgetting he had, and went to class.

Above the castle, the ward-structure still held in some places and thinned in others. He had Charms first period. After that there would be classes, assignments, and the consequences of letting them slide. Then, when he could steal the time, he'd be back in the sub-collection with the manuscript, because the pages still needed reading and there wasn't anyone else to do it.


# Chapter 14

The line was: *I will complete my assignments on time.*

By Monday afternoon, one of the consequences had caught up with him in Herbology.

Gavin wrote it for the fifteenth time, and the word *complete* came out looking like *conplete* because his hand was doing the thing again, the fine tremor in his left fingers that had been with him since November. He pressed harder and the quill dug a shallow trench through the parchment. The ink bled sideways into the tear.

He crossed it out and started the sixteenth line.

Professor Sprout was at her desk at the front of the room, marking third-year essays with the stub of a quill she'd broken that morning on a Venomous Tentacula pot that needed splitting. She held it like a pencil sharpened to its last inch, her thick fingers cramped around it, earth worked permanently into the creases of her knuckles. A pot of something small and yellow-green sat at the corner of her desk — she kept turning it a quarter-rotation every few minutes, chasing the afternoon light that came through the greenhouse-adjacent windows. Her attention had stayed on the essays for twenty minutes straight. The red annotations in the margins were detailed, careful, twice the length of anything the students had probably written themselves. Gavin suspected it was the sort of habit that cost her hours, and that she kept doing it for the same reason she still watered the Greenhouse Three seedlings every morning even though no one was taking N.E.W.T. Herbology this year. That, he thought, was how you got through a year like this: keep doing the old jobs and don't admit what the school had become.

The classroom windows were cracked open. April pressed through them: cut grass, warming stone, the distant sound of someone laughing on the grounds below. The courtyard had daffodils. Gavin could see them from where he sat, bright yellow things pushing up through the beds that nobody had maintained since September, doing it anyway because daffodils did not need institutional permission to exist.

*I will complete my assignments on time.*

He was not going to complete his assignments on time. He had not completed an assignment on time since February, possibly January. The Herbology worksheet on Fanged Geranium propagation cycles had been due eleven days ago and was still in his bag, blank except for his name in the upper-right corner, which he had written and then forgotten about because the properties of Fanged Geraniums were not relevant to anything, and what was relevant was the resonance pattern in the seventh ward layer that he'd found two nights ago in the sub-collection.

The degradation wasn't random. He'd known that since the Astronomy Tower, known it in his body and his restructured perception, but now he could see the structure of the non-randomness — how the substrate agreements that held the wards in place thinned along lines that looked, from the angle his cognition could hold them at, like the grammatical equivalent of wear. Not breakage. Forgetting. The wards were forgetting what they agreed to do, the way a word you say a hundred times in a row loses its shape and becomes just noise. The agreements that made Earth invisible to the thing outside were losing their coherence through something that resembled — this was the closest he could get — semantic erosion. The meaning was leaking out.

And if the forgetting followed a grammar, then the grammar could be read. And if it could be read, maybe it could be written back.

*I will complete my assignments on time.*

His handwriting was terrible. It had always been terrible. His mother had sent him a handwriting-improvement book in second year, a Muggle one with dotted letters you were supposed to trace, and he had used it for a week and then lost it under his bed. The lines he was producing now were worse than second year, though — the tremor in his left hand made his m's look like n's and his t's forked unpredictably, and the overall effect was less a sentence than an illustration of someone having a sentence happen to them without their full cooperation.

He was trying to hold two things at once. The ward degradation grammar, which occupied most of his working mind and demanded an attention he didn't have good words for — like listening to a melody and trying to remember the shape of each note after it passed — and the physical task of moving a quill across parchment to produce something that looked enough like English to satisfy a woman who was not checking anyway. The second thing kept losing.

The seventeenth line. He got through *I will complete my* and then the resonance pattern surfaced again — the relationship between the seventh layer and the substrate beneath it, how the agreements stacked, each one depending on the coherence of the one below it like a sentence depending on the grammar depending on the idea of language itself — and when he looked down at the parchment he had written *I will complete my substrate* and stopped with the quill still touching the page, a bead of ink pooling under the nib.

He crossed it out and started the eighteenth line.

This was his life. This was what it looked like from the outside: a fifteen-year-old sitting in a chair that wobbled on its left front leg, writing lines for missing a worksheet about Fanged Geraniums, with bad handwriting and ink on his fingers. From the inside it was the same, except that behind the handwriting and the wobbling chair, the planetary wards were thinning in a pattern that suggested they might have less time than he'd originally thought, and the question of whether the degradation grammar was readable — actually readable, not just perceivable — was the most important question anyone on Earth was currently working on, and the only person working on it could not spell *complete* without turning it into *conplete*.

He did not think this with any particular sense of irony. He was not the sort of person who stood outside his own situation and observed how absurd it was. He was just in it. The absurdity was structural, like the wobble in the chair, and equally beneath notice.

"Mr. Tench."

He looked up. Sprout was watching him over her broken quill, her face patient in the particular way that meant she was about to say something kind and completely wrong.

"Is everything all right?"

"Yeah. Yes, Professor. I'm fine."

"Because I've noticed — and I'm not the only one who's noticed — that your work this term has been..." She paused. She turned the yellow-green pot another quarter-turn and pulled a dead leaf from one of its lower stems with the practiced, unconscious motion of someone who had been tending plants for thirty years. "We understand the pressure everyone's under. This has been a difficult year for all of us. But your OWL examinations are in June, and if your marks continue on their current trajectory—"

"I know."

"Do you? Because you've failed your Transfiguration mock, your Potions practical was — I'm told it was not good — and now this." She gestured at his bag, where the blank Herbology worksheet presumably lived. "I'm not trying to add to your burden. I'm trying to make sure you're aware that there are consequences that will outlast this year."

OWL examinations. The phrase sat in his head for a moment, inert, like a stone he'd picked up and couldn't remember why he was holding. OWLs were in June. The wards were thinning now. If the degradation pattern he'd identified in the seventh layer was consistent across the other layers — and he thought it was, he was almost sure it was — then the rate of erosion suggested something he wasn't ready to calculate, something measured in years or decades, not centuries, and in that context OWLs were exactly as urgent as the governance structure of an ant colony to a person standing in the path of a flood.

But he could not say that. So he said, "I'll do better, Professor."

"I hope so." Sprout held still for another moment, the broken quill hovering over the essay, and Gavin had the sense she was deciding whether to push further — whether he was a student who needed pressing on or leaving alone. She settled on leaving him alone, and Gavin was grateful because the alternative was saying something insane.

"Finish your lines," she said. She went back to marking. The red quill moved across the third-year essay in her cramped, precise hand, and she adjusted the pot one more time, and the afternoon continued.

He finished the last five lines quickly, his handwriting degrading with each repetition until the final one, *I will complete my assignments on time*, was legible only in the sense that a person who already knew what it was supposed to say might be able to reconstruct it. He brought the parchment to Sprout's desk. The page rustled in her hand. She set it down more carefully than he expected and took a breath through her nose, and Gavin couldn't tell whether that meant pity, frustration, or just the worn-out patience of a teacher who'd watched too many students come apart this year for too many different reasons.

"You can go," she said.

He packed his bag. His nose had dripped without him noticing — just a drop, the kind he got now when he'd been thinking hard about the substrate for too long — and there was a small bloodstain on the cuff of his sleeve that could have been ink. He wiped his face with the back of his hand and left.

---

Miren was in the corridor outside. She had been waiting.

She was sitting on the stone windowsill with her legs drawn up, her bag beside her, looking at the grounds below. The late-afternoon light caught the bruise on her jaw, the one that had been there since March — faded now to a muddy yellow-green that she'd stopped trying to hide with her hair. She didn't talk about it. Whatever had happened, whoever had done it, she'd absorbed it into the set of things she carried this year without comment.

She turned from the window when he came out and slid off the sill. Her whole body had the braced stillness Gavin had learned meant she'd decided to have a conversation she'd been putting off, as if she were about to step into cold water.

"Can we talk?" she said.

"Yeah, sure."

"Not here."

She led him to an empty classroom down the corridor — one of the ones that had been used for Carrow detentions earlier in the year and then abandoned for a different room on the floor above. The desks were shoved against the walls. There were scorch marks on the stone floor and, in the corner, a dark stain that might have been ink and might not. Nobody had cleaned it. Miren sat on a desk and put her feet on the chair below it. Gavin set his bag down and leaned against the windowsill.

"So—"

"I've been keeping track," she said.

That was how she started. Not with accusation, not with heat. With specifics. She pulled a torn piece of parchment from her pocket — not proper notes, just a list in her quick, slanting hand.

"You've missed dinner fourteen times since February. Fourteen. You weren't in the common room twenty-two nights out of the last thirty. You failed your Transfiguration mock. You failed your Potions practical. You just sat detention for a Herbology worksheet, which—" She looked up from the parchment. "Gavin, it's a worksheet. You fill in boxes."

"I know."

"You did lookout for the supply run in March and you nearly got Emily Watkins caught because you weren't watching the corridor. You were staring at the ceiling with your mouth open."

"It wasn't the ceiling, it was—" He stopped. It had been the ceiling, technically. He'd been looking at the way the charm-work in the stone above him layered against the deeper substrate structure, and the pattern had been relevant to the resonance problem, and Emily Watkins had been twenty feet away carrying stolen Dittany, and he had not been watching. "That was bad. I know."

"Duncan got pulled out of Ancient Runes last week. Did you know that?"

"No."

"He was gone for three hours. He came back and wouldn't say what happened. He won't talk to anyone except me, and what he says to me is 'I'm fine,' which is what he always says, and he's not fine, he's—" She stopped herself. She breathed. Her fingers pressed flat against the desk on either side of her hips. "You were in the same room, Gavin. You were sitting four seats away from him when they came and took him out, and you didn't notice."

He hadn't noticed. He'd been in Ancient Runes, in the same room as Duncan, and he had not registered when Duncan was removed because his mind had been in the seventh ward layer, tracing the degradation pattern, and the classroom and its inhabitants had been as distant as a conversation in the next room when you were reading.

"I—"

"Don't." Her voice was flat. Not loud, not cruel. Just done with the soft versions. "Don't do the sorry thing. Don't tell me you've been stressed or you haven't been sleeping or it's the manuscript. I know about the manuscript. You told me about it months ago. You showed me the blurry quill. And I tried — I really tried to take that seriously. But you're worse now, not better, and everyone can see it, and I can't keep—"

She pressed her lips together. Outside the window, the courtyard was golden with late-day sun, and someone was crossing it toward the Great Hall with a bag of Quidditch equipment over their shoulder, walking fast, ordinary, going to dinner.

"I need to say something and I need you to actually hear it." She put the parchment back in her pocket. "From where I'm standing — from where anyone standing near you is standing — you found a book. The book was interesting enough that you stopped doing homework. Then you stopped eating with us. Then you stopped coming to the common room. Then you stopped showing up for things that actually matter. Duncan is getting hurt and you're in the sub-collection. People are running supply chains and message networks and hiding first-years and you're in the sub-collection. And when I ask you what you're doing down there, you tell me the planet is in danger from something I can't see and you can't prove, and—"

"That's not—"

"—and you're the only person who can fix it." She let that sit. "Gavin. You're a fifth-year who can't pass Transfiguration. That's not me being mean, it's true. You've never been the person who solves the thing nobody else can. You're not. You know you're not."

She said it without cruelty. That was the worst part. She was being careful with him — the same care she showed Duncan when she asked how his day had gone. Direct but not unkind, saying the true thing because pretending it wasn't true would be worse.

"I know how I sound," he said.

"Do you?"

"Yeah." He rubbed the back of his neck. The classroom smelled like old spell damage — a burnt-copper smell that had sunk into the stone and would probably never come out. Through the window behind him, the grounds were green and gold. "I know it sounds like I've cracked up. Or I've been alone too long and I've built something in my head that isn't there."

"That's what it sounds like."

"It's not what's happening."

"Then tell me. Really tell me. Not the version where you start talking about substrates and I watch you give up on me halfway through the sentence."

He looked at her. She was sitting on the desk with her feet on the chair and her hands flat on the surface and the fading bruise on her jaw, and she was asking him — properly asking, for what might be the last time — and he owed her an answer that was not the careful, hedged, half-explanation he'd been giving her for months.

"OK." He ran his hand through his hair. His palm came away damp. "Right. OK. When you cast a spell. Any spell. There's the bit you do. Wand, words, what you mean. Under that there's... support, I suppose. That's not right. It's more like floorboards and the beams under them. Most people only ever have to think about the boards. I've been looking underneath."

"You've said this before."

"I know. But that underneath bit — it wasn't always there. Something made it. Not people. Not wizards. Something else. And before it left, it put—" His eyes had moved to the wall behind her. He could feel it happening and couldn't stop it. The substrate in this room was layered — centuries of charm-work deposited on the old Hogwarts stone like geological strata, warming charms and sound-dampening charms and structural reinforcements — but beneath all of that, beneath the castle itself, the ward structure pressed against his awareness like a sound below the threshold of hearing against a dog's ear. Cold without temperature. Presence without substance. The thinning.

"What are you looking at?" Miren said.

"Sorry." He pulled his attention back. "I keep doing that. They put a kind of cover over the planet. Not a wall. More like making the whole place easy to miss. So whatever's outside would pass over us. And it's wearing out. I can see it. I know you can't verify that, I know there's no way for me to prove it—"

"You've told me this."

"Yeah."

"And you're the only person who can see it."

"I think so. I don't know. Maybe there are others, somewhere, but—"

"And you're working on fixing it."

"I'm trying to understand how it works. I can't fix something I don't—" He trailed off. His left hand was tremoring and he put it in his pocket. "I'm still learning. I'm bad at it. I know I'm bad at it."

Miren was looking at him. Not her usual look when she thought he was deflecting, or making excuses, or retreating into the manuscript to avoid what was happening in the real world. Something else. He'd seen her look at things this way before — at corridors she thought might have someone around the corner, at common room conversations she was trying to read — a concentrated, careful attention that took in more than most people bothered to.

"Gavin," she said, more quietly. "When you were looking at the wall just now. Your eyes were tracking something. Moving. Like you could see something there."

"I can. Sort of."

"There's nothing there."

"There is. You just can't—"

"I know. I can't see it." She was quiet for a moment. Her gaze moved from his face to the wall behind her and back. "You really believe this."

"It's not about believing. I can see—"

"I know. You can see it." She let out a breath and rubbed her thumb against the desk edge. Gavin had the uneasy feeling the row was sliding into something else now, the ready anger giving way to something less certain and harder to manage. "That's — honestly, Gavin. That's worse."

"What?"

"If you were just being lazy, I could work with that. If you'd checked out because the year was shit and you couldn't handle it — fine, I'd get it, I'd yell at you and you'd feel guilty and maybe you'd come to dinner. But you actually—" She stopped. "You tell me the planet's dying and your hand won't stop shaking and now you're bleeding again and half the time you go blank while I'm still talking. I don't know what to do with that."

"My nose isn't—" He touched his upper lip. His fingers came back red. He wiped it on his already-stained sleeve. "That happens when I think about the substrate too long. It goes away."

"It's blood coming out of your face."

"Yeah, and then it stops."

"When did you last eat?"

He tried to think. Toast at breakfast, probably. Or had that been yesterday? The schedule of meals kept losing its edges, how everything lost its edges when the resonance pattern pulled him under. He knew this was a problem. He was not unaware.

"That's what I thought," Miren said.

---

The light in the classroom had shifted. The sun had moved past the window and the room was dimmer, the scorch marks on the floor fading into the stone's natural gray. The courtyard below was emptying — students drifting toward dinner, the Great Hall's noise just barely audible through the castle's walls, the clatter and hum of an ordinary evening in a school that was not, this year, ordinary.

Miren hadn't moved from the desk. But the tightness had gone out of her shoulders, and the room felt flatter now that the anger was spent. What replaced it sounded worse to Gavin: tired, precise sadness.

"Here's what I'm asking," she said. Her voice was even. "There's a supply run tonight. Ernie needs lookouts. Hannah needs someone to carry healing stuff to a second-year in Ravenclaw who's been hiding from Dark Arts in an empty classroom. I'm going. Come with me."

He could do it. Find Ernie Macmillan. Stand in a corridor for an hour while stolen healing supplies changed hands. Watch for Filch. Pass the signal. Carry bandages to a girl too scared to go to class.

And while he did, the ward degradation pattern would continue its slow erasure, and the resonance structure he'd been mapping would continue to lose coherence, and the thin places would get thinner, and the time he spent watching for Filch would be time he did not spend on the only problem whose solution space included everyone on the planet, including the people the Dittany was for, including Miren, including Duncan, including the scared second-year hiding in a classroom, all of them inside the sphere of what the wards protected, all of them unaware that the protection was fading, all of them safe only because something built by a departed intelligence was still, barely, remembering what it was supposed to do.

"I'm working on the thing that makes all of that irrelevant," he said.

He heard the word as soon as it left his mouth. Grandiose. Cruel. The kind of word people used when they'd decided their own problem sat above everybody else's. But once it was out he could not find a cleaner one. The scale in his head stayed the same.

"Irrelevant," Miren said.

"That's not what I—"

"It's what you said."

"I know. It came out wrong. I don't mean it doesn't matter, I mean—" He pushed both hands into his pockets, the tremoring one and the still one. "If I stop now, it keeps getting worse. The wards are a bigger problem. That's all. I can't do both. I've tried. My head doesn't split that way."

"So you chose."

"I didn't choose. I just — ended up here."

"You chose this, Gavin. You keep choosing it. Every night you go down there instead of — that's you choosing."

He stayed where he was. Blood touched his upper lip again. He tasted iron.

"I'm sorry," he said.

"I know." Miren slid off the desk. She was standing now, her bag in her hand, and she looked tired in a way that was not about sleep. "But you're not here, Gavin. You're sorry and you're not here, and I don't — I don't know what that does for anyone."

There was a pause. Outside, someone called a name across the courtyard — sharp, bright, ordinary — and then the sound faded into the general noise of the castle settling into evening.

"I'm going back to the common room," Miren said. "The run leaves in ten minutes. If you want to come, come now. If you don't, I'm done asking."

He didn't move. He just stood there with his hands in his pockets and his nose starting to bleed again, a thin warmth on his upper lip.

"Right," Miren said. "OK."

"It's not that I don't care."

"I know it's not." She pushed her hair back. The plait was half undone — she never re-did it during the day, just let it unravel until evening. "But I come to get you and two seconds later you're staring past me at something I can't see. I'm done pretending the blood and the shaking and whatever's happening to your eyes is normal. I'm done being the one who comes to fetch you when you don't want to come back."

"So you're—"

"I'm going to go do what I can do. What's in front of me. And you're going to stay here and do whatever it is you do." Her voice caught — not a sob, just a hitch on the word *do*, which she pushed past. "And maybe that's how it is now."

She stood there for a second, like she was waiting to see whether he'd finally cross the room. He didn't.

"Miren."

"What?"

He wanted to say the wards would keep thinning whether he ate dinner or not, whether he walked a corridor or not. He wanted to say she was standing inside the same problem as Duncan and the girl in Ravenclaw and every person in the castle. But in his head it all turned into the sort of sentence that sounded mad before you reached the end of it, so he left it alone.

"I don't know how to explain it better than I have," he said.

"Yeah." She nodded. "I know."

She stood in the doorway for a moment. The corridor behind her was warm with torchlight and the last of the afternoon sun, and the bruise on her jaw was almost invisible in that light, and she was his best friend, had been his best friend since they were eleven years old and sitting next to each other at the Sorting because their surnames put them close in the alphabet. One hand stayed on the frame. She didn't say anything else, and Gavin had the sense that if he let her walk away now, this version of them was over.

"Be careful," she said. "Whatever you're doing. Please."

"I will."

"Eat something."

"OK."

She left. Her footsteps moved down the corridor — quick, sure, the walk of someone who knew where she was going and what she was going to do when she got there — and then they were gone, and Gavin was alone in a classroom with scorch marks on the floor and a stain in the corner and silence.

He wiped his nose. He sat on the desk she'd been sitting on. It was still warm where she'd been, and the warmth faded as he sat there, and the classroom got dimmer as the last of the light moved past the window, and the castle hummed with the sound of hundreds of people going to dinner and he was not one of them.

Outside, the daffodils in the courtyard had closed for the evening. The grounds were empty. The sky above the castle was the pale, washed blue of an April evening, and behind the sky — above it, through it, in a direction that was not a direction — the wards held. Thinner today than yesterday. The seventh layer's resonance pattern flickering in a way that he was almost, almost able to read, the degradation grammar just past the edge of what his restructured cognition could reach, like a word on the tip of his tongue in a language he was still learning.

She could count missed dinners and lookout shifts and blood on his sleeve. He could see the seventh layer thinning over the castle. The two facts sat beside each other and did nothing to help.

He sat for a while. Then he opened his bag and took out the manuscript.

Its surface was warm against his fingers, as it always was — the not-parchment material holding some residual energy from whoever had made it, or from the agreements encoded in its shifting notation, or from something else entirely that he did not yet understand. He turned to the section he'd been working on. The notation moved, rearranging its representational relationships in response to his attention, and the ward-structure diagrams — he didn't have a better word for them — opened like a sentence continuing from where he'd left off.

He worked.


# Chapter 15

The next morning, on a Tuesday in late April, the Great Hall ceiling was the moment Gavin became sure something had shifted. He only worked out that it was Tuesday afterward, checking the class schedule he'd stopped following.

The Great Hall was three-quarters empty, which had been normal since October. Students clustered at their house tables in tight groups, leaving wide stretches of bare wood between them. The enchanted ceiling showed a spring sky — high cirrus, pale blue, the kind of morning that made the grounds look like they belonged to a different school than the one Gavin was attending. He sat at the Hufflepuff table and looked up at the ceiling and saw it for what it was.
