Seven agreements, interlocking: a display agreement mirroring the actual sky through a perceptual filter, a containment agreement keeping weather on the correct side, a light-sourcing agreement permitting the mirrored sky to illuminate the hall as if the ceiling were absent, and four structural agreements that kept the first three going across centuries without maintenance. He could see where each one committed into the substrate — slight distortions, compression points, like nail-marks in painted wood. Places where ancient intent had shaped the substrate and held it there.

It was neat work. Economical. And utterly boring at the grammar level. Like discovering that a cathedral had been built using six types of brick.

The first sign had come in the third-floor corridor, on the way to breakfast, when he'd noticed that the Permanent Sticking Charm on the portrait of Barnabas the Barmy was failing along its lower-left edge.

Not the portrait itself, not the frame. The agreement beneath the charm — the substrate commitment someone had made centuries ago, probably a long-dead Hogwarts custodian who'd wanted the painting to stay put — was thinning where it anchored to the stone. Old mortar crumbling between bricks, the structure sound but the joints going. A decade more and Barnabas would start to swing.

He kept walking. He was three steps past the portrait before he understood what had just happened.

He hadn't translated. He hadn't seen the portrait and then reached back through the notation he'd memorized, matching the visible effect to a substrate grammar, working out what was there the way he'd been doing for months — slow, effortful, one concept at a time, like reading a foreign language with a dictionary open beside the text. He'd just seen it. The agreement, the thinning, the timescale. All of it at once, the way you saw a word in English and knew what it meant without sounding out the letters.

He stopped in the corridor. A seventh-year Ravenclaw carrying an armful of textbooks so tall she had to tilt her chin to see over them edged past with a muttered "excuse me," and Gavin stepped aside without looking at her. His hands were shaking — the left one worse than the right, as it always was since the Cruciatus — but the shaking wasn't from fear. Something in his head had settled into place the way a dislocated joint settles back, and the relief was physical and strange and slightly sickening.

He served himself porridge. The seat to his left, where Miren had sat every morning for five years, was empty. He noticed this the way he'd noticed the failing charm on Barnabas: as a fact about the arrangement of things, present and requiring no response. She was sitting further down the table with two sixth-year girls who were involved in the DA supply network. He could see the back of her plait, the straight set of her shoulders. She didn't look over.

He finished his porridge, wiped the bowl with a piece of bread, and left the Great Hall. He was noticing everything. That was the strange part. Not that he had to work to see the substrate — he'd been doing that for months, with increasing facility — but that he couldn't stop seeing it. Every enchantment, every charm, every spelled object in the castle was now legible at two levels simultaneously: the surface, where a door locked and a candle floated and a staircase moved, and the grammar, where agreements sat in the substrate like syntax underlying a spoken language. It was like literacy — once you could read, you couldn't walk through a city without reading the signs. The shapes were always also words.

The corridors were busy. Students moved between classes in the tight, purposeful way that had replaced the old between-class chaos — nobody dawdled, nobody stopped to chat in the open. The Carrows had made corridors dangerous. You could be grabbed for anything, assigned a punishment for loitering, for looking wrong, for being in a group of more than three. Students moved like people in a downpour, heads down, pace brisk, and Gavin moved with them, his bag over one shoulder, his left hand in his pocket where the tremor was less visible.

Outside the Charms classroom, two fifth-years from Gryffindor were practicing Stunning Spells on each other. This was Carrow's homework — practice offensive hexes, demonstrate proficiency in class, and do not come unprepared — and the Gryffindors had the grim, dutiful look of students who understood that showing up without competence meant a different kind of lesson. The taller one cast first. Red light, clean trajectory, decent form. The shorter one went down, hit the wall, slid to sitting.

Gavin leaned against the opposite wall and watched.

At the spell level, a Stunning Spell was straightforward: directed magical energy organized to temporarily disrupt the target's conscious function. First-year understanding. But at the substrate level, what Gavin saw was different in kind, though not in complexity. The spell was a sentence. The grammar that made the sentence valid was an agreement: it is possible for directed magical intent to disrupt consciousness at a distance. This agreement was part of a broader class — the entire family of offensive magic shared a root agreement about the permissibility of one magical actor affecting another's physical or cognitive state without consent.

The shorter Gryffindor got up, shook his head, and cast back. His Stunner was shakier — the substrate sentence had a grammatical wobble in it, like a clause with a misplaced modifier — and the taller one dodged rather than blocking, which was probably smart.

Gavin could see how to fix the shorter student's form. Not at the wand level — he'd be useless at coaching Stunning Spells, his own were mediocre on a good day — but at the substrate level. The grammatical wobble was in the intent specification: the caster was targeting the other student's general area rather than committing to a specific disruption mode. If Gavin could have said *tighten the subordinate clause that specifies where in the target's cognition the disruption applies*, the spell would have landed cleanly. But there was no way to say that in any language the student spoke, and the advice would have sounded like nonsense.

He pushed off the wall and walked on.

He was halfway to Ancient Runes when he heard it.

The sound came from around the corner of the fourth-floor corridor: Amycus Carrow's voice, thick and carrying, saying something Gavin couldn't make out. Then a scream.

Gavin stopped. Other students stopped too — you learned to stop when you heard Carrow, learned to press yourself against the wall and become small, because movement attracted attention and attention attracted punishment. Three Ravenclaw girls ahead of him went still against the stone. A heavy-set Hufflepuff sixth-year named Marcus Belby — not the famous one, a different Belby, a quiet kid who always wore a second scarf even indoors and smelled faintly of the greenhouses where he spent his free periods — put one hand flat against the wall as though bracing for something.

Gavin looked around the corner.

Carrow had a student against the wall. A boy, small — third-year, maybe — with ink-stained fingers and a school tie knotted too short so the fat end barely reached his sternum. Gavin had seen him in the library before. He always sat at the same table near the Restricted Section rope, always had the same book open — Wilbert Slinkhard's *Defensive Magical Theory*, which was ironic or defiant or both, because the Carrows had pulled it from the syllabus in September. The book was on the floor now, spine cracked, pages bent where it had landed.

The boy screamed again.

At the wand level, Gavin saw what every student in the corridor saw: the Cruciatus Curse, red-orange light from Carrow's wand held steady, a child in agony. The boy's back arched and his heels drummed against the stone floor and his hands clawed at nothing and the sound he made was not really a scream but something past a scream — a high, raw exhalation that went on too long, that kept going, the sound of a body producing noise because noise was all it could do.

At the substrate level, Gavin saw something else entirely.

The Cruciatus was a sentence. One tight construction, built on a substrate permission that let directed magical intent light up a target's pain signals without laying a hand on them. That was all it was. Simpler, in fact, than the Stunning Spell the Gryffindors had been practicing. Translated into the closest English Gavin could manage, the permission was six words long. Six. And nothing about it looked specially guarded. No deeper anchoring. No harder lock. It sat in the same kind of join as Levitation Charms, Colour-Change Charms, the spell holding Barnabas to the wall.

Gavin looked at it and saw where he would edit.

The knowledge arrived without anything that felt like a revelation. He had edited substrate agreements before — the quill, the visibility rules, other small experiments that had cost him nosebleeds and headaches and hours of blurred vision. This was larger, yes, but not stranger. He could see the anchoring points. He could see which one to touch.

He would not have to block it. He would not have to shield the boy. He would only have to spoil the fit between intent and effect. Carrow could slash his wand through the air and mean it all he liked. Nothing would take.

Carrow lifted his wand. The boy crumpled. He lay on the stone floor breathing in fast, shallow gulps, his ink-stained fingers twitching against the flagstones. His book lay beside him, pages fanned open to a chapter on Shield Charms.

"Get up," Carrow said. He was sweating. He always sweated when he used the Unforgivables — Gavin had noticed this before, months ago, the way everyone noticed things about Carrow while trying not to be seen noticing. But now, with the substrate visible, Gavin understood the sweating differently: the physical cost of the Cruciatus was not borne by the caster's magical reserves but by the caster's neural engagement with the substrate agreement. Carrow's body was responding to the act of participating in a pain-grammar, even from the casting side. The sweat had nothing to do with exertion. It was his nervous system registering what it was doing.

Carrow wiped his forehead with his sleeve. He had pushed the sleeves of his robes up, the way he always did, as if punishment were manual labour.

The boy got up. Slowly. One hand on the wall. He picked up his book and held it against his chest with both arms and walked away with the careful steps of someone whose legs had not yet agreed to hold him. Ink on his fingers. Tie too short. A third-year who liked reading about Shield Charms in a school where shields weren't enough.

Gavin watched him go.

The Ravenclaw girls peeled themselves off the wall and hurried away. Marcus Belby exhaled and adjusted his scarf and went wherever he was going. The corridor emptied in seconds — quickly, quietly, everyone pretending they hadn't seen, because seeing meant remembering and remembering meant being a witness and being a witness was dangerous.

Gavin did not move. He kept looking at the place in the substrate where the Cruciatus sat and tracked its anchoring outward. The pattern opened fast.

The Cruciatus and the Killing Curse shared a root.

Different surface effects. Same buried permission: directed magic could reach into another person's body and overrule it. Pain. Control. Death. Three branches off the same join.

And that join was ordinary. Not sealed off. Not hidden behind anything grander than age and habit. It sat in the substrate beside hundreds of other load-bearing pieces the castle and the world relied on.

Avada Kedavra — the spell that had shaped Voldemort's war, that had killed Harry Potter's parents and Cedric Diggory and hundreds of others — rode that same buried piece. So did the Imperius. Gavin could see the cut now. Not a shield, not a counter-curse, not anything as visible as a duel. A change at the root, and every killing curse in the world would hit air.

The corridor was empty now. He checked his watch. He was fourteen minutes late for Ancient Runes. There was dried blood under his left nostril from a nosebleed he hadn't noticed starting, and his left hand was trembling against his thigh.

He wiped his nose on his sleeve, shifted his bag to his other shoulder, and walked to class.

He was thinking about Voldemort's war.

---

Gavin skipped dinner. He'd been skipping dinner more often than not since the break with Miren — not because of the break itself, not exactly, but because eating in the Great Hall meant sitting among people he used to talk to and now didn't, and the energy required for that performance was energy he'd rather spend on the manuscript. He took a bread roll from the kitchens — the Hufflepuff common room was adjacent, the house-elves knew him by now, they didn't ask questions — and ate it dry on the walk to the seventh floor, dropping crumbs on the stairs. The bread was good. He didn't notice.

The sub-collection was cold, as it always was — the dry, mineral cold of deep stone, older than the castle's heating charms, older than the castle itself. The light from the walls was faint and steady, the luminescence that had been there before anyone thought to install torches. The corridor sequence that led here had taken twelve minutes tonight. Sometimes the castle's shifting architecture made it faster, sometimes slower, and Gavin had stopped trying to predict which. He sat on the floor with his back against the shelf that had once held the manuscript and spread the pages across his knees. The stone was cold through his trousers. He'd stopped bringing a cushion weeks ago because it was one more thing to carry and the cold had become part of the routine, like the nosebleeds, like the headaches, like the tremor in his left hand that flared when he was tired.

The notation moved. It always moved — not in the looping, repetitive manner of wizard photographs, but representationally, the symbols shifting to show relationships from different angles, rotating perspectives the way you might turn a glass in your hand to catch new light. Gavin watched the notation and understood it and the understanding was not translation. He was reading the way you read in your own language: the meaning arrived without a processing step, the symbols and the concepts they carried collapsed into a single act of comprehension.

He was working on ward-substrate coupling. The section of the manuscript that described how the planetary wards drew on the local magical substrate to sustain themselves. It was the hardest thing he'd tried to understand — designed not for a fifteen-year-old human brain but for the Builders, for minds that had operated magic as a medium of existence — as fundamental and unconscious as breathing. The specific problem he'd been staring at for three days was a coupling description that his cognition kept sliding off of, like trying to hold the shape of a four-dimensional object in a mind built for three dimensions. He could get two of the variables and the third would dissolve. He could hold the third and lose the relationship between the first two.

But tonight his mind kept returning to the corridor.

He could end the war.

The thought had the quality of standing in a kitchen and realizing you could burn down the house — the capability just there, mundane, available, and the gap between having it and using it a gap of decision, not of power.

He could make lethal magic impossible.

He sat with this. The notation on the manuscript page shifted in front of him, and he let it shift without tracking it, because the ward-coupling problem could wait for the time it took to think this through properly.

The work itself was ugly but legible. He would need to map the full spread of that root — every anchoring point, every dependent structure — and trim away only the part that licensed torture, compulsion, and killing. He could not simply tear the whole thing out. Directed magic had to be able to touch a body; healing charms needed that reach, and Stunning Spells did too. But the vicious part was narrower than the whole. He could feel where it narrowed.

It would take time. He estimated — and the estimate was rough, because he'd never attempted anything at this scale — perhaps three weeks of focused substrate work. Maybe four. The nosebleeds would be bad. The headaches would be worse. But the work was within his capability. He could see the architecture. He could see the path.

Three weeks, and every Killing Curse in the world would fail. Every Cruciatus would produce nothing. Every Imperius would slide off. Voldemort's entire war machine would lose its most terrifying weapons overnight, and neither Voldemort nor any of his Death Eaters would understand why, because the change would be invisible to anyone who couldn't perceive the substrate. They would cast their curses and nothing would happen, and they would try again and nothing would happen, and the confusion would give the other side — the Order, the DA, the Ministry holdouts, whoever was left — time to organize and fight on terms that didn't include the threat of instant death.

He could save hundreds of lives. Probably more.

Gavin wiped blood from his nostril — another nosebleed, the small, slow kind that came from sustained substrate perception even without active editing — and looked at the manuscript pages spread across his knees.

The ward-coupling problem looked back at him.

Three variables, interlocking. The planetary wards drawing on the local magical substrate to sustain themselves, and the draw thinning because the coupling mechanism was degrading, and the degradation accelerating because the wards had been designed for an Earth whose magical substrate was denser than the current one. The Builders had set the wards before they left. In the millennia since, the substrate had thinned — whether from natural decay or from the cumulative demands of generations of wizarding magic or from some process the manuscript described in notation Gavin couldn't yet parse — and the wards were starving. They still held, but the way a campfire holds in light rain: less each hour, the heat dropping, the flames lower, the wood hissing, the core shrinking.

He'd felt the wards from the Astronomy Tower in March. The spatial pressure of their thinning — not visible, but sensible, like a ceiling settling, all creak and give overhead. They were thinner now than they had been then. They would be thinner in May. How much time he had before they reached a critical threshold, he didn't know — the manuscript's notation on failure modes was in a section he hadn't fully decoded yet.

He might have years. He might have months. He might have decades. He didn't know, and the not-knowing was its own pressure, constant and low and impossible to set down.

What he did know was this: if the wards failed, the thing beyond them would not attack. It would not invade. It would not arrive in any sense that implied intention or direction. It would simply be there — a rising water table, indifferent and total — and its presence would be incompatible with magical life. Not through hostility. Through a condition that did not support what was currently living here, the same way a waterlogged field didn't support a dry basement.

Everyone would die. Not just wizards. Everything magical — every charm, every enchantment, every ward, every magical creature, every magical plant, the entire substrate infrastructure that the wizarding world didn't know it existed within and depended upon. The Muggles would probably survive — the condition beyond the wards was incompatible with magical life specifically, not with life broadly — but everything that made the wizarding world possible would cease to function, and the cascading failures would be catastrophic in ways Gavin could model roughly but not predict.

He sat on the cold stone floor of the sub-collection and held these two problems in his mind at the same time.

Three weeks to end the war. Three weeks of ward study lost.

The wards were failing on a timescale he could perceive but couldn't measure precisely. What he could feel — every time he let his perception open to that scale — was that the margin was not generous. The thinning was not leisurely. He didn't have the luxury of solving both problems sequentially, first the war and then the wards, because the wards might not wait.

And there was the other thing: the war was ending. He could feel that too, though not through the substrate — through the castle's ordinary social atmosphere, the rumours that filtered down even to a boy who had stopped participating in the school's communal life. Harry Potter. Something was building. The DA was more active than it had been all year. The Carrows were losing control, reacting with escalating cruelty because escalation was the only tool they had. The regime was cracking.

If the war would end on its own — and it might, it might not, but it was moving toward something — then spending three weeks disabling lethal magic would be spending three weeks on a problem that was already being addressed. Not by him. By other people, braver people, people who had chosen the fight he hadn't chosen.

But the wards were not being addressed by anyone. No one else could perceive them. No one else knew they existed. If Gavin didn't work on them, no one did, and the thinning continued, and the campfire in the rain burned lower, and eventually the fire went out and the cold came in.

The scale made the choice short and horrible.

The war was killing people now. Hundreds, maybe. Ward failure would erase magic altogether: every spell, every creature, every hidden structure the wizarding world lived inside without knowing it. One was a house fire. The other was the reservoir above the house giving way.

Gavin sat with this and waited for the decision to feel like a decision.

It didn't.

The answer did not get kinder for being obvious.

He thought about the boy in the corridor. The ink-stained fingers twitching against the flagstones. The sound he'd made that wasn't a scream. The book pressed to his chest afterward, the one about Shield Charms, and the way he'd walked away on legs that didn't trust themselves yet. That boy would be Cruciated again. Tomorrow, next week, whenever Carrow felt like it. Other students would be Cruciated. Some would be killed, in the battle that was building — Gavin could feel the approaching conflict as a perturbation in the substrate, a pressure-change like weather building, the distortion of large-scale magical forces gathering toward a single point. People he'd sat next to in the Great Hall would die. People who'd passed him in corridors for five years without either of them learning the other's name.

He was going to let it happen, and it wasn't because he didn't care. He sat with the boy's face and the sound the boy had made and the ink on the boy's fingers, and he cared about it, and the caring did not buy him another three weeks.

He picked up the manuscript page he'd been working on. The ward-coupling problem. Three variables, interlocking. He'd been losing the third when he held the first two, for three days now, and the frustration of it had become its own weather, a constant overcast in his working hours. He looked at the notation and let it shift and this time — because his mind was running differently tonight, because the corridor had clarified something about what he could and could not afford to spend his time on — he felt the third variable hold.

Not clearly. Not fully. But he could feel its shape the way you could feel the shape of a word you'd forgotten — it was there, just past where he could close his hand, and if he kept working he might reach it.

He worked.

The sub-collection was silent except for the faint mineral settling of old stone, the sound of a building so deep in the castle that the ordinary noises of school life — footsteps, laughter, doors — didn't reach it. His nose bled intermittently. He wiped it on his sleeve, which was already stained brown in overlapping rings from earlier nosebleeds, a history of evenings spent doing exactly this: sitting on cold stone, reading notation that moved, trying to hold more than his mind was built to hold. He kept reading. The coupling mechanism. The local draw. The wards overhead, invisible to anyone without substrate perception, thinning — slowly, structurally, with the kind of patience that made it easy to pretend it wasn't happening.

Somewhere in the castle above him — in a corridor or a common room or the Great Hall — someone cast a spell. He felt it as a flutter in the substrate, a tiny turn of intent he could now read without effort. A homework charm, probably. Or a hex thrown in anger. Or a curse thrown in cruelty. The substrate registered only that something had taken hold, not whether it belonged in a classroom or a torture chamber.

He could change that. He was choosing not to.

Outside the castle, past the corridors and the grounds and the spring warmth he couldn't feel from down here, the world was green and growing. The Forbidden Forest stirred with things waking after winter. Flowers pushed through cracks between the courtyard flagstones, ordinary and persistent. In the greenhouses, the plants that Professor Sprout tended were doing what plants did in April. In the lake, the giant squid was doing whatever the giant squid did. The world was performing its small, seasonal renewal, as it did every year, oblivious to everything except the sun and the soil and the rain.

And above all of it, visible only to Gavin if he opened his perception to that scale, the wards stretched in vast interlocking patterns across the sky — beautiful, ancient, failing. Thinner than they had been last month. The thing beyond them leaned against the thinning barrier with the patience of geology, the patience of weather, the patience of a condition that didn't know it was waiting because it didn't know anything at all.

Gavin turned a page. The notation shifted, showing the coupling mechanism from a new angle, and something in the relationship between the first two variables caught at the edge of the third, and he leaned forward, his nose bleeding onto the stone floor, and tried to understand how a long-departed civilization had solved a problem he was only beginning to know how to ask.

Students slept in dormitories behind stone walls, inside a castle inside a country inside a world inside a thinning shell of ancient agreements, and none of them knew how thin the shell had become, or that the only person working on it was a fifteen-year-old Hufflepuff with a nosebleed and bad handwriting, sitting on a cold floor, choosing the world over the war.

Over the next few days, the damage in the castle stopped looking temporary. Scorch marks stayed on walls. Broken glass stayed broken. Corridors felt less like school than a place where damage had become ordinary. Gavin noticed that too, and kept working.


# Chapter 16

The fifth-floor corridor had been safe two days ago. Now there was a scorch mark shaped like a handprint on the left wall and the ceiling tiles had been blasted loose above the entrance to what used to be the Muggle Studies classroom, exposing old iron brackets and something that looked like copper pipe except Hogwarts didn't have pipes. Gavin stepped over a pile of broken glass — someone had taken out the display case holding a trophy for the 1987 Inter-House Herbology Competition, though he couldn't tell whether that had been defiance or accident — and kept walking.

The substrate around the scorch mark was still unsettled. He felt it before he saw it: the agreements governing the wall's surface were slightly loose where the spell had landed, the stone's certainty about its own composition frayed at the edges. Two days ago the mark would have drawn him into a closer look, the residual substrate distortion interesting in a clinical way. Now he just noted it and moved on. The castle was full of small wounds like this. Every corridor had them.

Somewhere above him, something heavy fell and something lighter shattered. The sounds reached him through layers of stone — a whump, then a bright spray of breaking glass, then voices he couldn't distinguish. He paused and listened. No screaming. He kept going.

The school had been coming apart for the past week. Not the sudden, dramatic kind of collapse — not a revolution, not a battle — but the slower kind, the kind where each day's damage seemed manageable and then you looked up and realized the structure had shifted. Dumbledore's Army had moved from graffiti and coded resistance to something that was nearly open warfare. He'd passed two seventh-years yesterday casting Shield Charms at each other in the Charms corridor, drilling, and neither had looked at him or tried to hide what they were doing. This morning there was still a fan of diluted blood in the grout where a rebounding hex had clipped a fourth-year above the ear. Someone had thrown dittany on it and left in a hurry, so the corridor smelled sharp and green over the iron smell. A third-year Ravenclaw had hexed Alecto Carrow's classroom door with a Colloportus so layered that she'd had to cancel her afternoon session and wait for Flitwick to undo it. Flitwick had taken two hours. Gavin suspected he could have done it in twenty minutes.

And the Room of Requirement had started swallowing students. That was the word his mind offered: swallowing. They went in and didn't come back. Neville Longbottom had gone first, three or four days ago, and since then a steady trickle had followed him — mostly Gryffindors, some Hufflepuffs, a few Ravenclaws who had calculated that the risk of hiding was now lower than the risk of attending class. They left their beds unmade and their trunks half-packed and their friends covered for them at mealtimes with excuses so transparent they were clearly deliberate, not real cover stories but small acts of defiance in themselves. Gone to the hospital wing. Visiting family. She was feeling ill. The excuses dared the Carrows to call them liars. Increasingly, the Carrows didn't.

That was the part that registered even in the back of Gavin's mind, where the ward-maintenance problem ran like a programme he couldn't quite close. Amycus Carrow had gone quiet. He still patrolled the corridors, still assigned detentions, still stood at the front of the Dark Arts classroom with his sleeves pushed up and sweat darkening the collar of his robes. But the volume had dropped. The commands that used to fill a corridor now came out clipped and uncertain, and the students who heard them no longer flinched the way they had in October or November or February. The fear was still there — Carrow was still dangerous, still armed, still backed by a regime that used the Cruciatus the way other schools used Saturday detention — but it had thinned into something more like wariness. Students kept him in the edge of their vision and gave him room to pass. They didn't drop their eyes anymore.

Twice in the past two days Gavin had seen Carrow standing alone at the end of a corridor, not patrolling, not going anywhere. Just standing. His jaw worked and his hands opened and closed at his sides. He looked like a foreman on a site where the workers had stopped listening, calculating whether to shout louder or walk away, and aware that neither option would help. Gavin had seen his sister Alecto at breakfast this morning, eating quickly with her head down, not scanning the tables the way she usually did. They were both nervous and the nervousness was legible even without substrate perception.

At the substrate level it was something else. The school's magical structure — the layered agreements that held the castle together, the wards within wards that governed everything from the shifting staircases to the enchanted ceiling — was under stress. Not breaking, not failing. Just vibrating slightly, like a window in a strong wind. The amount of hostile magic being cast inside the school had increased, and the agreements that maintained order were absorbing it. Every hexed door and blasted corridor and practice duel left a small residue in the substrate, and the residues were accumulating. Gavin felt the buildup behind his eyes before it tipped over into pain.

The rumour had reached even him. Harry Potter had done something. He'd been seen, or he was coming, or he'd already come. The details shifted by the hour — broke into Gringotts, escaped on a dragon, spotted in Hogsmeade, coming to Hogwarts — but the specifics didn't matter as much as the fact that the student body had started believing them. Something was moving. The long stalled weight of the war had shifted, and the people who had been enduring the regime with their heads down were lifting them.

Gavin turned the corner onto the seventh floor and nearly walked into a group of four students heading the other direction. They were carrying things: a canvas bag that clinked with potion bottles, a folded blanket, what looked like a first-aid kit with Madam Pomfrey's seal on the clasp. The girl in front was a sixth-year Hufflepuff named Elara Stelwick, and she had a burn scar on the back of her left hand, shiny and oval, from November when Carrow had made them practise fire-conjuring on each other. She carried the canvas bag with that hand as if she'd stopped noticing the scar months ago. The boy behind her — tall, thick-set, wearing trainers under his robes instead of proper shoes — was chewing on the string of his hood and staring straight ahead.

Elara glanced at Gavin. He glanced at the bag.

"You didn't see us," she said.

"Yeah, all right."

They passed. He felt the substrate shift as they moved toward the Room of Requirement — the Room's architecture flexing to receive them, its agreement with the castle expanding in a way that operated more like a negotiation than a spell, closer to what the Builders had done than to any wand magic. He'd noticed that weeks ago. It didn't help with the maintenance problem. He let it go.

The corridor sequence to the sub-collection was unchanged. The seventh-floor passage shifted and reconnected itself in its usual not-quite-predictable pattern, and the temperature dropped as he moved deeper into the older stonework. The torches ended. The walls took on their own faint luminescence, mineral-cold and steady. The air went dry and smelled of limestone and nothing else.

The sub-collection was as he'd left it that morning. The manuscript lay open on the stone shelf he'd been using as a desk, held flat by two pieces of quartz he'd taken from the Hufflepuff common room's decorative rock collection. The notation on the exposed pages moved in its slow, representational way — not animated, not alive, but re-presenting, the same relationships showing themselves from different angles the way rotating an object showed different faces. He sat on the floor, pulled his legs under him, and read.

The ward-maintenance section was the worst part of the manuscript. The entity section was more disturbing, the passages describing what existed beyond the wards rendered in notation that taxed his perception until his nose bled and the edges of his vision went grey. The maintenance section was worse in a different way: it was practical, it was necessary, and it was impossible.

The operations it described were not hard in the way a difficult spell was hard, where more power or more precision or more practice could close the gap. They were hard the way arithmetic was hard for a dog. They assumed a cognitive architecture that Gavin did not have. The Builders who'd created the wards had maintained them through acts of unified perception — holding simultaneous awareness of all the variables in a local ward structure and adjusting them together as if closing a hand. All the fingers moved at once because they were part of the same motion.

The specific operation he'd been working on for three days involved a thin place in the ward structure he'd mapped from the Astronomy Tower in March. The repair required simultaneous adjustment of what the notation represented as seventeen interlocking parameters — the permeability coefficients, the coherence thresholds, the phase relationships between adjacent segments. Each parameter depended on the current state of all the others. You couldn't adjust one and then adjust the next because adjusting one changed the context for the next.

He could hold six of the parameters in his awareness at once. On a good day, with rest and quiet and no substrate noise from the school above him, he could sometimes manage seven. The procedure needed seventeen.

It was like being given a recipe that called for an oven he didn't own and couldn't build. The recipe wasn't wrong. He was just the wrong species of cook.

He'd spent three days looking for a workaround and the manuscript offered none, because the manuscript had been written for readers who could do what it described. He was the wrong reader. The wards continued to fail. Nobody else was reading.

He read. The notation shifted under his attention, showing him relationships he could almost follow, connections that resolved when he held his focus at exactly the right angle and dissolved into noise when his concentration slipped. The spring warmth of the upper castle did not reach the sub-collection. The stone under him was the same temperature it had been in January. He sat on the cold floor and failed to understand and read again.

---

He left the sub-collection two hours later because he needed to eat and because the afterimages had started. The notation's representational movement was lingering in his peripheral vision even when he looked away from the manuscript — shapes that shifted and re-presented at the edges of what he could see, as if the notation had imprinted itself on his visual processing and wouldn't let go. He'd learned in January that this meant he'd pushed too far and needed to let his eyes settle. That lesson had cost him a bruised shoulder and a cracked pair of glasses when he'd walked into a door frame because the edges of solid objects had gone slightly uncertain.

The seventh floor was empty. The corridors had the held-breath quality of a building that knew something was coming — not the ordinary between-classes quiet of students moving purposefully, but the taut silence of a space that had been cleared. Outside the windows, the grounds were absurd with spring. The lawns had turned the bright, almost edible green of early May, and there were flowers in the courtyard below — daffodils, mostly, and something purple — and the late-afternoon light came in at the angle that made the Scottish Highlands look painted. A warm day. Birdsong from the forest edge. Completely wrong, given everything.

He took the back staircase down toward the kitchens. Fourth floor, third floor, the connecting passage behind the tapestry of Barnabas the Barmy — the shortest route if you wanted to avoid the main corridors and the remaining Carrow patrols. He was thinking about the maintenance problem. Specifically, he was wondering whether the seventeen-parameter operation could be decomposed into a sequence of smaller operations, each involving six or fewer parameters, performed in order rather than simultaneously. It would be like doing long division instead of seeing the answer whole — slower, uglier, but possible for a mind that couldn't hold the full picture. The question was whether the sequential approach would preserve the interdependencies or destroy them. He thought it might destroy them. He was thinking about this when he heard the kid.

A small sound. Not crying — the caught, pressed-down sound of someone trying to be quiet because being loud was dangerous this year.

He stopped. The passage behind the tapestry was narrow and lit only by the glow that seeped through the tapestry's weave. It took him a moment to find the source: a boy sitting on the floor against the left wall, knees drawn up, cradling his right arm against his chest. A first-year. Gavin could tell by the size of him — small, sharp-faced, not yet used to his own proportions. Ravenclaw tie, the blue barely visible in the dim passage. His robes were too big for him, bought with room to grow or handed down from someone larger, and his shoes were scuffed pale at the toes in the way that meant he kicked things when he walked, a habit he probably wasn't aware of. His right wrist was bent at an angle that wrists did not go.

Gavin crouched. "Hey. Can I see?"

The boy looked up. His face was white and his eyes were wet but he wasn't crying, which was either courage or shock. In his left hand — the good hand — he was holding a folded piece of parchment, tight enough to crease it.

"I fell," the boy said. His voice was thin and careful. "The stairs moved. I wasn't — I was going to send a letter and the stairs just—"

"Yeah. They do that. Let me see your wrist."

The boy held it out. The break was clean — Gavin could feel it before he touched the skin, the way he felt everything now, the double image of the physical and the structural. The radius was fractured at a specific point. At the substrate level, the bone's agreements about its own integrity had been interrupted: the rules that said this bone is whole, continuous, load-bearing had a local exception at the point of impact, and that exception was generating pain signals that the boy's nervous system was translating into the white face and the wet eyes and the bitten lip.

Gavin reached for the break.

What happened next took less than a second.

His mind went to the substrate. Not to the Episkey he'd used before on scraped knees and small cuts — not to the spell, not to his wand — but to the agreement that defined the bone's structural state. The fracture was an exception to the bone's default rules. Exceptions could be revoked. His attention closed around the point of the break like a hand closing around a stone and he began to edit: not healing the bone but making the fracture less structurally valid, reducing the exception's claim on reality, so that the bone's default state — whole, continuous, bearing weight — simply reasserted itself the way water filled a hole.

He hadn't decided to do this. He hadn't reached for his wand and then changed his mind. He hadn't even formed the intention to use the First Art. His perception had seen the break and his mind had gone to the substrate the way another person's hand might have gone to their wand — automatically, below the level of decision, with the same blind speed as catching a falling glass.

The boy gasped.

Gavin stopped.

He'd been doing it for perhaps half a second. The bone was already partly corrected. Not healed — corrected. The distinction mattered enormously and was invisible to anyone who couldn't perceive the substrate. Magical healing knit tissue: it told the body's existing processes to work faster, reconnecting what had been separated, building a callus, leaving a remodelling scar that Madam Pomfrey could read like a signature. What Gavin had been doing was different. He'd been removing the fracture's validity — making the break less real, so the bone's wholeness simply reasserted. No knitting. No callus. No scar. The bone wouldn't have been healed; it would have been a bone that had never been broken, in a body that had broken it three minutes ago.

Madam Pomfrey would notice. If she examined the wrist, she would notice. A bone with no history of fracture in a child who had just fractured it — that gap would be visible to any competent healer.

He pulled back from the substrate and drew his wand.

"Episkey."

The spell was rough. His wand work had been deteriorating all year and the Episkey landed with too much force — the boy flinched, and the bone knit with an audible crack that made them both wince. But it knit. Magically. Tissue reconnecting, callus forming, the swelling beginning to subside. The mundane, explainable kind of healing that left the right residue.

The boy's face went from white to a raw, blotchy pink as the pain dropped.

"Better?" Gavin said.

The boy flexed his fingers carefully. "Yeah. Yeah, it's — ow. But yeah." He looked at Gavin's wand, then at Gavin, with the expression of someone trying to decide whether to trust what had just happened. "Thanks."

"Don't put too much weight on it tonight. If it still hurts tomorrow, go see Pomfrey."

"She's been — they say she's really busy. With the Room people and everything." The boy tucked his mended wrist against his chest and got up, still clutching the folded parchment in his good hand. His too-big robes dragged on the stone floor. "Thanks," he said again, and went, his scuffed shoes receding down the passage until the sound faded and the held-breath quiet of the castle settled back in.

Gavin stayed where he was. Crouching in the narrow passage behind the tapestry, one knee on the cold floor, his wand still out.

He hadn't decided. That was the thing. His conscious practice of the First Art — the deliberate, effortful sessions in the sub-collection where he tried to hold six variables and failed at the seventh — was clumsy, approximate, exhausting. The gap between intention and execution was wide enough to make him feel stupid on a daily basis. But the reflexive edit he'd just done on the boy's wrist had been precise. Clean. Faster and more elegant than anything he'd managed in deliberate practice. His unconscious mind was better at this than his conscious mind, which meant the First Art had settled into whatever layer of cognition handled the things you did without deciding to do them — catching, flinching, breathing, the operations that ran beneath the surface of thought.

He thought about what would have happened if he hadn't caught it. The boy would have walked away with a perfect wrist. A wrist that had been corrected at the level of reality rather than healed at the level of magic. Nobody would have known, probably. Nobody would have looked closely at a first-year's mended wrist. But probably was the problem. If someone had looked — if Pomfrey had examined it, if anyone with training had checked — the absence of healing would have been conspicuous. A bone with no history of injury. An impossible clean gap where the evidence of fracture should have been.

And the next question, the one they wouldn't know to ask but that Gavin couldn't stop asking himself: what did it mean for a living body to be edited at the substrate level? Healing worked with the body's own processes. Substrate correction worked around them. The bone's biology hadn't healed; the bone's rules had been rewritten. Was there a cost to that? A side effect? The manuscript didn't say, because the manuscript didn't contemplate its techniques being used on beings whose bodies relied on fixed biological rules. The Builders hadn't had bodies in the sense that mattered. They wouldn't have needed to ask.

He stood up. His left hand was doing the intermittent tremor — the Cruciatus remnant from November that came and went, came and went, tied to stress or fatigue or both. He pressed it against his thigh until it settled. Then he put his wand in his pocket and went to the kitchens.

The house-elves gave him bread and cold chicken and a mug of tea that was too hot to drink. He sat at the long table in the kitchen's warmth and ate without tasting. The kitchen smelled of roasting meat and baking bread and something sweet — a pudding being assembled for a dinner that fewer students attended each day. Two house-elves were arguing near the ovens about the quantity of potatoes, a dispute conducted in high, rapid voices and resolved by a third elf who intervened with a serving spoon and a pointed look. Gavin ate his bread and drank his tea when it had cooled enough and thought about the substrate edit and the boy's wrist and the fact that his reflexes had outstripped his conscious ability, and what that meant for the work, and whether it was a sign of progress or something else.

---

He went back to the sub-collection after dark.

The route was harder at night. Not because of Filch — even Filch seemed to have lost his stomach for catching students when the students who got caught ended up in Carrow's detentions, and the detentions had become the kind of thing that Filch, for all his petty cruelty, had not signed on for. But the Carrows still patrolled, and their patrols had become erratic in the past few days. Amycus had been spotted on the first floor at midnight and the seventh floor twenty minutes later, which either meant the castle was moving him or he was moving himself in the panicked, purposeless way of a man who'd been told something was coming and couldn't decide what to do about it. Gavin thought it was the latter. The substrate around Carrow had a specific quality these days — a tight, contracted feel, like the magic in his vicinity was pulling inward, bracing.

He reached the sub-collection without meeting anyone. The entrance — not a door, just a place where the corridor ended and older stone began — was as he'd left it. The air was dry and cool and smelled of mineral dust. The faint luminescence of the walls was steady, unchanged by anything that happened in the castle above. The manuscript lay open on its stone shelf, the quartz weights holding the pages, the notation moving in its slow patient loop.

He sat. He read.

The seventeen-parameter problem. He tried the sequential-decomposition approach he'd been considering on the way to the kitchens: take the full operation and break it into sub-operations of six or fewer parameters each, performed in sequence rather than simultaneously. It was a trade — elegance for accessibility, parallel processing for serial. Like looking at a painting through a keyhole, one section at a time, and hoping the sections would add up to the whole.

He got through the first two sub-operations. The notation cooperated, or seemed to — the adjustments to the first six parameters took hold, and when he moved to the second set, those adjustments seemed to hold as well. For a brief, reckless moment he thought it might work.

The third sub-operation showed him why it wouldn't. The parameters in the third set depended on the simultaneous state of the parameters in the first and second sets, and by performing them sequentially he'd disrupted those states — the first set had drifted while he worked on the second, and the second was already shifting in response to the drift in the first, and the whole structure was now less stable than before he'd started. He'd made it worse. Not much worse. A fraction. But the thin place was thinner by a margin he could perceive, and the margin was his fault.

He sat back. Breathed. The cold of the sub-collection floor had settled into his lower back and ankles where his robes didn't cover. He pulled his robe tighter and looked at the notation and understood, again, the shape of the problem.

The Builders had maintained these wards as a single continuous act of perception and adjustment. The operation was singular. The seventeen parameters were not seventeen separate things being tracked but seventeen aspects of one thing, the way the fingers of a closing hand were not five separate motions but one motion distributed across five digits. To decompose the operation into pieces was to miss the point in the most fundamental way possible — like trying to understand a chord by playing the notes one at a time. Each note was correct. The chord was gone.

He needed to hold seventeen variables at once. His mind held six. The gap was not something practice could close. It was architectural — a limitation of human cognitive hardware, the same way a human eye couldn't see infrared. Not a failure of effort but a fact about what he was.

He went back to the notation. There had to be another way. The Builders had left the manuscript as a teaching tool; they'd anticipated that someone would need to learn this. Maybe not a human someone — maybe they'd anticipated another Builder, or a being with the right cognitive architecture, returning to perform the maintenance. But the manuscript was addressed to a reader, and Gavin was the only reader it had, and the wards were failing at a rate that was constant and patient and would not wait for a better student.

He found a passage he'd translated weeks ago and re-read it. The notation described the maintenance operations as something the manuscript called — in Gavin's rough, imperfect translation — a single shape made of attention. Not a sequence. Not a checklist. A shape. The way a sphere was not a stack of circles but a single geometry.

He didn't know how to make a shape out of attention. But the phrase nagged at him. Shape. Not effort, not force, not knowledge. Shape. He turned the concept over while the cold of the stone seeped up through his robes.

Then something changed.

It was subtle at first. A faint wrongness in the substrate — not wrongness, exactly, but a shift that set his teeth on edge. The substrate flexed. Not here, not in the sub-collection's small stone rooms, but broadly: a large-scale perturbation pressing against the edges of his perception.

He set the maintenance problem aside and attended.

People. A lot of people. Moving toward Hogwarts with intention — not the ambient, everyday intention of students going about their routines, but concentrated, directed magical will, the kind that came from people gathering for a specific purpose. The substrate registered the convergence as pressure and vibration at once. A single person casting a spell was a tap. What Gavin was feeling was the buzz of a crowd before anyone spoke, the vibration of many instruments being tuned at once. He couldn't tell who they were or what they wanted, not from substrate perception alone. But the scale was large. Dozens of people. More. Moving with purpose.

He waited. The perturbation grew.

Over the next hour it intensified from a background pressure to a steady, unmistakable distortion. More people were arriving. More magical will was concentrating. The ward structures overhead — the vast interlocking patterns that he perceived as something between architecture and music, beautiful and degrading at their margins — began to resonate with the buildup below them. Dust sifted from the ceiling joints and settled across the manuscript in a pale grit. The quartz weights clicked against the stone shelf. Somewhere in the outer room an old seam gave a sharp crack.

Then feet hammered past the entrance.

Gavin got up and stepped to the threshold. Three students came through the old corridor at a half-run, lit by the wall-glow in brief jerks. The first was a seventh-year Ravenclaw Gavin knew by sight, wand out, soot black on one cheek. Behind him two girls were carrying a younger boy between them. The boy's left sleeve had burned away from wrist to elbow. The skin underneath was wet and pink and starting to blister, and every time his heel struck stone he made a short animal noise through his teeth. One girl's hands were wet to the wrists; when she hit the wall rounding the corner she left a red print there.

"The back stair's gone," the Ravenclaw said, not really to Gavin, more to the corridor itself. "Other way. Keep moving."

They were already past him. Burnt wool and dittany and blood hung in the air after them.

Gavin went to the turn where his route back normally opened. The passage had shifted while he'd been reading. Where the narrow descending run should have been there was seamless limestone and a spill of fresh mortar powder across the floor. The way he'd used all year to get back to the kitchens and Hufflepuff was gone.

He stood with dust on his hands and felt the school above him filling. The conflict itself wasn't the problem. He had made his assessment of the war months ago and nothing in the intervening weeks had changed it. The Voldemort war was a human-scale event in a human-scale conflict, and the worst outcome — the regime permanent, the persecution institutional, the Ministry a puppet of a man who called himself Lord and believed blood determined worth — was terrible at the scale of human suffering and irrelevant at the scale of what Gavin was working on. The wards did not care who sat in the Minister's chair. The thing beyond the wards, patient and incompatible and as indifferent to wizarding politics as weather was to the arguments of ants, would continue to press against the thinning barriers regardless of which faction won the argument above.

But the substrate noise of a major magical battle — the distortion caused by hundreds of people casting simultaneously, the sheer volume of agreements being invoked and violated and broken — would make the ward-maintenance work impossible. The delicate perceptual tracking he needed, the ability to hold his six parameters in simultaneous awareness and search for the shape that might let him hold more, required a quiet substrate. A surgeon needed a still table. What was coming would be an earthquake. And now the castle had eaten his way out on top of it.

He would lose hours at best, maybe days, while the corridors reconfigured or the fighting cleared. The wards did not have days. The thin places he'd mapped in March were thinner now. The degradation was constant and patient. Each day that passed without maintenance was a day the margins eroded a little further, and the margins were where the wards would eventually fail, and the failure would be quiet and total and would not announce itself to anyone except the boy standing in an old corridor with mortar dust on his hands.

He went back to the manuscript. The pages were dusted white. The notation's representational movement was jittering under the steady vibration from the gathering war, and the red handprint in the outer corridor kept dragging at the edge of his attention. He brushed grit off the lower half of the page and narrowed his focus. Four parameters. Five. A brief flash of six before the substrate vibrated and he lost the fifth.

More feet passed at irregular intervals. Once he heard someone say, "Hold that there," followed by the wet cough of somebody trying not to choke. Once the old stone overhead rang hard enough to sting his teeth. The weight of the wizarding world's unresolved conflict was concentrating on this castle, tonight, and the building hummed with it.

The notation trembled. He lost his fifth parameter again.

He took the manuscript, the quartz weights, and his notes into the innermost room and set them on the stone table there. It put more wall between him and the blocked corridor, though not enough to shut the battle out. The floor still answered distant impacts. Dust still shook loose from the ceiling joints in fine pale threads.

He sat in the dry limestone cold of the innermost room, the manuscript open before him on the stone table, the quartz weights ticking whenever the floor answered the noise above. The wards overhead were thin and thinning. The thing beyond them continued in its way, which was not malicious, which was not intentional, which was simply incompatible with everything that existed below the wards in the way too much load was incompatible with a beam already splitting along the grain — not through will but through the dull fact of pressure. His usual route out was gone. Smoke and blood smell had reached even here. The notation jittered on the page.

Gavin Tench, fifteen years old, mediocre at everything except the one thing that mattered and not good enough at that, sat at the stone table with the blocked corridor somewhere beyond two turns of old limestone and tried to hold six variables in his mind while the world above him prepared to tear itself apart over a problem he could solve in seconds and wouldn't, because the wards were failing, and the wards were all that mattered, and the wards did not care about the war.

He brushed dust from the margin and turned a page. The notation moved. He read.


# Chapter 17

The coupling pattern was almost visible. Gavin had abandoned the limestone floor for the stone table in the innermost room, farther from the blocked outer corridor, and he'd been staring at the same three lines of notation for the better part of an hour, the manuscript's representational shifts moving in slow cycles that his translation framework could only approximate, and he thought he was beginning to see it — not understand it, not yet, but see it. The ward-maintenance cycle and the substrate's ground state shared something. A resonance. A harmonic. The Builders' notation for this was dense and layered, showing relationships that collapsed when he tried to render them in the runic shorthand Babbling had given him, but the shape of the thing was there. The wards reached into the substrate's base grammar and synchronized with it, and when that synchronization held, the wards regenerated. Or reinforced. Or something the Builders had a glyph for that mapped onto no English verb he knew.

He was reaching for his quill to make a note when the substrate screamed.

Not screamed — there was no sound. But the perceptual channel the First Art had opened in his cognition, the one that let him feel the substrate the way a normal person felt temperature, convulsed with input. Hundreds of spells, all at once. Combat magic. The specific churn of offensive and defensive castings meeting, rebounding, shattering, recasting. It was like trying to read while someone held a lamp an inch from his face — not painful, exactly, but obliterating.

He gripped the edge of the stone table. The stone was cold under his fingers — it was always cold down here, the sub-collection's deep limestone chill that never warmed regardless of the season — and he held on to the solidity of it while his substrate perception convulsed. The delicate coupling pattern he'd been studying drowned in the noise. He couldn't distinguish the ward-maintenance notation from the cacophony of war magic flooding the layer beneath everything. It was like trying to hear a single voice in a stadium. The voice was still there — the coupling was real, the resonance was real — but it was buried under a wall of signal he couldn't filter.

The battle. The one everyone had been expecting. Harry Potter and whatever the rest of it was — the Order, the DA, the resistance movement Miren had been feeding supplies into for months. He'd felt the preliminary disturbances earlier in the evening: the castle's defensive enchantments engaging, the massive protections someone — McGonagall, probably — had raised around the school grounds. Those had registered as a distant hum in the substrate, tolerable, the kind of ambient interference he'd learned to work through. This was different. This was hundreds of people trying to kill each other simultaneously, and every spell was a substrate event, and the aggregate was a white-out in the perceptual channel he couldn't close.

He waited. Maybe the fighting would settle into a pattern, something rhythmic he could filter the way you learned to read in a room with a ticking clock. It didn't. Combat magic was chaotic by nature — no two spells at the same interval, no regularity to the destruction, just raw concurrent noise.

A minute passed. Two. The noise thickened. Someone in the castle above him was casting Fiendfyre — he could feel it as a specific kind of substrate agony, the cursed fire's magical feedback loop generating a signal that escalated exponentially, a note that drowned out everything around it the way a car alarm drowns out conversation. He flinched. The coupling pattern was gone. The fragile connection he'd been tracing between the ward-maintenance cycle and the substrate's ground state had dissolved like ink in moving water, and he'd need to start over, and he couldn't start over while the substrate was screaming.

"Shit," he said, to the manuscript, to the luminous walls, to the cold mineral air of the sub-collection's innermost room.

He sat for another five minutes, jaw tight, the tremor in his left hand doing its familiar flutter against the table edge. The Fiendfyre subsided — someone had put it out or it had consumed what it was fed — but the general combat noise only worsened. More spells, more casters, more disruption. The pattern he'd been studying was irretrievable. He'd been weeks getting close to it — weeks of twelve-hour nights in this room, translating the same dense passages, trying to hold the shape of the coupling mechanism in his mind long enough to see its structure. And now the thread was gone, scattered by the noise of a war he wasn't in, and the irritation he felt was so sharp it tasted like metal in the back of his throat. He knew, in the distant way you know things about yourself that you can't do anything about, that the irritation was the wrong reaction. That the right reaction would be fear, or urgency, or the basic survival instinct that sent those three third-years running for the Room of Requirement. But the wards were failing and the coupling mechanism was the key and the war had just cost him weeks of work, and what he felt was angry.

He packed the manuscript pages into his bag one by one, careful with them, the way he was always careful with them. The bag was a school satchel with an ink stain on the front flap from third year. He slung it over his shoulder, pushed his chair back from the stone table, and walked through the three outer rooms of the sub-collection — the shelves carved into ancient stone, the self-lit walls, the dry cold that smelled like limestone — and, where the blockage in the outer corridor had shifted enough to leave a narrow way through, stepped into the seventh-floor corridor.

***

Sound arrived first. The real kind, the kind that worked on eardrums. Explosions from somewhere below — deep concussive impacts that he felt through the floor. A structural groaning from the castle walls, stone stressed past its tolerance. Voices: screaming, shouting spells, a high frantic wailing that could have been a student or the castle's own wards protesting the damage being done to them.

The corridor floor was covered in grit and stone dust. A section of ceiling had come down thirty feet from where he stood, rubble piled waist-high and a jagged hole punched through to the floor above. Through the hole he could see flashes of red and green light. Spellfire.

He stepped over the rubble. A suit of armor had toppled against the wall, its halberd lying across the corridor like a barricade. He edged around it. The suit's visor had been blown open, and the interior was scorched.

He needed to go down. The fighting was concentrated on the upper floors and the grounds, judging by the substrate distortion — the signal was strongest above him and to the north, where the main entrance opened onto the grounds and the lakeshore. If he went down and south, toward the third or second floor, the classrooms on the quieter side of the castle, he might find enough distance from the combat to work. The substrate noise wouldn't vanish, but it might thin to something he could think through.

He took the staircase at the end of the corridor. One entire landing was missing — blasted away, the stone steps dangling over open air, a fifteen-foot drop to the floor below where he could see more rubble and what looked like a fallen torch bracket still smoldering. He gripped the iron railing and climbed around the gap where the landing had been, his bag swinging, his wet shoes slipping on broken stone, his fingers white on the rail. He kept going down.

On the sixth floor, three students ran past him. Two girls and a boy, younger than him — third-years, maybe — heading for the staircase that led up to the Room of Requirement. One of the girls was carrying a tabby cat pressed against her chest with both arms, the cat's mouth open and panting, its claws sunk into her robes. She had tear-tracks cutting through the dust on her face but she wasn't crying now. She was just running. None of them looked at Gavin. He stepped to the side to let them pass and continued down.

On the fifth-floor landing, a window had blown in. Night air came through, cool and smelling of smoke and wet earth and the green scent of May. Past the broken glass he could see the grounds — not the gentle darkness of an ordinary night but the violent flickering of combat. The green-white flash of Killing Curses in the distance. The orange glow of something burning near the Quidditch pitch. Figures moving on the lawn, small at this height, casting and falling and casting again. The sound was closer here: the percussive snap of dueling magic, someone yelling a name he didn't catch.

He turned the corner into the fifth-floor corridor and stopped. Two people were fighting ahead of him. A student — an older boy Gavin didn't know, Ravenclaw by his tie, backed against a stone column — was casting Shield Charms that kept splintering on impact. The Death Eater facing him was a broad-shouldered woman with her hair tied up in a tight knot. She had a wedding ring on her wand hand, and her robes had been altered — hemmed shorter at the ankle, practically, as if she'd expected to be on her feet tonight and had prepared for it. She cast with the unhurried regularity of someone hammering a nail, each spell hitting the boy's shield and breaking a little more of it.

The duel blocked the direct route. There was a side passage twenty feet back that would take him around to the next staircase.

Gavin turned and took the side passage. Behind him, the Ravenclaw's shield broke — he heard it go, the specific brittle sound of a Protego failing under sustained force — and then a spell connected, and then the corridor was quiet. He didn't look back. The side passage was narrow and cold and empty, and he followed it to the fourth-floor staircase.

On the fourth floor, the fighting was sparser. A portrait had been burned through, the canvas curled and blackened, the frame still on the wall, the subject gone. The neighboring portraits were empty too — fled to frames in safer parts of the castle, or just fled. Water was running from somewhere — a burst pipe, maybe, or a spell that had hit plumbing — pooling along the flagstones in a thin sheet that reflected the torchlight in wavering orange. He stepped through it. His shoes soaked immediately, the cold spreading through the leather. There was glass on the floor too, from a shattered window or a broken display case, and he heard it crunch under his steps and adjusted his path without thinking about it — the automatic navigation of an obstacle, the way you'd step around a puddle on a pavement.

He was crossing the fourth-floor landing, heading for the stairs down, when the curse came.

A corridor opened to his right. He'd been passing it without looking — his attention was mostly on the substrate distortion and whether the signal was weakening as he descended (it was, slightly, which supported the idea that the castle's enchantment architecture acted as a propagation medium with distance-dependent attenuation). A man came out of the corridor at a run, a thin man in Death Eater robes that were too large for him, the sleeves hanging past his knuckles so that only his fingers and his wand were visible. He had blood on his face — his own or someone else's, there was no way to tell — and he was casting at everything in front of him, panicked, not aiming. His wand moved in sloppy arcs and spells flew from it in a scatter.

One of the spells came at Gavin. A Stunner, probably, or possibly something worse — the wand movement was imprecise enough that the caster himself might not have known what he was throwing.

Gavin was thinking about the castle's enchantment architecture as a substrate waveguide. If the castle's own permanent enchantments created a structured medium for substrate propagation, then the distortion from combat magic wouldn't follow a simple falloff with distance — it would follow the castle's magical geometry, channeling along corridors where the enchantments were densest, pooling in junctions, and possibly creating dead zones where the architecture cancelled the noise out. The thought was half-formed, more geometry than language. The curse came at him from the right, a red-white streak in his peripheral vision.

The reflex was faster than thought. He didn't decide to do anything. The substrate around the incoming spell — the specific grammatical structure that allowed magical force to propagate along that trajectory with that intent — became less valid. It didn't meet a shield or a counterspell. The agreement that made the curse a curse relaxed, the way a knot loosens in warm water. Between one foot of air and the next, the spell lost coherence. It wasn't deflected or absorbed. It simply stopped being a spell. The light went out of it and there was nothing there.

Gavin turned the corner and continued toward the staircase, still working through the waveguide idea. If the castle created dead zones, and if he could sense where they were, he might find a workspace with genuine substrate quiet even during a battle. The key would be finding a room at the intersection of —

Behind him, in the corridor he'd just left, the Death Eater was standing still. Gavin was already gone, already thinking about something else, and the man's confusion — the staring at his own wand, the checking whether the wand still worked, the baffled look at the empty space where his spell had been heading — belonged to a story Gavin was not in.

He found the classroom on the third floor by trying doors. The first two were locked. The third opened onto a room that had been used for Arithmancy — desks shoved against the walls, a blackboard still covered in chalk equations in a small precise hand, dust on every surface. The windows faced south, away from the heaviest fighting. Through the glass, the grounds were dark and quiet on this side of the castle. Inside, the substrate noise was still there — a constant grind at the edge of perception, like the hum of electrical equipment in a room you've been in too long — but it was manageable. He could think through it.

He pulled a chair to the center of the room, set his bag on the nearest desk, took out the manuscript pages, and arranged them in the order he'd been working from. The desk was wooden and scratched and had someone's initials carved into the corner — R.H., in careful letters, the work of a student who'd been bored in Arithmancy. He sat down. The chair creaked under him. The manuscript pages lay under his hands, their not-quite-parchment surface faintly warm the way it always was, as though the material retained heat from a source that had nothing to do with the room's temperature.

He found the section on the ward-maintenance coupling. He picked up his quill. Outside, a long way off, something exploded and the windows rattled in their frames.

He worked.

***

He didn't know how long it had been when the door opened.

The substrate noise had shifted several times — peaks and troughs as the fighting concentrated and dispersed across the castle and grounds, weather systems of violence that he tracked with the peripheral attention he'd learned to keep on the substrate while the rest of his mind worked. The coupling pattern was coming back. With sustained concentration and the relative quiet of this room, he'd managed to relocate the section of notation that described the relationship between the ward-maintenance cycle and what he'd been thinking of as the substrate's idle state — the base condition of the magical grammar when no active spells were distorting it. The coupling was there. He could see the shape of the self-repair mechanism, could almost read the Builders' notation for how the wards and the substrate harmonized, and if he could hold the thread for another —

The door opened.

Miren stood in the doorframe.

She looked like the battle. Stone dust in her hair, her plait half undone, loose strands stuck to the sweat on her neck. A cut above her left eyebrow, deeper than a graze, still bleeding sluggishly — a line of blood had run into her eye and she'd wiped it away, smearing red across her temple. Her robes were torn at the right shoulder, showing the shirt underneath. A burn on her left forearm, the skin red and blistering in a stripe that ran from wrist to elbow, the kind of wound that came from a near-miss with something incendiary. She was gripping her wand in her right hand, her knuckles bloodless around it. She was breathing through her mouth — fast, shallow, the breathing of someone who had been running and fighting and couldn't stop either one.

She looked at Gavin. Sitting at a desk. The manuscript open. His quill in his hand. His bag on the floor.

Neither of them spoke for a moment. The muffled sounds of the battle came through the walls and floor, distant, like noise from another building.

"People are dying," Miren said.

"I know."

"Gavin." She took a breath. "People I — kids from our year. Ernie Macmillan's group got pinned down near the greenhouses. There's a kid — Colin something, fourth year, Gryffindor — he's been running messages between the barricades and he's tiny, he's going to get himself killed. There are Death Eaters inside the castle. In the corridors."

"I know."

"Then what the fuck are you doing."

It wasn't really a question. Her voice came out clipped and raw, worn thin by however many hours she'd spent out there. She wasn't trying to sound outraged. She looked too tired for that. She looked like she'd reached the point where this was simply what came out when she found her best friend sitting at a desk during a war.

"I'm working on the wards," he said. "The coupling between—"

"The wards." She said it flat. Not mocking. Flat was worse than mocking. "Right."

"Miren—"

"No. Don't." She wiped her face with the back of her wand hand, smearing blood from the cut across her cheekbone. She didn't seem to register doing it. "I thought you might be hurt. When I couldn't find you in the Great Hall, or at the barricades, or anywhere people were actually—" She gestured with her wand. At the walls. At the battle beyond them. "I went looking. Because I was worried."

He didn't answer. His left hand was trembling on the desk — the old Cruciatus tremor, which surfaced when his body was under stress even when his mind wasn't.

"And you're here." She looked at the manuscript spread across the desk. At his quill. At the notes he'd been making on a scrap of parchment in his ugly hybrid notation. "Doing this."

"It's not—"

"I know it's not homework, Gavin, I'm not—" She stopped. Pressed her lips together hard enough that they whitened. When she spoke again her voice was quieter. "Can you help?"

"With the wards, I think the coupling mechanism—"

"Not with the wards. Right now. The battle. Can you help with the battle?"

He looked at her. She had a smudge of ash on the side of her neck and that flat brightness in her eyes he'd learned to read as fear she was holding down by force. He'd known her since they were eleven. Usually he could tell what that look meant. This time he only knew she wasn't leaving without an answer.

"Yeah," he said. "I could."

Something shifted in her expression. "What do you mean, you could?"

He put the quill down. His left hand was still trembling and he moved it to his lap.

"The combat magic. All of it — the Killing Curse, the Cruciatus, the combat spells. At the substrate level, they're agreements. Rules that say this specific configuration of intent and wand movement and magic produces this specific effect. And I can..."
