He paused, not for drama but because the next part always sounded wrong in ordinary language. "I can edit those agreements. Make them less valid. Not block the spells — make it so the grammar that produces them doesn't work anymore. Like changing the rules of a language so that certain sentences can't be formed."

She stared at him. The cut over her eyebrow dripped. A drop of blood fell past her eyelid and she blinked it away.

"How long would that take?" she said.

"Twenty minutes. Maybe less. The lethal stuff — the Killing Curse — it's actually simpler at the substrate level than most combat magic, it's a very clean agreement, it would be easier to—" He stopped. Her face told him the technical specifics were not what she wanted. "Yeah. I could do it."

"Then do it."

He'd known she would say it. He'd known since she walked through the door — maybe since the night months ago when he'd sat in the sub-collection and understood both what the First Art could do to the war and what the ward failure would do to everything, and understood that the two things could not both be his problem.

"I can't," he said. And then, because that was wrong and Miren could always tell when he was being imprecise: "I won't."

"You won't."

"If the wards fail, Miren — I've told you this. If the wards fail, everyone dies. Not the people in this castle. Everyone."

"Everyone's dying now."

"No. Some people are dying now." He rubbed his face. His fingers smelled like the manuscript — that faint mineral warmth that never entirely washed off. "If I stop working on the wards to fix the battle, the battle gets fixed and the wards keep failing. And the wards..."

He looked for a word that wouldn't sound monstrous and couldn't find one.

"The wards what?" she said.

"The battle is small," he said. "Compared to the other thing. That's — sorry, that's a terrible way to put it. But the battle is small."

Miren didn't respond. She stood in the doorway with blood drying at her temple, looking at him as if trying to fit what he'd just said to the boy she'd known since first year and not getting the pieces to line up. A fifteen-year-old boy sitting at a desk telling a girl with blood on her face that the war she'd been fighting in was small.

"People I know are dying," she said. "People you know."

"I know."

"Duncan's out there."

The name landed. Not in the substrate — in the old human place where names still had weight. Duncan. Thin, careful Duncan who ate slowly and laughed at bad puns and who had been systematically brutalized for ten months because of who his parents were.

"I know," Gavin said.

"And you won't help."

"If the wards collapse, Duncan dies anyway. Everyone does. And I'm the only person who's working on it. There isn't anyone else who can even see the wards. Nobody else knows they exist. If I stop—" He was aware his voice had gone flat, the way it did under pressure, and he couldn't do anything about it. "There isn't anyone else."

"There might not be anyone left by morning."

"There won't be anyone left at all if the wards fail."

He heard himself. He knew how it sounded. He knew because he'd heard it before, inside his own head, across months of nights in the sub-collection while the school collapsed around him. The argument was rational and it sounded insane and both of those things were true.

The battle sounds filtered through the walls. Somewhere above them, something collapsed — a floor or a ceiling giving way, the deep groan of old stone breaking, the crash and rattle of debris settling.

Miren was quiet for a long time. When she spoke, her voice was different.

"You really believe this," she said. Not a question.

"Yeah."

"That the whole world's going to end."

"Not end. The wards fail, and what's outside them... it doesn't attack. It's just there. And if the wards aren't there, it and us are in the same space. And that's—" He'd tried to explain this before and it had never worked and it wasn't going to work now. "It'd be like trying to live underwater. Not because the water's trying to kill you. Just because you can't breathe it."

Miren looked at him. He looked back. She was still holding her wand in her white-knuckled grip. The burn on her forearm was blistering in the classroom's dusty light. She needed Dittany. She needed whoever was doing healing work down in the Great Hall to clean that cut and dress the burn and she'd been up here, looking for him, instead.

The expression on her face was something he'd never seen before. Not anger — he'd seen her angry, seen the sharp questions and the clipped sentences and the way she held herself still when she was furious. This wasn't that. Her mouth had gone still. She looked from his face to the manuscript and back again, and Gavin had the sick sense that she was finally fitting those pieces together into a shape he didn't have another name for. He wasn't a villain, or a coward, or checked out. He was something else. A boy whose frame of reference had expanded past the point where people dying in the corridors outside registered as the most urgent thing happening tonight. A boy who could end a war in twenty minutes and wouldn't because the war wasn't important enough.

"Gavin," she said. Just his name.

"Yeah."

She didn't say anything else. She turned and walked out and pulled the door shut behind her. The latch clicked. Her footsteps went down the corridor — fast, because she was going back to the battle, because that was what Miren did with terrible things. She walked into them.

Gavin sat in the quiet classroom. The sounds of the distant battle came through the walls like a neighbor's television — muffled booms, a sustained crackling that might have been sustained spellfire or might have been fire. Then feet hammered past his door. Something heavy banged the stone hard enough to shake dust from the frame, and a girl's voice in the corridor said, "Keep pressure on it, keep pressure on it," while someone else made a wet choking sound that cut off every few steps.

His left hand was trembling against his thigh. He lifted it onto the desk and pressed it flat against the wood, against the initials someone had carved there during Arithmancy, and held it until the tremor stilled.

The room smelled like chalk dust and old wood and, faintly, smoke. Through the south-facing windows, the grounds were dark. Miren was out there with her burn and her cut and her wand. Duncan was out there somewhere. Whoever had just been dragged past his door was out there, or on the way to the Great Hall, or bleeding through someone's hands. The whole war was out there, finishing or not finishing, and he was in here.

The manuscript page was in front of him. The coupling pattern. The ward-maintenance cycle. The thing he'd been close to before she walked in.

He picked up the quill. He worked.

***

The substrate noise shifted and changed as the hours passed. He tracked it with the peripheral awareness he'd developed over months of practice — the part of his attention that monitored the substrate's state the way a sailor monitors weather, automatically, without it pulling focus from the work at hand. Peaks of combat rolled through the castle in waves. A surge of dark magic on the ground floor — concentrated, vicious, the substrate equivalent of a building fire. A lull. Then fighting in the corridors again, spellfire crackling through the castle's magical architecture like static through wiring.

He worked through all of it. The manuscript's notation was its own world, stable and precise in a way that nothing in the current magical framework could match. The Builders had designed these symbols to represent relationships that existed below the level of individual spells, below the level of magical intent, in the grammar of reality itself. And the coupling mechanism — the thing Gavin had been chasing for weeks, the thing the battle had scattered and that he was now, carefully, painstakingly, reassembling from the notation — was becoming clear.

The wards' self-repair cycle operated through resonance with the substrate's ground state. When the resonance held, damage to the wards reversed. The degradation he'd been perceiving for months — the thinning, the weak spots, the places where the boundary between Earth and what lay beyond it had worn to translucence — all of it could be corrected if the resonance was restored. The problem was that the resonance had been drifting for centuries. The substrate's ground state was not static. It changed — slowly, over timescales that made human history look like a single afternoon — and the wards, designed to harmonize with a ground state that no longer quite existed, were falling out of tune. Not breaking. Drifting. Like an instrument built for a key that the orchestra had gradually, imperceptibly shifted away from.

If he could map the drift, he could calculate the correction. Maybe. He was a mediocre fifth-year with bad handwriting and failing marks in every subject that wasn't Ancient Runes, working on a problem the Builders had designed for a civilization that used magic the way humans used oxygen. The gap between what was needed and what he could provide was enormous. But the coupling mechanism was the foundation, and he was looking at it, and for the first time the shape of the next step — not the solution, just the next step — was becoming visible in the notation's representational shifts.

Then the substrate shuddered.

He looked up from the manuscript. The sound from the battle hadn't changed — the same muffled booms and crashes, the same distant voices. But the substrate had convulsed with a single massive event. Two forces colliding. The distortion was enormous, bending the substrate around the impact the way heavy stone bends a wet tablecloth, a warping so large it made every other spell-event of the night seem like background static. Something was happening in the heart of the castle — the Great Hall, maybe, or somewhere close to it. Two concentrations of magical force, one pressing against the other, the substrate groaning under the weight of both.

He sat very still. The distortion peaked, held, and then—

A structure broke.

Not a wall. Not a spell. A structure in the substrate itself. Something that Gavin had perceived as a constant — a background feature of the magical world, a binding that connected dozens of individual signatures to a central node, a web of agreements that had been load-bearing for the entire architecture of dark magic that had defined wizarding Britain for the past year. It snapped. Instantly. Like a line under too much tension, the failure total and clean.

The substrate rippled outward from the break. It passed through the classroom, through him, through the castle walls. He felt it wash over the grounds outside. Dark Marks — he could perceive them as specific substrate agreements, bindings that tied individual casters to the central node — unraveled simultaneously. Dozens of them. The agreements that sustained them simply stopped being valid, their anchor point gone, and they dissolved back into the substrate's ground state like salt into water.

The combat noise in the substrate changed. The organized structure of the fighting — Death Eaters working in coordination, their magic synchronized through the shared binding — collapsed into confusion. The rhythm broke. Spells stuttered. A final surge of combat flared as the remaining fighters processed what they were feeling, and then the fighting began to die. Not all at once, but steadily, the substrate equivalent of rain tapering off.

Potter. It had to have been Potter, though Gavin didn't know the details and didn't need them. From somewhere below the south windows came a shout that was half cheer, half sob. He glanced up in time to see two figures on the lawn by the outer wall: a man in torn black robes reeled sideways, dropped his wand, and clamped both hands over his left forearm like it had turned white-hot. The student facing him, in scorched school robes, hit him with a Stunner while he was still staring at his arm. He went down in the churned mud and didn't get up.

That would be it, then. The central node of the dark magical framework had gone, and every dependent structure was coming apart with it.

Good. The substrate noise would die down. He could work more clearly now.

The combat magic faded over the next several minutes. Isolated spells still fired — disarming charms, stunners, the scattered engagements of a battle in its final collapse — but the organized warfare was over. The castle's own enchantments, strained and damaged, began to resettle into their ground state with the slow groaning of a building relaxing after an earthquake. The substrate gradually cleared. The noise that had been drowning out his perception all night thinned and thinned until the delicate structures he'd been studying emerged from the din the way stars emerged from a fading sunset — the same structures that had been there all along, hidden by the noise.

And there, in the coupling between the wards' self-repair cycle and the substrate's ground state, was the place he'd been reading wrong.

The specific harmonic at which the wards and the substrate had once synchronized was encoded in a representational structure he'd been misreading for weeks, taking a frequency relationship for a spatial one because the Builders' notation used the same glyph-root for both and the distinction depended on a context marker he'd only now learned to read. The resonance wasn't a frequency in any acoustic sense. It was a pattern of agreement — a way the wards' existence rhymed with the substrate's ground state. The Builders had designed the wards to be part of the substrate's natural grammar, woven into the base condition of magical reality so seamlessly that perceiving the substrate and perceiving the wards were almost the same act. And the drift — the centuries-long failing — was a change in the grammar itself. The substrate's ground state had shifted, and the wards hadn't shifted with it, and the gap between the two was where the degradation lived.

He could see the gap. He could see enough of the drift to know the problem had a shape. He could not yet see how to close it — that was months of work at least, maybe years, and he might never be able to do it at all, a fifteen-year-old Hufflepuff trying to perform maintenance on structures designed by a civilization that had used magic as the medium of their existence. Every time he tried to press past that, the insight threatened to flatten back into notation. The coupling mechanism was still the key. What he had, for the first time since he'd sat on the sub-collection floor and hyperventilated with the weight of what he'd learned, was a better grip on it.

He reached for a scrap of parchment and his nose started bleeding. He blotted it with his left sleeve — the sleeve already had old rust-colored stains from previous sessions, a record of months of nosebleeds that he'd stopped bothering to wash out — and began making notes. The notation he used was his own cobbled system, an inadequate hybrid of the Builders' representational glyphs and the runic cipher Babbling had given him. It was clumsy and imprecise and maybe enough to keep the idea from vanishing before he could test it properly.

The light in the classroom was changing. The windows had been dark when he'd arrived; now they were gray, then pale. The first thin light of May 2nd crept across the dusty Arithmancy equations on the blackboard, over the desks pushed against the walls, over the boy sitting at a single desk in the center of the room with blood drying on his sleeve and the warm manuscript under his hands.

The sounds from the castle had changed too. The battle sounds were gone. In their place: a stretcher thumping a wall somewhere down the corridor. A man shouting for Madam Pomfrey. A girl crying hard enough to hiccup between breaths. And somewhere, not close but not far, a voice calling a name over and over — the same name, the same desperate rhythm, the voice of someone who hadn't found the person they were looking for and would not stop calling until they did or until someone made them stop.

Gavin heard it. He heard the crying, the stretcher, the name being called, the silence where the explosions had been. He registered it as part of the world — the world, with all its specific and ordinary grief, its people looking for other people in the rubble, its morning after a night that had changed everything for everyone except him. The world he was trying to keep.

He looked up from the manuscript. Through the window, the sky was pale blue at the horizon and darker above, the last stars of the night dissolving into morning. The grounds below were visible now — churned earth, scorch marks, a collapsed section of wall near the greenhouses. Smoke rose from somewhere to the north.

The wards were there, overhead, invisible to anyone else — a vast interlocking architecture that wrapped the planet, perceivable only through the substrate awareness the manuscript had built into his cognition. He could see them. They were thinner than they had been yesterday. They were holding.

Beyond them — past the wards, past the boundary of everything the wizarding world knew or could perceive — the thing that was not a thing occupied its space. Patient in the way erosion was patient. In the way a crack worked through stone a winter at a time. It would be there tomorrow, and the day after, and every day until the wards failed or held. It was not waiting for anything. It was simply there.

Gavin looked down at the manuscript. The notation on the page shifted in its slow representational cycle, the Builders' symbols showing him a problem that had finally begun to show its shape and that nobody else in the world was working on or would ever be able to work on.

The classroom had done what he needed it to do. The real work was still down in the limestone rooms, with the manuscript spread properly and the castle quiet enough to think. He stacked the pages, slid them carefully into his satchel, and stood.

By the time he made it back to the sub-collection, the substrate noise had thinned almost to nothing. Not suddenly. More like pressure easing out of the world a little at a time. He sat on the limestone floor with the manuscript open on his knees and tried to catch the pattern before it flattened back into notation.


# Chapter 18

By dawn the substrate had gone quiet.

Gavin noticed it the way you notice a headache ending — not the sudden absence of pressure but the slow realization that it had been there and now it wasn't. For hours the battle's output had saturated everything: hundreds of spells fired simultaneously, each one flexing the agreements that made it possible, the accumulated noise of it drowning out the fine structures he needed to perceive the way a brass band drowns out a whisper. He'd sat with the manuscript open on his knees and tried to catch the pattern before it flattened back into notation, but every time his perception tried to settle, another volley of combat magic dragged the substrate sideways and the patterns dissolved.

He didn't know how long the battle had lasted. Time had a different quality in the sub-collection even on normal nights — the old stone didn't carry sound from the castle above, and the self-lit walls held the same faint glow whether it was noon or midnight. But the substrate carried everything, and through it Gavin had felt the fighting as a continuous distortion, the magical equivalent of trying to read during an earthquake. He'd tried to work anyway. He'd sat on the limestone floor with his back against the shelf where he'd first found the manuscript seven months ago, and he'd held the pages open, and he'd stared at notation that blurred and re-formed every time a cluster of killing curses hit the same stretch of corridor two hundred feet above his head. He'd accomplished nothing.

And then it stopped. There was a trailing-off, a scattering of final percussive jolts — single spells, then silence, then another, then silence that held — and then a quiet that spread outward through the substrate until the last resonance died out. The agreements were still there, still intact, still governing how magic worked, but nobody was using them hard enough to make the whole system ring.

Gavin closed his eyes and felt the wards.

They were there. Overhead and everywhere, the vast architecture the Builders had left behind, the planetary-scale structures that filtered Earth from the kind of attention that would unmake magical life as casually as a footstep unmakes an anthill. He couldn't see them with his eyes — that wasn't how it worked — but through the restructured cognition the manuscript had built in him over months, he could feel their shape the way you feel the shape of a room in the dark: edges, distances, the places where the structure was thick and the places where it had worn thin and let through a quality of awareness that didn't belong here.

They were thinner than they'd been when he'd checked them upstairs. He couldn't tell if the battle had done it — the massive expenditure of war magic stressing substrate joints that were already worn — or if it was the ordinary daily degradation he'd been tracking for months, as indifferent to whatever the wizards below were doing to each other as rot in roof beams is indifferent to the family eating under them.

He opened his eyes and looked at the manuscript. The notation had settled. He could read it again.

He read for what might have been half an hour. The section on ward-substrate coupling that the battle had disrupted — the Builders' notation for the interrelation between active magical use and the passive structures that held the wards in place. He'd been close to understanding something about it before the fighting started, a connection between the resonance patterns of the wards and the way the substrate responded to load, and he'd lost it in the noise, and now the thread was there again, fragile, waiting for him to pick it up.

He picked it up. He followed it for two pages. The notation shifted under his perception, meaning surfacing and sliding away before he could hold it, and he concentrated until his nose started to bleed.

He pinched the bridge of his nose with his left hand and kept reading with the blood running over his fingers. The tremor in that hand was worse today — it always got worse when he was tired — and the blood pooled in the crease between his thumb and forefinger and dripped onto his robes. He didn't wipe it.

The footsteps reached him before the person did. The sub-collection's corridors carried sound in odd ways — the old stone bounced echoes around corners, arriving before they should have, so he heard her for nearly a minute before she appeared in the doorway. He knew it was Miren from the rhythm. She walked faster than most people, even when she wasn't in a hurry.

She stopped in the doorway and stood there and looked at him.

Gavin lowered the manuscript page. He wiped his nose on his sleeve, smearing blood across the back of his hand, and looked up at her.

She looked like she'd been through a building collapse. Stone dust in her hair, turning the dark plait grey-white where it hadn't come loose, and it had come loose on one side so the hair hung over her ear in a knotted tangle she hadn't bothered to fix. A cut on her forehead, shallow but long, still seeping along the edge of her hairline. Her robes were torn at the shoulder and there was something dark on her left side — blood, not hers, from the way it had soaked into the fabric in a broad stain rather than spreading from a wound. The old bruise on her jaw, the one from February that had never quite faded, sat underneath a new one, fresher, already purpling along the same cheekbone as though the same bone kept catching everything meant for her face.

She was breathing hard. She'd come quickly through the damaged corridors, the route to the sub-collection that was already unreliable on normal days and must have been worse now with the battle's damage to the castle's upper structure.

"It's over," she said.

Gavin nodded. He'd felt it in the substrate — not the specific moment of Voldemort's death, but the cessation of combat, the pattern of magical output collapsing from hundreds of simultaneous sources to scattered individuals to nothing.

"I know," he said. "I felt the fighting stop."

She stood in the doorway for another moment. Then she came in and sat down against the opposite wall, three feet from him, and closed her eyes. She let her head fall back against the stone. The faint light from the walls caught the cut on her forehead and the dried blood and the dust in her hair, and she looked older than fifteen. She looked like someone who'd been awake for thirty hours and most of those hours had been the worst of her life.

Neither of them said anything for a while.

The sub-collection's quiet was its own kind. The mineral smell of limestone. The dry cold that had nothing to do with weather. The light from the walls, steady and faint, casting almost no shadows because it came from everywhere at once. Above them, somewhere in the castle's newer construction, something creaked and settled — the building adjusting to damage, the same sounds an old house makes after a storm, except the storm had been a war and the house had been full of children.

"Harry killed him," Miren said. Her eyes were still closed. "In the Great Hall. I wasn't there for it — I was outside, near the greenhouses. But they said the spell rebounded or something. People were cheering." She paused. "It didn't sound like cheering. It sounded like — I don't know. Something else."

"Yeah," Gavin said.

Miren opened her eyes. She looked at the ceiling — the limestone, unmarked, older than the rest of the castle by centuries. A single crack ran across it that hadn't been there before, a thin fracture line where the battle's concussive force had reached even this deep.

"People died, Gavin."

"I know."

"Do you want to know who?"

He put the manuscript page down. He set it carefully on the floor beside him, the pages aligned, their faint warmth fading into the cold stone, and he gave her his full attention. It felt strange. He'd been giving all his attention to the notation for hours and redirecting it was like turning a heavy piece of furniture. But he turned it.

"Yeah," he said. "Tell me."

She told him.

Marsh. The sixth-year who'd done the Carrow impression at Christmas dinner, the one who'd practised it for weeks beforehand and revealed it to the table with the timing of someone who understood that comedy was mostly about waiting, and the table had laughed, and Duncan had laughed so hard that pumpkin juice came out of his nose, and Gavin had laughed too because it was Christmas and they were scared and Marsh had made them briefly not scared. He'd stayed to fight. A wall had come down on him in the third-floor corridor. They'd found him under the rubble afterward, and he'd been dead for at least an hour by then, and a first-year had been sitting beside the pile of stone because she hadn't known what else to do.

Colin Creevey, the Gryffindor. Gavin thought he was fifteen, maybe sixteen — he couldn't remember. Creevey had snuck back into the castle to fight after being evacuated. He'd been too young to be there. The specifics of how he'd died were unclear, or Miren hadn't heard them, or she'd decided not to repeat them.

A Ravenclaw girl named Anand whom Gavin had sat next to exactly once in Herbology. She'd had dirt under her fingernails the whole lesson and she'd known the Latin name for every plant in greenhouse three and she'd corrected Professor Sprout on a soil acidity question, quietly, without embarrassment, and Sprout had conceded the point with a small nod that said she was impressed. A killing curse. Anand hadn't known it was coming.

There were others. Miren named them in a flat, even voice, one after another, the way you read a list you've already read too many times. Some Gavin recognized from corridors, from classes, from the muffled year they'd all shared in this damaged school. Some he didn't. A few were adults — Lupin, who'd taught Defence in Gavin's second year, the one with the patches on his robes and the tired kind voice that made you feel like he actually wanted you to learn the material. Tonks, the Auror, who Gavin had never spoken to but had seen in the corridors once, her hair bright pink against the stone grey of the castle walls.

Miren told him all of it. When she was done she put her hands on her knees and pressed down, and he could see her fingers whiten with the pressure against the fabric, as though she needed to feel the floor was solid.

"There are more," she said. "I don't know all the names yet. They're still — the Great Hall's a triage ward. Madam Pomfrey's got people everywhere, and there are house tables flipped on their sides for barriers, and they've laid out the..." She stopped. Started again. "Some of them are Death Eaters. Nobody's moving them yet. Nobody's sorting who's who."

"Are you hurt?" Gavin asked. He was looking at the cut on her forehead, the dark stain on her robes.

"This isn't mine." She touched the blood on her side. She didn't say whose it was. "The cut's nothing. I've had worse from Quidditch."

She hadn't played Quidditch this year. Nobody had. But it was something to say, and they both let it sit there without correcting it.

"Duncan?" Gavin said.

"Alive. He's upstairs." Something shifted in Miren's face — the flat reporting voice cracked for a moment and something else came through, not grief exactly but a kind of bewildered tenderness. "He was in the Room of Requirement with the younger kids during most of it. He came out near the end. He's been carrying water to the Great Hall for the past two hours. Him and two third-years with jugs. Just going back and forth."

Gavin could picture it. Duncan, thin and careful, his fair hair unwashed, the split lip that had healed badly, carrying water jugs because they were the thing in front of him that needed doing. Not dramatic. Not the kind of bravery anyone would write about. Just the repeated walk between the kitchen corridor and the Great Hall with something heavy in his hands, because people were hurt and thirsty and he could carry water.

"OK," Gavin said.

The quiet settled again. Somewhere above them, distantly, a sound that might have been crying or might have been the castle's stressed stone — the sub-collection filtered the world above through old limestone so that everything arrived distant and approximate, like hearing rain through a thick wall. It was impossible to tell pain from architecture from this deep.

Miren was watching him. It wasn't the way she'd looked at him in the classroom during the battle — that had been something between fury and horror, the look of someone seeing a person they loved choose something monstrous with perfect calm. This was different. Quieter. She was studying him with the careful attention of someone trying to map a landscape that had changed since the last time she'd looked. Not with shock — she'd had time to process — but with the steady focus of someone who needed to understand what she was looking at before she could decide what to do about it.

"You worked through it," she said. It wasn't a question.

"I tried. The substrate was — I couldn't do much. The noise from the fighting disrupted everything."

"The noise."

"The magical output. Hundreds of people casting simultaneously, it saturates everything I'm trying to perceive. It drowns it out."

"So the battle was an inconvenience."

The word sat between them. Miren said it without heat — not as an accusation but as a statement she was testing the weight of, the way you test whether a step will hold before you commit your weight to it.

"I didn't mean it like that," Gavin said. Then he stopped, because he had meant it like that, and she knew it, and lying about it now would be worse than the truth. "I mean. Yeah. For the work. It was."

She nodded. Slowly. She wasn't approving, just taking it in.

"Marsh is dead," she said. "Anand. Creevey. Lupin. All of them. And for you it was a disruption to your work."

"I know what that makes me sound like."

"Do you?"

"Yes." He looked at the dried blood on his hand — his blood, from the nosebleed, already browning against his skin. "I don't know what to do with that. I can't — I can hear it when I say it. I know what it makes me."

There was a long silence. Long enough that Gavin could hear the faint persistent drip of water finding its way through battle-damaged stone above, trickling down through new cracks in old limestone to places it hadn't reached before. A new path through old rock.

"I saw the curse," Miren said. Her voice had changed. Lower. Slower. She was feeling her way through something she'd been turning over for hours, maybe longer. "During the battle. I was near the east staircase and there was a Death Eater — not one I recognized, nobody important — and he cast something at you and it just stopped. The light was there and then it wasn't. Like somebody had turned it off."

Gavin remembered. Or didn't, exactly — he'd been thinking about the ward-substrate coupling problem, and the curse had been an interruption he'd dealt with reflexively and immediately let go of. His perception had registered the incoming spell as a substrate event, identified the agreement that made it valid, and edited that agreement locally so the curse was no longer a thing that existed. The whole process had taken less time than blinking. He had continued walking.

"I saw that," Miren said. "And I saw you keep walking like nothing had happened. Like a killing curse — it was green, Gavin, I know what green means — was the same as someone bumping you in a corridor."

"I wasn't thinking about it."

"I know." She was quiet for a moment. "That's what's frightening."

Another silence. Miren drew her knees up and rested her chin on them and looked at him across the three feet of limestone floor, and her face was very tired and very young and not angry. That was the thing he kept coming back to. She wasn't angry anymore. The anger had been there for months — the slow burn of watching someone she cared about choose something she couldn't see — and it had been there in the classroom during the battle, white-hot and desperate. But it was gone now, or used up, or it had turned into something else that didn't have a name, a quiet exhausted recognition that sat somewhere between grief and acceptance.

"The things you told me about," she said. "The wards."

"Yeah."

"They're real."

It wasn't a question. Gavin looked at her.

"I can't see what you see," she said. "I know that. I'm not going to pretend I understand any of it. The substrate, the Builders, the — whatever it is out there. I don't understand it. But I watched you dissolve a killing curse without noticing and then refuse to stop a war because it wasn't worth the interruption, and I've been thinking about that while I was pulling Creevey's body off the lawn, and the only way any of it makes sense — the only way you're not just..." She stopped. Started again, more carefully. "The only way you're not completely broken is if what you told me is true."

Gavin looked at the blood on his hand. He rubbed the dried flakes with his thumb, feeling them crumble against his skin, and the touch of it — the specific grain of dried blood against his own thumb — was the thing that was real right now, more real than anything he could say.

"It's true," he said.

"Yeah." Miren pressed her forehead against her knees and spoke into the fabric of her robes. "I know it is. I think I've known for a while. Since the quill, maybe. Or since the way your eyes go when you're looking at something I can't see. Like you're reading a page that isn't there." She lifted her head. "You really see them? The wards? Right now?"

"I can feel them. It's not visual, exactly. It's more like — " He stopped. The old problem. The words didn't exist for what he perceived because the perception wasn't built for human language, and every time he tried to describe it he ran into the same wall, the same gap between experience and speech that had been widening all year. "They're there. They're always there now. And they're thinner than they were yesterday."

"Because of the battle?"

"Maybe. Partly. They've been thinning for centuries. The battle might have — I don't know. The substrate took a lot of stress from all that combat magic. But I haven't been able to check properly. The noise only just stopped."

"And beyond them. Beyond the wards."

"It's still there."

Miren held his gaze. Her eyes were dark and red-rimmed and steady. "Are you going to fix them?"

There it was. The question he'd been asking himself since December, since the first time he'd felt the wards directly from the Astronomy Tower, since the manuscript had shown him the scope of what was failing and the scale of what failure meant. The question that had no good answer because the honest answer was the worst one.

"I don't know," he said.

Miren waited. She could do that — listen with a fierce attention that was more intense than speech, a quality of listening that had always made Gavin feel she could see further into what he was saying than he could himself. She was doing it now. And it was different from all the times before, because this time she was listening to something she was prepared to believe.

"The wards were built by something that — they weren't human, Miren. They weren't even close to human. They used magic the way we use... I don't know, air. It was their medium. Their environment. And they built the wards as they were leaving, and the wards were designed for that kind of intelligence to maintain, and I'm a fifteen-year-old who failed his Transfiguration OWL and can't do a proper Switching Spell." He looked at the manuscript on the floor beside him. The notation on the exposed page shifted slowly, relationships between concepts drifting and reconfiguring, and he could read about a third of what it was saying. "I've been working on this for seven months and I can read maybe a third of the notation on a good day. And the parts I can read — the maintenance protocols, the coupling mechanics — they're the introductory sections. The Builders' equivalent of a primer. For children. For their children, who could do things I can't even imagine. And the primer is the hardest thing I've ever tried to understand."

"So you can't fix them."

"I don't know. I might not be smart enough. The problem might be too big." He picked at the last flake of dried blood on his knuckle. "But nobody else can see them. Nobody else knows they're failing. Nobody else is working on it."

"Just you."

"Just me."

Miren looked at him for a long time. The thin line of dried blood from her hairline to her eyebrow had darkened to a rust colour, and the dust in her hair had settled into the creases of her plait so that it looked grey in the sub-collection's pale light. She looked older than fifteen. She looked like someone who had survived something and was still deciding what surviving it meant.

"Right," she said.

She stood up. She was slow about it — stiff, bruised, running on nothing — and she steadied herself with one hand against the wall and stood there looking down at him. He was sitting on the floor of the sub-collection with the manuscript beside him and the blood on his hand and the faint light from limestone walls and the work that would outlast everything she'd just survived and everything she was going back to.

"Miren."

"Yeah."

"I'm sorry about Marsh."

Something crumpled in her face. Just for a second — a flash of grief that she put away with the speed of someone who'd been putting grief away all night because there was too much of it to hold and she'd been holding it for hours and would hold it for years but not right now, not here, not standing in a room full of old stone where a different kind of loss was measured on a different kind of scale.

"He was funny," she said. Her voice was thick. "He was really funny and he was seventeen and he stayed because he said someone had to make sure the Carrows knew what their own faces looked like when they were scared. He practised that impression for weeks. He had Alecto's walk down and everything."

Gavin didn't say anything. There was nothing to say that was adequate to a seventeen-year-old who'd been brave and funny and was now under a pile of stone, and the silence was better than the wrong words, so he let it be.

"I'll come back," Miren said. "Or — I don't know. I'll come back."

"OK."

She turned and walked out of the sub-collection. Her footsteps echoed in the corridor — fast, then fading, then they were gone — and the old quiet reasserted itself, the quiet that had been here before the castle was built and would be here after, and Gavin was alone.

---

He sat with the names for a while.

Marsh, Anand, Creevey, Lupin, Tonks, the others Miren had listed in her flat voice. People who had been alive yesterday morning and were not alive now, people who had died in the specific, physical, irreversible way that people die — not substrate events, not agreements ceasing to be valid, but hearts stopping, blood leaving the places it was supposed to be, eyes going fixed on whatever they happened to be looking at when the light that meant seeing left them. Marsh had been looking at a wall that was falling on him. Anand hadn't seen it coming. Creevey had come back when he should have stayed away.

He thought about Marsh's Carrow impression. The sixth-year's face screwing up into Amycus's habitual sneer, the way he'd pitched his voice low and whiny at the same time, the timing of the reveal — the table leaning in, waiting, and then Marsh's face shifting and the laughter breaking out like something that had been held too long, Duncan's pumpkin juice coming out his nose, the Christmas crackers and the thin, specific warmth of people being together in a place that was trying to make togetherness impossible. That had been four months ago.

Gavin knew what this made him. Or what it would make him to anyone who could see his mind from the outside. The boy who sat in a cellar and worked while his classmates died upstairs. The boy who could have stopped it and chose not to. The boy who filed human deaths under background noise and kept reading notation about planetary-scale maintenance because the math was clear even if everything else was ruined.

He wasn't sure the morality was unclear. That was the part he couldn't get past. From where he sat — where the wards were perceptible and their thinning was measurable and the thing beyond them was as real as the floor under his legs — the war had been a tremendous, lethal, heartbreaking rounding error. Fifty dead. A hundred. Against a ward failure that would end magical existence on a planetary scale, not through violence or intention but through the simple incompatibility of what was out there with what was in here. The numbers were not close. They were not in the same category of event. And knowing that didn't help with the fact that Marsh was dead and had been funny and seventeen and that Gavin had been reading a manuscript while it happened and would go on reading the manuscript now that it was over.

Both things were true. He didn't know how to hold both at the same time. But they fit in his hands whether he knew how to hold them or not.

He picked up the manuscript.

The notation had settled fully now. In the hours since the substrate went quiet, the representational structures on the page had returned to their resting state — the slow drift of relational indicators, the clusters of meaning that Gavin could parse if he held his focus and let his restructured cognition meet the notation halfway. He found the section he'd been working on before the battle. Ward-substrate coupling. The relationship between the ambient magical load — every spell cast by every wizard on Earth, the accumulated weight of the magical world's daily operations — and the passive structures that held the wards in place.

The Builders' notation for this was dense and recursive. Each clause referred to three or four other clauses, which referred to others, and the web of dependencies was deeper than anything Gavin had encountered in the manuscript so far. He could see the shape of what it was describing without being able to follow the full argument: the wards were not static barriers but dynamic systems that compensated for the ongoing use of magic below them, the same way a living membrane compensates for pressure differentials on either side, and when the magical load exceeded certain parameters or fell below others, the ward-substrate coupling shifted, and the compensation became less exact, and the places where it was least exact became the thin spots he could feel overhead.

He could see that much. What he couldn't see was how to fix it. The Builders' maintenance protocols were written for a cognition that could perceive the entire ward structure simultaneously — all of it, planet-wide, with the resolution to distinguish individual coupling points and the processing capacity to model their interactions in real time. Gavin could perceive the wards the way someone on the ground perceives a cloud formation: in broad shape, in general movement, in local texture where the structure dipped low enough to resolve detail. The Builders had perceived them the way a meteorologist perceives weather: as a single dynamic system, every point connected to every other, the whole thing legible as one object.

He was not a Builder. He was not a good wizard. He was a fifteen-year-old Hufflepuff whose best subject this year had been Ancient Runes and whose worst had been everything else, and the manuscript's primer-level notation — the introductory material, the part designed for the Builders' young — was the hardest thing he'd ever tried to understand. The maintenance work it described was orders of magnitude beyond what the primer prepared you for.

But the primer was open on his lap, and its notation was legible to him — a third of it, maybe less today because he was tired and his nose was still bleeding and his left hand wouldn't stop shaking — and there was nobody else.

The crack in the ceiling, the new one from the battle, let in a thin line of light. Not the sub-collection's own luminescence but actual morning light, falling through damaged stone from somewhere in the castle above. It was a narrow beam, almost vertical, and it struck the floor two feet from where Gavin sat, illuminating dust motes that drifted in the faint currents of air that the crack had admitted. The light was warm and gold-white and it fell on old stone that had not seen sunlight in centuries.

It was morning. The first morning after the battle. Above him the Great Hall was full of the wounded and the dead and the people carrying water to both. Students were emerging into the light. They would find their friends and learn who was gone and hold each other and begin the long, stunned work of rebuilding a world that had Voldemort's absence in it where his presence used to be. The war was over. The regime was over. The school would be repaired. Students would come back in September and be sorted and eat feasts and take classes and sit OWLs and graduate and have lives, and the wards would thin, and thin, and thin, and nobody but Gavin would notice.

He looked at the beam of morning light on the floor. He thought about going upstairs. He thought about the Great Hall, the triage tables, the sound of grief and relief mixed together into something that didn't have its own name. He thought about Duncan carrying water jugs with two third-years. He thought about sitting with people and being part of the human world for a while, the world where people died and you mourned them and then you kept going.

Then he looked at the manuscript.

The notation on the open page described the first coupling interface — the simplest junction between ambient magical load and ward structure, the place where the two systems communicated. In the Builders' framework, this interface was the foundational maintenance point: the first thing you would check, the equivalent of taking a patient's pulse before anything else. Gavin had been studying it for three weeks. He understood maybe forty percent of it. The rest remained opaque — not meaningless, not random, but not yet accessible to his level of cognition. Sixty percent of an introductory passage written for children of a civilization that had used magic as its native medium of existence.

He turned the page.

The notation shifted. New relationships emerged as the exposed section connected to the one beneath it, the representational structures reorganizing to show a different face of the same system, the way turning a prism shows a different angle on the same geometry. Gavin's perception adjusted. The new section was denser — more recursive, more deeply embedded, more dependencies — and his understanding of it was less than before, maybe thirty percent, maybe twenty-five. He would sit with this page for days before it yielded what it held, and then he would turn to the next page, and the next, and each one would be harder, and the work would take longer than he could estimate because he didn't know how deep it went.

The beam of morning light moved across the floor as the sun climbed over the Scottish mountains outside. The first full sunrise of a world without Voldemort. The light was warm and ordinary and it fell on old stone and dust and manuscript pages and Gavin's shoes and the dark spots where his nosebleed had dripped, and it did not care what it touched, and neither did the morning.

Above the castle — above the broken towers and the rubble and the scorch marks and the students blinking in the new daylight and the dead laid out in the Great Hall under conjured sheets — the wards held. Gavin could feel them without trying. They were always there now, a constant in his restructured perception, as present as the sound of his own pulse. They were thinner than yesterday. They were thinner than last week. They were thinner than they'd been in September, when he'd sat on the Hogwarts Express as a mediocre fifth-year with no plans beyond scraping through his OWLs, and they would be thinner tomorrow, and thinner the day after that, and the thinning would continue at the same slow, immense, patient pace whether anyone worked on them or not.

And beyond the wards, in the space that the Builders' architecture was designed to make irrelevant, the condition persisted. It was not a creature. It was not a mind. It was not a threat in any sense that implied intention or hostility or malice. It was simply there, and when Gavin let his perception edge toward the fact of it, the limestone room around him seemed briefly mistranslated: the corners too exact, the air a fraction too thin, the warmth of the manuscript on his knees no longer something he could take for granted. It occupied what it occupied. Its presence was incompatible with what the wards protected not because it wanted anything but because the rules there were wrong for the kind of life Earth had. It did not notice Earth. The wards made sure of that. And the wards were thinner than yesterday, and they would be thinner tomorrow, and they would go on thinning with the patience of a crack working through stone — nothing to see for years, then one day the wall gives way.

Gavin turned another page. The notation shifted again. He leaned forward, and his nose dripped blood onto the warm surface of the manuscript, and he didn't wipe it, and the light from the cracked ceiling fell across his hands and the page and the small room made of stone that was older than anything the wizards above him had ever built.

He read.
